Refuting the Expression and Anthropomorphic Analogies
Refutation of anthropomorphic analogy for divine speech diversity.

Learning Objective
This lesson refutes the opponents' analogy of spiritual beings taking physical form to explain the unity of divine speech, and clarifies the correct understanding of Gabriel's manifestation.

Core Idea
Spiritual beings do not become physical; their manifestation is a change in density or creation.

The opponents resort to analogies of angels taking human form to argue that diverse expressions can convey a single meaning. The author dismisses this as empty talk and demands substantiation. He then offers the correct view: Gabriel is a subtle being that can become dense and appear visibly, or we speak of annihilation and recreation. The argument about substance and extension is also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' analogy
They liken spiritual beings (e.g., Gabriel) taking physical form to the idea that diverse expressions can convey a single meaning.
2
Understand the author's criticism
The author calls the analogy empty and asks for clarification: what does 'taking form' mean? How does manifestation occur?
3
Learn the correct explanation
Gabriel is a subtle being who becomes dense to appear, or is annihilated and recreated. A single substance does not become multiple without addition of other substances; two extended things cannot interpenetrate.
This refutes the idea of spiritual beings physically transforming into another substance.

فإن المعقول من أقسام الكلام ذوات مختلفة وحقائق متباينة، فإن القصة التي جرت ليوسف وإخوته صلوات الله عليهم غير القصة التي جرت لآدم ونوح وإبراهيم وموسى وعيسى فالخبر عما جرى في حق شخص كيف يكون عين الخبر الذي جرى في حق شخص آخر؟ والأوامر والنواهي التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور نبي مخصوص غير الأوامر التي توجهت على قوم أخر في دور آخر، فكيف يمكنكم القول باتحاد الأخبار كلها على اختلافها في خبر واحد، والقول باتحاد الأوامر على تفاوتها في أمر واحد؟ ثم كيف يمكنكم الجمع بين معنى الخبر وحقيقته أنه خبر عما كان أو سيكون من غير اقتضاء وطلب وبين معنى الأمر وحقيقته أنه اقتضاء وطلب لأمر لم يكن حتى يكون، فليس في الخبر حكم واقتضاء وليس في الأمر خبر وإنباء وبين النوعين فرق ظاهر؟ فكيف يمكن القول باتحادهما؟

نعم، هما يتحدان في حقيقة الكلامية لكنهما يختلفان بالنوعية كالحيوانية والإنسانية والعرضية واللونية، فمن رد الكلام بأنواعه وأقسامه إلى الخبر فقد أبطل الاقتضاء وعطل الحكم والطلب ومن رد الكلام إلى الأمر فقد أبطل معنى الخبر وعطل القصص، ومن المعلوم أن النوعين موجودان في جنس الكلام، ومذكوران في الكتب الإلهية، وأما من رد الاختلاف والكثرة فيهما إلى العبارات فقد أبعد النجعة، فإن العبارات إن طابقت المعاني خبرا لمخبر، وأمرا لمأمور، ونهيا لمنهي، فقد تعددت المعاني تعدد العبارات، وإن لم تطابق فليست هي تعبيرات عنها، وإنما هي عبارات لا معنى لها وذلك كلام المجانين.

وأما استرواحكم إلى تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني وتشكل الملك بالبشر، وظهور المعنى بالعبارات فذلك في أسماعنا شبه كلمات وكلمات فارغة، فما التمثل والتشكل؟ وكيف الظهور والتبين؟ حققوا لنا ذلك إن كانت العبارة مشتملة على حقيقة، وإلا فالمعلومات لا تحتمل أمثال هذه المجازفات. والذي عندنا أن جبريل شخص لطيف يتكاثف فيتراءى للبصر كالهواء اللطيف الذي لا يرى، فيتكاثف فيرى سحابا، أو نقول بإعدام وإيجاد لا يتمثل ولا يتشخص، وبالجملة جوهر واحد لا يصير جواهر إلا بانضمام جواهر إليه ونحن لا نعقل من الجواهر إلا المتحيز، والمتحيزان لا يتداخلان، فلا معنى لما تمسكتم به.
For what is understood from the divisions of speech are different essences and distinct realities. The story that occurred with Yusuf and his brothers—may God's blessings be upon them all—is not the story that occurred with Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus. So how can the report about what occurred concerning one person be the very report that occurred concerning another person? And the commands and prohibitions that were directed at one people in the era of a particular prophet are not the commands that were directed at another people in another era. So how can you claim the unification of all reports, despite their differences, into a single report, and the unification of commands, despite their disparities, into a single command? Then how can you combine the meaning and reality of a report—that it is a report about what was or will be, without entailment and request—with the meaning and reality of a command—that it is an entailment and request for something that was not until it becomes? For there is no judgment and entailment in a report, and there is no report or notification in a command, and between the two types there is an apparent difference. So how can one claim their unification? Yes, they are united in the reality of speech-ness, but they differ in kind, like animality and humanity and accidental-ness and color-ness. Whoever reduces speech, with its types and divisions, to a report has nullified entailment and canceled judgment and request; and whoever reduces speech to a command has nullified the meaning of a report and canceled stories. And it is known that both types exist in the genus of speech and are mentioned in the divine books. As for whoever reduces the difference and multiplicity in them to expressions, he has gone far astray. For if the expressions correspond to the meanings—a report about that which is reported, a command to that which is commanded, and a prohibition to that which is prohibited—then the meanings are as multiple as the expressions; and if they do not correspond, then they are not expressions of them; rather, they are expressions without meaning, and that is the speech of madmen. As for your resorting to the likening of the spiritual to the physical, and the angel taking the form of a human, and the manifestation of meaning through expressions—that, to our ears, is like words and empty words. So what is this likening and taking form? And how is the manifestation and clarification? Substantiate that for us if the expression contains a reality; otherwise, known things cannot bear such reckless assertions. What we hold is that Gabriel is a subtle person who becomes dense and appears to the eye like the subtle air that is not seen; then he becomes dense and is seen as a cloud. Or we say by annihilation and creation, without taking form or becoming individuated. In sum, a single substance does not become multiple substances except by the addition of other substances to it, and we only understand from substances that which is extended in space (mutehayyiz), and two extended things cannot interpenetrate. So there is no meaning to what you have clung to.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that all of God's speech (kalam) is of a single type—either all news (akhbar) or all commands (awamir). The author begins by arguing that the reports (akhbar) about different prophets and events are distinct realities; the story of Yusuf is not the same as the story of Adam, so how could the report about one be identical to the report about another? Similarly, commands and prohibitions directed to different communities in different prophetic eras are not identical. The author then asks how one can claim the unification of all reports or commands into one. He further highlights the definitional difference: a report informs about what was or will be without entailment or request, whereas a command entails a request for something that does not yet exist. Thus, reports and commands are fundamentally different and cannot be united. The author then concedes that they are united in the genus of speech (kalamiyya) but differ in species, like animality and humanity. Those who reduce all speech to report nullify entailment and command; those who reduce it to command nullify the meaning of report and cancel stories. Both types exist in the genus and are found in scripture. He then criticizes those who attribute the diversity merely to different expressions, arguing that if expressions correspond to meanings, the meanings are as diverse as the expressions; if not, the expressions are meaningless. Finally, he attacks the opponents' analogy of spiritual beings taking physical form, calling it empty. He offers the correct view: Gabriel is a subtle being that can become dense and appear visibly, or we speak of annihilation and recreation without physical manifestation. A single substance (jawhar) does not become multiple without addition of other substances, and two extended things cannot interpenetrate, so their analogy is baseless.

Key Terms
تمثل
تشكل
جوهر
متحيز
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's explanation for how Gabriel appears to prophets?
A) Gabriel is a physical being that changes shape.
B) Gabriel becomes dense like a cloud or is annihilated and recreated.
C) Gabriel takes full human form with a different substance.
D) Gabriel manifests by interpenetrating with other substances.

Key Takeaway
The analogy of spiritual beings taking physical form is invalid; divine speech diversity is real and not explained by such empty analogies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author calls the opponents' reasoning 'empty words'. Pause and ask yourself: In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, are there ideas you hold that are based on your own imagination rather than revelation? Will you commit to learning the 'how' only from the Quran and Sunnah, and not from your own mind?
?
The passage insists that a single substance cannot become multiple without addition. Do you find it difficult to accept that Allah's speech is one yet diverse, without any change in His essence? Can you truly submit to the principle of 'bila kayf' (without asking how) for the qualities of your Lord? What must you do to train your heart to accept this?
?
Gabriel appears by becoming dense or by annihilation and creation. When you think of the spiritual realm, do you imagine it as a copy of this physical world? How does this lesson free you from such limited thinking? What concrete step will you take tonight to contemplate the majesty of Allah's creation beyond the physical?

Ibn Kullab on the Eternal Speech of God
Ibn Kullab's view on God's eternal speech and its temporal modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Kullab's distinction between the eternal essence of God's speech and its temporal characterizations as command, prohibition, news, or inquiry.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal, but its specific modalities depend on created existence.

Ibn Kullab, the founder of the Kullabi school and key influence on Ash'ari theology, made a subtle but important distinction regarding God's attribute of speech. This passage reports his view that while God's speech is eternal in its essence, it is only properly called a 'command' or 'prohibition' when it relates to something that exists. This nuanced position preserves both the eternality of the attribute and its relevance to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the single Arabic sentence and its literal English translation. Notice the two key parts: the negative statement (لا يتصف) and the exception (إلا عند وجود).
The sentence is incomplete in the original source; the doctrine is still fully presented.
2
Identify the core distinction
Ibn Kullab affirms that God's speech is eternal (في الأزل) but denies that it is characterized as command, prohibition, etc., in eternity. These characterizations only apply when the speech 'exists' – that is, when it comes into relation with created things.
This distinguishes the eternal essence of speech from its temporal functions.
3
Consider the theological significance
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it upholds the eternality of divine speech (against the Mu'tazila who say speech is created) while avoiding the problem of saying God eternally commands things that do not yet exist.
This is a key point in Ash'ari theology.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you grasped the timing of the characterizations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : ذهب شيخنا الكلابي عبد الله بن سعيد إلى أن كلام الباري في الأزل لا يتصف بكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبارًا إلا عند وجود
The Ash'aris said: Our teacher al-Kullabi, Abdullah ibn Sa'id, held that the speech of the Creator in eternity is not characterized as being a command, prohibition, news, or inquiry except when [it] exists.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Ibn Kullab as transmitted by the Ash'ari school. It states that God's speech (kalam) is eternal (in al-azal), but its specific qualities—such as being a command (amr), prohibition (nahy), news (khabar), or inquiry (istikhbar)—are not attributed to it in eternity. Rather, these attributes apply only 'when it exists' (illa 'inda wujud). The phrase is ambiguous: it likely means that the speech becomes actually a command when the commanded thing exists, or that the speech is not characterized as command, etc., until the time when the speech is addressed to someone or something comes into existence. This preserves the eternality of the divine attribute of speech while affirming that its specific modalities are contingent upon creation. The Ash'aris thus distinguish between the eternal essence of speech and its temporal qualifications.

Key Terms
أشعرية
كلابي
كلام الباري
الأزل
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's speech is not a command in eternity, then it is not eternal.
Correction: The Ash'aris (following Ibn Kullab) hold that speech is eternal in its essence, but its specific modalities (command, prohibition, etc.) are relational and only apply when there is an existing object or recipient.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when does Ibn Kullab say that God's speech is characterized as command, prohibition, news, or inquiry?
A) In eternity without condition.
B) Only when it is recited by humans.
C) Except when the thing exists.
D) Only after the creation of the world.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Kullab held that God's eternal speech is not actually a command, prohibition, etc., until it exists in relation to its object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's command only becomes a command when it exists for you, does that mean you can ignore it until it 'exists' in your consciousness? What would it take for you to live as if God's eternal speech is already here?
?
You often think of God's speech as a book on your shelf. But this lesson says its modalities depend on existence. Is your relationship with the Quran built on its eternal nature or on what it demands of you now? Which is more urgent?
?
The Ash'aris preserved the eternity of speech while honoring its relevance. Where in your life do you treat God's commands as eternal abstractions rather than present realities? What is one command you will obey today as if it exists just for you?

Ibn Kullab on Divine Speech: Eternal Essence, Temporal Properties
Ibn Kullab explains that divine speech's properties are temporal except for information.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Kullab's distinction between the eternal essence of divine speech and its temporal properties like command and prohibition.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal; its commanding nature arises only when addressees appear.

Ibn Kullab, a foundational figure in Ash'ari theology, proposed a nuanced view of divine speech. He argued that while speech itself is eternal, its identification as command, prohibition, etc., depends on the existence of addressees. This passage explains his reasoning and compares these properties to attributes of acts like creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between eternal speech and its temporal descriptions.
Pay attention to the analogy with attributes of acts.
2
Identify the thesis
Ibn Kullab states that speech in preeternity is not described as command, prohibition, etc., except when addressees exist. Identify this key claim.
3
Understand the analogy
Compare 'command, prohibition' to 'creates, provides' — these are attributes of acts that are temporal relations, not eternal.
This helps see that God's essence does not change.
4
Analyze the exception for information
Ibn Kullab says speech is always 'information' even in preeternity because it does not require an addressee. Why does he need to say this?
It preserves the categories of speech without implying change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : ذهب شيخنا الكلابي عبد الله بن سعيد إلى أن كلام الباري في الأزل لا يتصف بكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبارًا إلا عند وجود المخاطبين واستجماعهم شرايط التكليف، فإذا أبدع الله العباد وأفهمهم كلامه على قضية أمر وموجب زجر أو مقتضى خبر، اتصف عند ذلك بهذه الأحكام، فهي عنده من صفات الأفعال بمثابة اتصاف الباري تعالى فيما لا يزال بكونه خالقا ورازقا، فهو في نفسه كلام لنفسه أمر ونهي وخبر وخطاب وتكليم لا لنفسه، بل بالنسبة إلى المخاطب وحال تعلقه، وإنما يقول : كلامه في الأزل يتصف بكونه خبرا؛ لأنا لو لم نصفه بذلك خرج الكلام عن أقسامه، ولأن الخبر لا يستدعي مخاطبًا، فإن الرب تعالى مخبر لم يزل عن ذاته وصفاته، وعما سيكون من أفعاله، وعما سيكلف عباده بالأوامر والنواهي.
The Ash'aris said: Our teacher al-Kullabi, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id, held that the speech of the Eternal (God) in preeternity is not described as being a command, prohibition, information, or inquiry except when the addressees exist and they fulfill the conditions of obligation. When God creates the servants and makes them understand His speech according to the circumstance of a command, a cause of deterrence, or the requirement of an information, it becomes described with these rulings at that time. So in his view, they are among the attributes of acts, analogous to the Eternal's being described as creator and provider. So it is in itself speech for itself; command, prohibition, information, address, and speaking are not for itself, but rather relative to the addressee and the state of its relation. He only says: His speech in preeternity is described as being information because if we do not describe it as such, speech would fall outside its categories, and because information does not require an addressee. Indeed, the Lord is eternal informer about His essence, His attributes, what will be of His actions, and what He will obligate His servants with of commands and prohibitions.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage expounds the view of Ibn Kullab (al-Kullabi) regarding the divine attribute of speech (kalām). He distinguishes between the eternal essence of speech and its temporal properties (command, prohibition, etc.). These properties only arise when there are actual addressees who meet the conditions of obligation. This makes them 'attributes of acts' (ṣifāt al-afʿāl), similar to creation and providing, which are temporal. In preeternity, God's speech is only described as information (khabar) because information does not require an addressee—God eternally informs about Himself, His attributes, and future events. This preserves the categories of speech without implying change in God's essence.

Key Terms
الكلابي
كلام الباري
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار
صفات الأفعال
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech changes over time because it can be a command, prohibition, etc.
Correction: Ibn Kullab clarifies that the speech itself is eternal; only its relational properties (command, prohibition) are temporal, arising when addressees exist.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Kullab, when does God's speech become described as a command or prohibition?
A) In preeternity
B) Only when addressees exist and fulfill conditions of obligation
C) After the creation of the universe
D) After the revelation to prophets

Key Takeaway
Ibn Kullab maintained that divine speech is eternal in essence, but its specific forms (command, etc.) are temporal relations to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech as a command only applies when you exist and are addressed, do you truly live as if every command in the Quran is a direct, eternal word from Him to you? What would change if you fully embraced that reality today?
?
Ibn Kullab says God is eternally informing about Himself. Are you genuinely seeking that information in your daily life, or are you content with ignorance? What one step can you take this week to learn more about His essence as revealed in His speech?
?
The temporal nature of command and prohibition means they are relevant only for those who hear and can respond. Are you responding to God's commands with the urgency of someone who knows they are addressed directly, or do you delay and make excuses? Write one command you have been ignoring and commit to acting on it.

Al-Ash'ari on Eternal Divine Speech
Al-Ash'ari's view on eternal divine speech and objection response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's position that divine speech is eternally command, prohibition, and information, and how he defends the coherence of an eternal command addressing a nonexistent but known creature.

Core Idea
God's eternal command applies to all future beings on condition of existence.

Al-Ash'ari differs from Ibn Kullab by insisting that God's speech is not just a single attribute but includes command, prohibition, and information from eternity. This raises the objection: how can there be a command without an addressee? Al-Ash'ari responds by noting that the commanded action may be future, and the addressee is known to God even if not yet created. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Ash'ari uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the objection and response
The passage presents an implicit objection: how can an eternal command exist without an eternal addressee? Al-Ash'ari's response is based on the condition of existence. Find how he argues.
The key phrase is 'on the condition of existence' (على تقدير الوجود).
3
Reflect on the analogy
Al-Ash'ari supports his response with an analogy: we today are commanded by the same eternal command given to the Prophet's contemporaries. Consider why this analogy strengthens his argument.
This shows that delay does not affect the eternal nature of the command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند أبي الحسن الأشعري كلام الباري تعالى لم يزل متصفا بكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا، والمعدوم على أصله مأمور بالأمر الأزلي على تقدير الوجود، ثم قال في دفع السؤال : إذا لم يبعد أن يكون المأمور به معدوما لم يبعد أن يكون المأمور معلوما، وعضد ذلك بأنا في وقتنا مأمورون بأمر الله تعالى الذي توجه على المأمورين في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإذا لم يبعد أن يتأخر وجود المأمور عن الأمر بسنة لم يبعد أن يتأخر عنه بأكثر ولم يزل.
According to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the speech of the Eternal is eternally described as being a command, prohibition, and information. The nonexistent, according to his principle, is commanded by the eternal command on the condition of existence. Then he said in refuting the objection: If it is not far-fetched that the commanded thing be nonexistent, it is not far-fetched that the commanded person be known. He supported that by saying: We, in our time, are commanded by the command of God which was directed to those commanded in the time of the Prophet, peace be upon him. So if it is not far-fetched that the existence of the commanded be delayed from the command by a year, it is not far-fetched that it be delayed by more, and it remains eternal.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who differs from Ibn Kullab. Al-Ash'ari holds that God's speech is eternally described as command, prohibition, and information—these properties are essential and eternal, not temporal. The objection arises: how can a command exist without an addressee? Al-Ash'ari responds by noting that the commanded action (al-ma'mūr bihi) may be nonexistent (future), so it is not far-fetched that the commanded person (al-ma'mūr) be nonexistent but known to God. He supports this by pointing out that we today are commanded by the same eternal command given to the Prophet's contemporaries, even though we were nonexistent then. Thus delay does not negate the eternal nature of the command.

Key Terms
الأشعري
الأمر الأزلي
المعدوم
المأمور
دفع السؤال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal command requires an eternal addressee.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the command applies on condition of existence, so the addressee need not be eternal.

Checkpoint
How does al-Ash'ari respond to the objection that an eternal command requires an eternal addressee?
A) He denies that command implies an addressee
B) He says the addressee is known to God even if not existing
C) He says command is metaphorical
D) He says the command is not really eternal

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defends the eternity of divine command by showing that a nonexistent but known addressee is coherent, just as a future commanded action is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God’s command is one and eternal, addressing you even before you existed. Do you truly live as if every moment of your life stands under that single, ancient command? Which specific command of God do you most often treat as if it belongs only to a past generation, and what will you change today to show it applies to you now?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that a nonexistent person can be known and commanded by God. If God knows every sin you will ever commit before you commit it, does that make you more or less responsible? How does this truth challenge the way you think about hidden sins or future obedience?
?
The same eternal command that was given to the Prophet's companions is directed at you right now. If you truly believed that God’s command is not distant but immediate and timeless, what one area of your life would you stop delaying obedience in? What is stopping you from acting on that belief today?

God's Speech: Eternal News, Temporal Commands
Classifies divine speech attributes as either eternal or arising upon address.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of divine speech attributes into attributes of action and the special case of news being eternal.

Core Idea
Divine speech includes eternal news but temporal commands.

This passage from a classical Ash'ari source clarifies a nuance about God's speech. While commands and prohibitions arise only when there are creatures to address, the attribute of informing (khabar) is eternal because God always knows and can inform about Himself and future events. We'll see how this preserves the eternal nature of speech while accommodating created recipients.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the distinction between speech in itself and speech in relation to the addressee.
Pay attention to the terms: أمر, نهي, خبر, خطاب, تكليم.
2
Identify the attributes listed
The passage lists command, prohibition, news, address, and speaking. Which of these are considered attributes of action? Which are described as eternal?
The author argues that news is eternal, while the others arise upon addressing.
3
Focus on the argument for news being eternal
Why does the author insist that news must be described as eternal? The passage gives two reasons: without it, speech would lose a division; and news does not require an addressee.
Contrast with command and prohibition which require an addressee.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This preserves the unity and eternity of God's speech while accounting for temporal address. God is eternally the informer even before creatures exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخاطبين واستجماعهم شرايط التكليف، فإذا أبدع الله العباد وأفهمهم كلامه على قضية أمر وموجب زجر أو مقتضى خبر، اتصف عند ذلك بهذه الأحكام، فهي عنده من صفات الأفعال بمثابة اتصاف الباري تعالى فيما لا يزال بكونه خالقا ورازقا، فهو في نفسه كلام لنفسه أمر ونهي وخبر وخطاب وتكليم لا لنفسه، بل بالنسبة إلى المخاطب وحال تعلقه، وإنما يقول: كلامه في الأزل يتصف بكونه خبرا؛ لأنا لو لم نصفه بذلك خرج الكلام عن أقسامه، ولأن الخبر لا يستدعي مخاطبًا، فإن الرب تعالى مخبر لم يزل عن ذاته وصفاته، وعما سيكون من أفعاله، وعما سيكلف عباده بالأوامر والنواهي.
Those addressed and that they fulfill the conditions of obligation. So when God creates servants and makes them understand His speech upon a matter of command and a cause of prohibition or a requirement of news, He becomes described at that time by these judgments. They are, according to this view, from the attributes of action, like the description of the Exalted Lord as eternally being creator and provider. So it is in itself speech; it is command, prohibition, news, address, and speaking not for itself, but in relation to the addressee and the state of its attachment. And he says: His speech in eternity is described as being news because if we did not describe it as such, speech would exit its divisions, and because news does not require an addressee, for the Lord is an eternal informer about His essence and attributes, and about what will be of His actions, and about what He will oblige His servants with commands and prohibitions.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage classifies the attributes of divine speech—command, prohibition, news, address, and speaking—as attributes of action (ṣifāt al-afʿāl) according to some scholars. These attributes are not eternal in themselves but arise only when God creates servants and addresses them. The passage then argues that even on this view, news (khabar) must be described as eternal; otherwise speech would lose one of its essential divisions. It notes that news does not require an addressee—God is eternally informing about Himself, His attributes, and future events. Thus the eternal character of news is preserved even if command and prohibition are not eternal.

Misconception: Some may think that because some speech attributes are temporal, all of God's speech is temporal.
Correction: The passage clarifies that news (khabar) is eternal because it does not require a recipient; God is eternally the informer about Himself and future events.

Checkpoint
According to the view presented in this passage, which of the following attributes of divine speech is described as eternal?
A) Command (amr)
B) Prohibition (nahy)
C) News (khabar)
D) Address (khiṭāb)

Key Takeaway
While commands and prohibitions are temporal attributes of action, news (khabar) is eternal because God is eternally the informer about Himself, His attributes, and future events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's news about you—your essence, your actions, your response to His commands—has been eternally known to Him. If that news includes your disobedience, what comfort can you find except in His mercy? What specific sin will you repent from today because you realize His command is a temporal call you must answer now?
?
You hear God's commands and prohibitions in time, but His news about your obedience is eternal. Does that make you feel trapped or liberated? How will you face the command you have been avoiding, knowing that the One who commands you has always known whether you will obey?
?
If news is eternal and does not require an addressee, then God's speech about you is not dependent on your existence. How does that perspective humble you? What does it mean for your daily choices, knowing that your actions are already recorded in His eternal news?

Ash'ari's View on Eternal Command
Presents Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's view that divine speech is eternally command, prohibition, and news.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari argued that God's eternal command applies to non-existent persons conditionally, based on future existence.

Core Idea
God's eternal command addresses future existence, not current presence.

What does it mean for God's speech to be eternally commanding, even when the person commanded does not yet exist? Ash'ari defends this seemingly paradoxical notion with a clear logical analogy. In this lesson, we explore his reasoning and see how he distinguishes between the eternal nature of the command and the temporal obligation of the commanded person.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of Ash'ari's argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the compact structure: statement → objection → reply → supporting analogy.
2
Identify the objection and Ash'ari's answer
The objection is about commanding a non-existent person. Ash'ari answers by comparing the commanded act (which can be non-existent) to the commanded person. Understand why he thinks the two cases are analogous.
The key phrase: 'if it is not far-fetched that the commanded thing is non-existent...'
3
Examine the supporting example
Ash'ari uses the example of commands from the Prophet's time that still apply to later generations. Reflect on how this shows that a command can be eternal while its obligation is future.
The delay of a year is extended to any duration, showing the principle is general.
4
Summarize the doctrine
Write a one-sentence summary of Ash'ari's view on eternal command and how it applies to non-existent persons. This will help internalize the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند أبي الحسن الأشعري كلام الباري تعالى لم يزل متصفا بكونه أمرا ونهيا وخبرا، والمعدوم على أصله مأمور بالأمر الأزلي على تقدير الوجود، ثم قال في دفع السؤال : إذا لم يبعد أن يكون المأمور به معدوما لم يبعد أن يكون المأمور معلوما، وعضد ذلك بأنا في وقتنا مأمورون بأمر الله تعالى الذي توجه على المأمورين في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإذا لم يبعد أن يتأخر وجود المأمور عن الأمر بسنة لم يبعد أن يتأخر عنه بأكثر ولم يزل.
And according to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the speech of the Exalted Lord is eternally described as being command, prohibition, and news. The non-existent, on his principle, is commanded by the eternal command upon the condition of existence. Then he said in repelling the objection: If it is not far-fetched that the commanded thing is non-existent, it is not far-fetched that the commanded person is known. And he supported that with the fact that we in our time are commanded by the command of God that was directed to those commanded in the time of the Prophet, peace be upon him. So if it is not far-fetched that the existence of the commanded person is delayed from the command by a year, it is not far-fetched that it is delayed from it by more and forever.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage states Ash'ari's position that God's speech is eternally command, prohibition, and news—these descriptions are eternal. He then addresses an objection: how can a non-existent person be commanded? Ash'ari replies that if it is acceptable for the commanded act (al-ma'mūr bihi) to be non-existent at the time of command, then it is also acceptable for the commanded person (al-ma'mūr) to be non-existent; the command is conditional on future existence. He supports this with the example of commands given in the Prophet's time still applying to later generations—the command is eternal, but the obligation arises when the person exists. Thus delay does not invalidate the eternal command.

Key Terms
الأمر الأزلي
تقدير الوجود
مأمور
دفع السؤال
أمر
نهي
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent person cannot be commanded at all, so God's command must be created temporally.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that commanding a non-existent is permissible when the command is conditional on future existence, just as the commanded act can be future. The command remains eternal.

Checkpoint
What is Ash'ari's main argument for the eternal command applying to non-existent persons?
A) The command itself must become existent when the person exists.
B) If the commanded act can be non-existent, the person can also be non-existent at the time of command.
C) The non-existent is not commanded.
D) The command is renewed with each person.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari affirms that God's eternal command is conditional on future existence, so it can address non-existent persons without being temporally created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal command obligates you before you were even born, how seriously do you take the commands that you learn about today? In what ways do you act as if they are optional or only applicable when convenient?
?
The Ash'ari analogy shows that commands given in the Prophet's time still bind you now. Have you ever treated a command of God as 'for them, not for me'? What would change if you truly believed that every command in the Qur'an and Sunnah is an eternal address to you personally?
?
Ash'ari says 'it is not far-fetched' that the command precedes the commanded person by forever. When you delay obedience, are you treating God's command as if it will expire or as if it has no authority over you? What is one command you have been delaying, and what will you do today to fulfill it?

The Relation of Eternal Attributes to Non-Existent Objects
Extends the objection to all eternal attributes and resolves it via attribute suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how eternal divine attributes like knowledge and power relate to non-existent objects through the attribute's intrinsic suitability, not through a direct link to the non-existent.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes relate to non-existents through their capacity, not through a current link.

The objection that an eternal command cannot relate to a non-existent commanded thing is just one instance of a broader problem: how can any eternal attribute relate to something that does not yet exist? In this passage, al-Shahrastani argues that the solution lies in understanding that attributes like knowledge and power have an inherent suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) for all possible objects, not a specific attachment to currently existing things. He also addresses hearing and sight, which only relate to existents, showing a nuanced view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader objection
Notice that the passage begins by stating the objection applies to every eternal attribute. Understand that this is an expansion from just the command case.
2
Grasp the role of suitability (ṣalāḥiyya)
The key solution is that attributes like knowledge are 'suitable for perceiving everything conceivable' and power 'suitable for bringing into existence everything valid.' This is how they relate to non-existents—through their capacity.
3
See the special case of hearing and sight
Unlike knowledge and power, hearing and sight only relate to existents. The passage uses this to clarify that the eternal command is similar: it only relates to the commanded thing when it exists and is capable of obligation.
This shows that not all attributes relate to non-existents in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن هذا الإشكال لا يختص بمسألة الأمر، بل هو جارٍ في كل صفة أزلية تتعلق بمتعلقها أزلا أنها كيف تتعلق بالمعدوم؟ أليس الله تعالى عالما قادرا والعالم معدوم ؟ وكيف يتعلق العلم والقدرة بنفي محض وعدم صرف أفعلى تقدير الوجود؟ فكيف يتصور التقدير في حق الباري والتقدير ترديد الفكر وتصريف الخواطر، وذلك من عمل الخيال والوهم أم يتعلق بالوجود حقيقة والوجود محصور متناه؟ ونحن نعتقد أن معلوماته ومقدوراته لا تتناهى، وإنما ذلك فيما لم يوجد، ويمكن أن يوجد أم تقرر أن العلم صفة صالحة لدرك كل يتصور ما يعرض عليه من غير قصور والقدرة صالحة لإيجاد كل ما يصح وجوده من غير تقاصر، ثم ما يصح أن يعلم ويجوز أن يوجد لا يتناهى. فعلى هذا المعنى نقول : المعلومات والمقدورات لا تتناهى، والمتعلق من حيث المتعلق راجع إلى صلاحية الصفة للكل، ومن حيث المتعلق راجع إلى صحة المعلوم والمقدور، وكذلك قولنا في السمع والبصر وكونه سميعاً بصيراً، بل الجمع بين المسألتين هاهنا أظهر فإن السمع لا يتعلق بالمعدوم وكذلك البصر، فلا يكون المعدوم مسموعا ومبصراً، بل إنما يصير مدركًا بهما حيث يصح الإدراك، وهو حال الوجود فقط لا قبله تحقيقا كان أو تقديرا، كذلك الأمر الأزلي يتعلق بالمأمور به حتى يصح التعلق وهو حال الوجود المتهيأ لقبوله من كونه حيا عاقلا بالغا
And the truth is that this objection is not specific to the issue of command, but rather it applies to every eternal attribute that relates to its object eternally: how does it relate to the non-existent? Is God not knowing and powerful while the known is non-existent? How do knowledge and power relate to pure negation and sheer non-existence? Or upon the condition of existence? So how is the condition (taqdir) imagined in the case of God, while condition (taqdir) is the oscillation of thought and the management of thoughts, which is the work of imagination and fancy? Or does it relate to existence in reality while existence is limited and finite? Yet we believe that His objects of knowledge and objects of power are infinite, and that is only in what does not exist but can exist. Or is it established that knowledge is an attribute suitable for perceiving everything that can be conceived, without deficiency, and power is suitable for bringing into existence everything whose existence is valid, without inability. Then that which can be known and may exist is infinite. So in this sense we say: the objects of knowledge and objects of power are infinite. The relation (al-muta'allaq) in respect of the attribute goes back to the suitability of the attribute for all, and in respect of the object goes back to the validity of the known and the possible. And similarly our statement regarding hearing and sight and His being hearing and seeing. Rather, the combination of the two issues here is more evident, for hearing does not relate to the non-existent, and likewise sight; so the non-existent is not heard nor seen. Rather, it becomes perceived by them where perception is valid, and that is only in the state of existence, not before it, whether actual or conditional. Likewise, the eternal command relates to the commanded thing such that the relation is valid, and that is in the state of existence prepared to accept it, being alive, rational, and mature.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage resolves the earlier objection by showing it applies to all eternal attributes (knowledge, power, hearing, sight, etc.), not just to command. The solution is that the relation of an eternal attribute to its objects is based on the attribute's suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) for all possible objects, not on a specific current attachment. For hearing and sight, the relation only occurs when the object exists; for knowledge and power, they encompass both existent and non-existent in that the attribute is capable of knowing or creating anything that can be known or created. The infinite range of objects is explained by the attribute's capacity. The eternal command similarly relates to the commanded thing only when it exists and is capable of obligation (alive, rational, mature). Thus the objection is resolved by reinterpreting the relation as conditional on the object's existence while the attribute itself remains eternal and unconditioned.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute must have an eternal object to which it relates.
Correction: Eternal attributes relate to objects through their suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) for all possible objects, not through a current link to an eternal object.

A) Knowledge directly perceives non-existents as actual.

C) Non-existents cannot be known.
D) Knowledge only relates to existents.

Key Takeaway
Eternal attributes relate to non-existent objects through the attribute's intrinsic suitability for all possible objects, not through a direct link to the non-existent.

The Eternal Attributes and Their Relation to Non-Existent Objects
The author addresses the objection about eternal attributes relating to non-existent objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the idea that God's eternal attributes can relate to non-existent objects, and the concept of suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) as the basis for this relation.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes relate to objects through suitability, not existence.

The objection that God's eternal attributes cannot relate to non-existent objects is a common philosophical challenge. The author addresses it by clarifying that the relation is based on the attribute's inherent suitability to its object, not the object's current existence. Let's see how this argument unfolds, starting with the objection and its scope, then the author's answer, and finally its application to other attributes like hearing, sight, and command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the flow: objection, answer, examples.
The passage is a continuous argument—notice how the author moves from the general objection to specific attributes.
2
Identify the objection and its scope
The objection asks: how can an eternal attribute relate to a non-existent object? The author says this question applies to all eternal attributes, not just command.
The objector doubts the very possibility of relation to non-existence.
3
Understand the answer: suitability (ṣalāḥiyya)
The author explains that knowledge and power are inherently suited to apprehend or create anything that can be known or created, regardless of its current existence. The relation is based on the attribute's fitness, not the object's existence.
This shifts the focus from the object's state to the attribute's capacity.
4
Apply the answer to other attributes
The same logic applies to hearing and sight (they relate only when objects exist) and to the eternal command (it relates when the commanded is alive, rational, capable).
The author shows that the objection is not unique to speech—it's solved consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن هذا الإشكال لا يختص بمسألة الأمر، بل هو جارٍ في كل صفة أزلية تتعلق بمتعلقها أزلا أنها كيف تتعلق بالمعدوم؟ أليس الله تعالى عالما قادرا والعالم [؟] معدوم؟ وكيف يتعلق العلم والقدرة بنفي محض وعدم صرف أفعلي تقدير الوجود؟ فكيف يتصور التقدير في حق الباري والتقدير ترديد الفكر وتصريف الخواطر، وذلك من عمل الخيال والوهم أم يتعلق بالوجود حقيقة والوجود محصور متناه؟ ونحن نعتقد أن معلوماته ومقدوراته لا تتناهى، وإنما ذلك فيما لم يوجد، ويمكن أن يوجد أم تقرر أن العلم صفة صالحة لدرك كل يتصور ما يعرض عليه من غير قصور والقدرة صالحة لإيجاد كل ما يصح وجوده من غير تقاصر، ثم ما يصح أن يعلم ويجوز أن يوجد لا يتناهى.

فعلى هذا المعنى نقول: المعلومات والمقدورات لا تتناهى، والمتعلق من حيث المتعلق راجع إلى صلاحية الصفة للكل، ومن حيث المتعلق راجع إلى صحة المعلوم والمقدور، وكذلك قولنا في السمع والبصر وكونه سميعا بصيرا، بل الجمع بين المسألتين هاهنا أظهر فإن السمع لا يتعلق بالمعدوم وكذلك البصر، فلا يكون المعدوم مسموعا ومبصرا، بل إنما يصير مدركا بهما حيث يصح الإدراك، وهو حال الوجود فقط لا قبله تحقيقا كان أو تقديرا، كذلك الأمر الأزلي يتعلق بالمأمور به حتى يصح التعلق وهو حال الوجود المتهي لقبوله من كونه حيا عاقلا بالغا متمكنا من الفعل كسائر الصفات على السواء، فليس يختص السؤال بمسألة الكلام

ووجه الحال ما قد سبق التقرير به.
And the truth is that this objection is not specific to the issue of command, rather it applies to every eternal attribute that relates to its object eternally: how does it relate to the non-existent? Is not God Most High knowing and powerful, while the known [?] is non-existent? And how do knowledge and power relate to pure negation and mere non-existence, or to an estimation of existence? So how is estimation conceived in relation to the Eternal, while estimation is the turning of thought and the management of ideas, and that is the work of imagination and illusion? Or does it relate to existence in reality while existence is limited and finite? And we believe that His objects of knowledge and power are infinite, and that is with respect to what has not existed and can exist. Or is it established that knowledge is an attribute fit to apprehend everything conceivable that is presented to it without deficiency, and power is fit to bring into existence everything whose existence is possible without failure, and then what can be known and may exist is infinite.

Thus, in this sense we say: the objects of knowledge and power are infinite. The relation, in terms of the relation, goes back to the suitability of the attribute for all; and in terms of the related object, it goes back to the soundness of the known and the possible. And likewise our statement concerning hearing and sight and His being Hearing and Seeing. Rather, the combination between the two issues is more evident here, for hearing does not relate to the non-existent, and likewise sight. So the non-existent is not heard or seen, but rather it becomes perceived through them only when perception is sound, that is, only in the state of existence, not before it, whether in actuality or estimation. Likewise, the eternal command relates to the commanded thing until the relation becomes sound, and that is the state of existence prepared to receive it, from its being alive, rational, mature, capable of action, like all other attributes equally. So the question is not specific to the issue of speech.

And the explanation of the matter is as previously established.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first states that a certain objection is not limited to the attribute of command but applies to all eternal attributes. The objection asks: how can an eternal attribute relate to a non-existent object? For example, God is knowing and powerful even when the objects of knowledge and power are non-existent. How can knowledge and power relate to pure non-existence? The objector questions the very concept of estimation (taqdīr) in relation to God, since estimation involves mental activity. The author then answers by establishing that the objects of God's knowledge and power are infinite. The relation of an attribute to its object is not contingent on the object's existence; rather, it is based on the attribute's suitability (ṣalāḥiyya). Knowledge is fit to apprehend whatever is presented, and power is fit to create whatever is possible. The same applies to hearing and sight: they relate only to existent objects, not to non-existent ones, but God hears and sees only when perception is possible, i.e., at the moment of existence. Similarly, the eternal command relates to the commanded thing only when the subject is alive, rational, and capable. Thus, the objection does not specifically target the issue of divine speech. The conclusion is that the matter is as previously explained.

Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge and power require the actual existence of their objects.
Correction: The author clarifies that God's knowledge and power relate to objects based on the attributes' suitability to know or create them, not on the objects' current existence.

C) Because God's power is unlimited, so He can know and do everything.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal attributes relate to objects through their inherent suitability, not through the objects' existence.

The Reduction of Divine Speech Categories
The author refutes the claim that divine speech must have the usual human divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech does not require the same divisions as human speech and how the author reduces them to statement and command.

Core Idea
Divine speech categories reduce to two: statement and command.

This passage addresses a common objection: that God's speech must have the usual divisions of human speech to be intelligible. The author counters by questioning those divisions and showing that many categories like interrogation and promise are really forms of statement or command. This clarifies the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break; focus on the argument up to that point.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim that a speech without divisions is unintelligible. Notice how the author responds by asking what those divisions are.
3
Follow the reduction argument
The author shows that the many proposed divisions can be reduced to two: statement and command. Trace how interrogation, promise, threat, and call are handled.
Pay special attention to how the interrogative is reinterpreted as confirmation.
4
Understand the treatment of command and prohibition
The author explains that command and prohibition do not combine, and that recommendation vs. obligation depends on the presence of a deterrent.
The text cuts off before completing the prohibition side, but the pattern is clear.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters for understanding divine speech as an attribute. The reduction avoids anthropomorphism and preserves divine transcendence.
This connects to the broader Aqeedah of Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن كلاما لا تتحقق له أقسام الكلام غير معقول.

قلنا: وما أقسام الكلام؟ فإن المتكلمين حصروها في ستة وسائر الناس زادوا أقساما مثل: النداء والدعاء، وزادوا في كل قسم من الأمر والنهي أقساما، مثل: أمر الندب وأمر الإيجاب ونهي التنزيه ونهي التحريم وفي كل قسم من الخبر والاستخبار أقساما، مثل: الخبر عن الماضي والمستقبل والمواجهة والمغايبة، وغير ذلك، ومن تصدى ليردها إلى ستة فقد قضى بتداخل أقسام منها في أقسام، ولغيره أن يتصدى لردها إلى قسمين الخبر والأمر، أما الاستخبار فلا يتصور في حقه تعالى على موجب حقيقة الاستفهام، بل حيث ورد فمعناه التقرير والإخبار كقوله تعالى: "أَلَسْتُ بِرَبِّكُمْ قَالُوا بَلَى" [؟] من غير الله إله مع الله، ومعنى الكل راجع إلى تقرير الخطاب للمخاطب، أن الأمر لا يتصور إلا كذلك.

وأما الوعد والوعيد فظاهر أنهما خبران يتعلق أحدهما بثواب فسمي وعدا، وتعلق الثاني بعقاب فسمي وعيدا، كما أمكن أن يرد النداء إلى الخبر: يا زيد، يا عمرو؛ أي: ادعو زيدا.

وأما القسم الثاني وهو الأمر فهو والنهي لا يجتمعان، لكن كلام الله تعالى إذا تعلق بمتعلق خاص على صيغة الأمر، ولم يتصل بتركه زجرا كان ندبا، وإن اتصل به زجرا سمي ذلك إيجابا، وكذلك النهي إذا لم يرد على فعله وعيد
Their statement: 'A speech that does not have the divisions of speech is unintelligible.'

We said: 'And what are the divisions of speech? The mutakallimun have limited them to six, while other people added divisions such as call and supplication, and they added in each division of command and prohibition subdivisions, such as command of recommendation and command of obligation, and prohibition of disapproval and prohibition of prohibition, and in each division of statement and interrogation, subdivisions, such as statement about the past and the future, and addressing the present and the absent, and so on. Whoever attempts to reduce them to six has judged that some divisions overlap with others, and another person may attempt to reduce them to two: the statement and the command. As for the interrogative, it cannot be conceived with respect to Him Most High according to the true meaning of interrogation; rather, wherever it occurs, its meaning is confirmation and declaration, like His saying: "Am I not your Lord? They said, Yes" [؟] besides God no god with God, and the meaning of all goes back to confirming the address to the addressee, that the matter cannot be conceived except thus.

As for promise and threat, it is apparent that they are two statements: one relates to reward and is called promise, and the second relates to punishment and is called threat; similarly, it is possible to reduce the call to a statement: "O Zayd, O Amr" meaning: I call Zayd.

As for the second division, which is the command, it and the prohibition do not combine. But the speech of God Most High, if it relates to a specific object in the form of a command and no deterrent against abandoning it is attached, it is a recommendation; if a deterrent is attached, that is called an obligation. Likewise, the prohibition if no threat is attached to its commission...'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that a speech without the normal divisions (like command, prohibition, etc.) is unintelligible, implying that God's speech must have these divisions. The author responds by asking what those divisions are. He notes that the mutakallimun have limited them to six, while others add more like call (al-nidā') and supplication (al-du'ā'), and further subdivide command and prohibition into categories like recommendation and obligation. The author then argues that one could reduce all divisions to two: statement (khabar) and command (amr). He explains that the interrogative (istikhbār) is not truly applicable to God; when it appears in revelation, it is actually a confirmation or declaration, as in the verse "Am I not your Lord?" The call (al-nidā') can be reduced to a statement (e.g., 'O Zayd' means 'I call Zayd'). Promise and threat (wa'd and wa'īd) are clearly statements about reward and punishment. As for command and prohibition, they do not combine; if the command is without a deterrent, it is a recommendation; if with a deterrent, it is an obligation. Similarly, prohibition without a threat attached to its commission is a prohibition of disapproval, and with a threat it is a prohibition of prohibition. The text ends mid-sentence at the page break, but the argument is that all divisions reduce to statement and command.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech must have the same categorical divisions as human speech (e.g., command, prohibition, interrogative, etc.).
Correction: The author shows that many human categories like interrogation, call, promise, and threat can be reduced to either statement or command, thereby preserving divine speech as a single attribute without anthropomorphic implications.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is the interrogative used in divine speech?
A) It is used to ask for information.
B) It has the meaning of confirmation and declaration.
C) It does not occur in divine speech at all.
D) It is reduced to the category of command.

Key Takeaway
All divisions of speech reduce to statement and command, and categories like interrogation are reinterpreted as confirmation when applied to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Qur'anic verses that begin with 'Am I not your Lord?' or similar questions, do you ever subconsciously imagine that God is uncertain or seeking information? How does this lesson expose that subtle anthropomorphism? Are you willing to repent from any diminished view of God's knowledge and power that such thoughts imply?
?
The author reduces all divine speech to just two categories: statement and command. This means every verse of the Qur'an is either telling you something true or commanding you to do something. Which category do you most resist? Is it the statements that challenge your worldview, or the commands that interrupt your desires? What concrete change will you make today in response to a verse you have been ignoring?
?
If divine speech does not include call, supplication, or interrogation as genuine categories, then your own prayers (du'a) are not 'calling' God in a human sense but rather acting upon a command or acknowledging a statement. Does this realization deepen your reverence in prayer, or expose laziness in your worship? What will you do differently in your next salah or supplication?

The Uncreated Divine Command: Quranic Proofs and Key Distinctions
Author proves divine command is uncreated using Quranic verses and priority argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran distinguishes creation from command, proving the command is uncreated, and how its priority over creation implies eternality.

Core Idea
The divine command is uncreated because the Quran contrasts it with creation and shows its eternal priority.

Sanusi now turns to a specific divine attribute: the command (amr). He argues it is uncreated, drawing on two Quranic verses and a priority argument. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, including a critical distinction between two types of attachment that resolves a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from verses to conclusion.
2
Analyze the First Proof from Contrast
Focus on the verse 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command' (al-A'raf: 54). How does contrasting creation and command imply command is not created?
If command were created, it would be included in 'creation', making the contrast redundant.
3
Examine the Second Proof from Priority
Look at the verse 'Our word to something, when We intend it, is that We say to it "Be", and it is' (al-Nahl: 40). Notice how command precedes the created thing. Why does this priority require eternality?
Priority over all originated things cannot be temporal; it must be essential and eternal.
4
Understand the Distinction that Resolves the Objection
Sanusi distinguishes between attachment of eligibility/correctness (the command is valid even if not executed) and attachment of action/reality (actual creation). How does this distinction refute the claim that command changes?
The command itself remains unchanged; only its relation to the object is conditional.
5
Summarize the Conclusion
From the proofs and distinction, conclude that divine speech is one: whether called command or report, it is distinct from creation and eternal. The command's attachment to temporal objects does not imply change in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ورد التنزيل بتسميته أمرا بحيث يتضمن جميع الأقسام في قوله تعالى: (وَمَا أَمْرُنَا إِلَّا وَاحِدَةٌ) [القمر: 50]، وفي قوله: (أَلَا لَهُ الْخَلْقُ وَالْأَمْرُ) [الأعراف: 54]، فالخلق والأمر يتقابلان كالفعل والقول، وعن هذا أمكن الاستدلال بهذه الآية حتى يقال: إن أمر الباري غير مخلوق، فإنه لو كان مخلوقا، لكان تقدير قوله: (أَلَا لَهُ الْخَلْقُ وَالْأَمْرُ) وهذا من فاسد الكلام، وقد ورد أيضا في القرآن ما يدل على أن الأمر سابق على الخلق، وذلك السبق لن يتصور إلا أن يكون أزليا، وذلك قوله سبحانه: (إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ) [النحل: 40]، والمتكون متأخر والأمر متقدم والتقدم على الحادث المطلق لا يكون إلا بالأزلية فتحقق من هذه الجملة أن كلامه تعالى واحد إن سميته أمرا فهو خلاف الخلق ومقابله، وإن سميته خبرا فهو وفق العلم سواء، وأنه إذا تعلق بالمأمور فيكون تعلقه مشروطا بشرائط، كما كان تعلق سائر الصفات مشروطا بشرائط، وقد عرفت الفرق بين تعلق الصلاحية والصحة، وبين تعلق الفعل والحقيقة واندفع الإلزام بهذا الفرق.
And the revelation has come with naming it 'command' in a way that encompasses all divisions in His saying: 'And Our command is but one' (al-Qamar: 50), and in His saying: 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command' (al-A'raf: 54). So creation and command are contrasted like action and speech. From this it was possible to infer from this verse until it is said: the command of the Exalted is not created, for if it were created, then His saying 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command' would be a estimation, and this is corrupt speech. And also in the Quran there is what indicates that command is prior to creation, and that priority can only be conceived as being eternal, and that is His saying, Exalted: 'Our word to something, when We intend it, is that We say to it "Be", and it is' (al-Nahl: 40). The thing that comes into being is posterior and the command is prior, and priority over the absolute originated thing is only by eternality. So it is established from this statement that His speech, Exalted, is one: if you call it 'command', it is other than creation and its counterpart; if you call it 'report', it is in accordance with knowledge. And that when it attaches to the commanded thing, its attachment is conditional upon conditions, just as the attachment of other attributes is conditional upon conditions. And you have known the difference between the attachment of eligibility and correctness and the attachment of action and reality, and the objection is repelled by this difference.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage proves that the divine command is uncreated. Two Quranic verses are cited: 'And Our command is but one' shows that command is not multiple; 'His is the creation and the command' contrasts creation and command, so if command were created it would be included in 'creation', making the phrase redundant—hence command is not created. A second proof uses al-Nahl 40: command precedes the created thing, and priority over all originated things can only be eternal, so command is eternal. The author then notes that divine speech is one: whether called command or report, it is distinct from creation. He addresses a potential objection: if command attaches to temporal objects, does that imply change? He responds that the attachment is conditional, like other attributes, and distinguishes between two types of attachment: one of eligibility and correctness (the command is valid even if not executed) and one of action and reality (actual creation). This distinction refutes the objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The priority of command over creation implies temporal priority, meaning the command is a temporal entity.
Correction: The author argues for essential priority, not temporal; the command is eternal because it precedes all originated things absolutely.

Checkpoint
How does the Quranic verse 'ألا له الخلق والأمر' (His is the creation and the command) prove that the command is uncreated?
A) It shows command is prior to creation
B) It distinguishes command from creation, implying command is not part of creation
C) It states that command is one
D) It says command is not changed

Key Takeaway
The divine command is uncreated and eternal, distinct from creation, and its attachment to temporal objects does not imply change in God due to the distinction between eligibility and actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak to others, your words change depending on the situation. But God's command is one and eternal, unchanged by what He commands. How does this contrast challenge your habit of making your words conditional on circumstances? Will you strive to speak with more consistency and integrity, reflecting the unchanging nature of divine speech?
?
You often think of God's command as something that happened in the past—'Be, and it was'—but the lesson shows it is eternally present. Do you live as if God's command is still speaking, still creating, still calling you right now? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that?
?
The author distinguishes between attachment of eligibility and attachment of actuality—God's command is valid even when not yet executed. Are there commands of God that you know are correct and eligible but you have not actualized? Why are you delaying, and what would it take to move from eligibility to action today?

The Unity of Divine Speech and the Analogy with Knowledge
Author responds to objection using analogy with knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the unity of divine speech by comparing it to the unity of divine knowledge, which encompasses all objects of knowledge without multiplicity.

Core Idea
One eternal speech can contain many meanings, just as one knowledge contains many objects.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial objection against the doctrine of the uncreatedness of the Quran. Critics argue that since commands and reports have different meanings, God's speech must be multiple. The author responds by drawing a powerful analogy with divine knowledge, showing that multiplicity does not necessitate division in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the first part of the source passage where the author acknowledges the objection and offers his response.
Notice the author agrees that expressions match meanings, but he does not stop there.
2
Understand the analogy with knowledge
The author compares different meanings in divine speech to different objects of knowledge encompassed by one knowledge. Ask yourself: how can one knowledge contain many different things without being divided?
This is the key analogy.
3
Apply the analogy to speech
Just as one knowledge encompasses many objects, one eternal speech ('the true saying') encompasses all commands and reports. Time distinctions do not affect it.
The Quranic verse 'And My word is not changed' supports this.
4
Reflect on the difficulty
The author notes that this is difficult to grasp because we are used to temporal multiplicity. Acknowledge the challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم: إن الاختلاف والكثرة في الأخبار والأوامر لا يرجع إلى العبارات فقط؛ إذ العبارات لا بد وأن تطابق المعنى، صحيح لكن تلك المعاني المختلفة كالمعلومات المختلفة التي يحيط بها علم واحد، وأن تلك المعلومات المختلفة إن فرضت في الشاهد استدعت علومًا مختلفة وإن شملها اسم العلمية، كذلك الإخبار المختلفة والأوامر المختلفة، وإن استدعت في الشاهد كلمات ومعاني مختلفة فقد أحاط بها معنى واحد هو القول الحق الأزلي، واختلاف الزمان بالماضي والمستقبل والحاضر لا يؤثر في نفس القول (وما يبدل القول لدي) كاختلاف الحال في المعلومات التي وجدت وستوجد لا يؤثر في نفس العلم، وهذا المعنى عسير الإدراك جدا لتكرر عهدنا بخلاف ذلك في الشاهد. ولو لاحظنا جانب العقل وإدراكه المعقول وجردناه عن المواد الجسمانية والأمثلة الخيالية، وصادفنا إدراكا كليا عقليا لا يختلف باختلاف الأزمنة، ولا يتغير بتغاير الحوادث، وكذا لو استيقظنا من نوم يقظتنا هذه بمنام، وطلعت نفوسنا على مشرق المعاني والحقائق رأت عاما من المعقولات وأشخاصاً من الروحانيات.
And their saying: that the difference and multiplicity in reports and commands are not due to expressions only, since expressions must correspond to meaning, is correct. But those different meanings are like the different objects of knowledge that are encompassed by one knowledge. And if those different objects of knowledge are assumed in the witnessed realm, they would necessitate different sciences even if the name of knowledge encompasses them. Likewise, different reports and different commands, even if they necessitate in the witnessed realm different words and meanings, yet one meaning encompasses them, which is the eternal truthful saying. And difference in time in terms of past, future, and present does not affect the saying itself—'And My word is not changed'—like the difference of state in objects of knowledge that have existed and will exist does not affect knowledge itself. And this meaning is very difficult to perceive due to the repetition of our custom with the contrary in the witnessed realm. And if we were to consider the aspect of intellect and its apprehension of the intelligible, and strip it of physical materials and imaginary examples, and we encounter a universal intellectual apprehension that does not differ with difference of times nor change with change of events; and likewise if we awoke from the sleep of this wakefulness of ours to a [higher] dream, and our souls rose upon the rising place of meanings and realities, they would see a general [aspect] of intelligibles and individuals of spiritual entities.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if many commands and reports have different meanings, does that imply multiplicity in divine speech? The author first agrees that expressions must match meanings. But he then uses an analogy with divine knowledge: just as one knowledge encompasses many and different objects of knowledge, so one eternal speech (the 'true saying') encompasses all meanings of commands and reports. Time distinctions (past, present, future) do not affect the eternal word, as confirmed by the Quranic verse 'And My word is not changed'. This is difficult to grasp because we are used to temporal multiplicity in the physical world. The author then encourages a kind of intellectual purification: by stripping the intellect from physical and imaginary attachments, one can attain a universal, timeless understanding—like awakening from a dream into a higher reality where one perceives spiritual entities and universal intelligibles. This is meant to help the reader accept the idea of one eternal speech containing many meanings.

Key Terms
اختلاف
عبارات
معاني
علم
قول
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That different commands and reports imply that God's speech changes over time.
Correction: The author argues that all are contained in one eternal speech, unaffected by time, just as one knowledge contains many objects.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to explain how many meanings can be in one divine speech?
A) Many words in one language
B) One knowledge encompassing many objects
C) One cause producing many effects
D) A single light source giving many colors

Key Takeaway
The author uses the unity of divine knowledge to argue that many meanings do not necessitate multiplicity in divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you read the Quran as a single eternal address from God to you right now, or do you unconsciously fragment it into past commands and future promises, losing the weight of its present authority?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses everything and remains unchanged, why do you struggle to believe that His speech commands you today with the same force as it did the first believers? What does that say about your trust in His eternity?
?
When you hear multiple commands in the Quran (prayer, charity, justice), do you feel overwhelmed by multiplicity, or do you seek the one unifying meaning behind them? What would change if you saw them all as expressions of a single eternal word?

Purifying the Intellect to Grasp Divine Speech
Author explains difficulty and calls for intellectual purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's call to purify the intellect from physical attachments and imagination in order to perceive the unity of eternal divine speech.

Core Idea
To perceive the oneness of divine speech, we must transcend material limitations.

The author acknowledges that the concept of one speech containing many meanings is difficult because we are used to multiplicity in the physical world. He then offers a method: stripping the intellect from material forms and engaging in universal intellectual apprehension, akin to awakening from a dream to a higher reality.

Guided Path
1
Acknowledge the difficulty
Read the opening sentence of this section: 'This meaning is very difficult to perceive due to our habit of the contrary in the witnessed realm.' Recognize why it's hard.
2
Understand the method of purification
The author suggests considering the intellect in its pure form, stripped of physical materials and imaginary examples. What does it mean to strip the intellect?
This is a call to abstract thinking.
3
Grasp the goal: universal intellectual apprehension
When purified, the intellect attains a universal apprehension that is unaffected by time and change. This mirrors the eternal nature of divine speech.
4
Analyze the analogy of awakening
The author compares our current state to a dream, and awakening to a higher state where we perceive spiritual realities. How does this support his argument?
This is meant to inspire the reader to seek higher understanding.

Source Text

And their saying: that the difference and multiplicity in reports and commands are not due to expressions only, since expressions must correspond to meaning, is correct. But those different meanings are like the different objects of knowledge that are encompassed by one knowledge. And if those different objects of knowledge are assumed in the witnessed realm, they would necessitate different sciences even if the name of knowledge encompasses them. Likewise, different reports and different commands, even if they necessitate in the witnessed realm different words and meanings, yet one meaning encompasses them, which is the eternal truthful saying. And difference in time in terms of past, future, and present does not affect the saying itself—'And My word is not changed'—like the difference of state in objects of knowledge that have existed and will exist does not affect knowledge itself. And this meaning is very difficult to perceive due to the repetition of our custom with the contrary in the witnessed realm. And if we were to consider the aspect of intellect and its apprehension of the intelligible, and strip it of physical materials and imaginary examples, and we encounter a universal intellectual apprehension that does not differ with difference of times nor change with change of events; and likewise if we awoke from the sleep of this wakefulness of ours to a [higher] dream, and our souls rose upon the rising place of meanings and realities, they would see a general [aspect] of intelligibles and individuals of spiritual entities.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if many commands and reports have different meanings, does that imply multiplicity in divine speech? The author first agrees that expressions must match meanings. But he then uses an analogy with divine knowledge: just as one knowledge encompasses many and different objects of knowledge, so one eternal speech (the 'true saying') encompasses all meanings of commands and reports. Time distinctions (past, present, future) do not affect the eternal word, as confirmed by the Quranic verse 'And My word is not changed'. This is difficult to grasp because we are used to temporal multiplicity in the physical world. The author then encourages a kind of intellectual purification: by stripping the intellect from physical and imaginary attachments, one can attain a universal, timeless understanding—like awakening from a dream into a higher reality where one perceives spiritual entities and universal intelligibles. This is meant to help the reader accept the idea of one eternal speech containing many meanings.

عقل
معقول
روحانيات

A) Physical objects in a new form
B) Universals of intelligibles and individuals of spiritual entities
C) The same world we see now
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
By detaching from matter and imagination, the intellect can attain a timeless understanding that mirrors the eternity of divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
