What if your comfort with multiplicity is a form of shirk in your heart? Will you commit to training your intellect to think beyond the physical?
?
The passage says you must 'awake from the sleep of this wakefulness' to perceive spiritual realities. What would it mean for you to realize that your current waking state is actually a dream? Are you ready to let go of the illusions you cling to for safety and truly seek the truth? When will you take a concrete step to purify your intellect today?
?
If you could see 'a general aspect of intelligibles and individuals of spiritual entities' directly, how might it change your daily priorities? What would you be willing to sacrifice to attain such vision? Are you prepared to act on that answer now?

Defending the Unity of Divine Speech
Al-Shahrastani refutes the objection that multiplicity of commands implies multiplicity in divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani refutes the objection that multiplicity of commands and reports necessitates multiplicity in divine speech, using rational distinctions and analogies from human experience.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one eternal attribute; multiplicity belongs to expression, not essence.

Al-Shahrastani tackles a key objection: if God's commands and reports are many, doesn't that make His speech multiple? Using insights from human knowledge, dreams, and intuition, he shows that multiplicity belongs to expression alone. This lesson walks through his powerful argument, which combines precise distinctions and vivid analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the dense style and the sequence of analogies.
The passage is from al-Shahrastani's 'Nihāyat al-Aqdām'.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection is that different commands and reports imply different divine utterances. Al-Shahrastani's response begins by distinguishing the attachment of knowledge and speech to their objects.
Look for the phrase 'وقد عرفت الفرق' – he references a prior distinction.
3
Analyse the analogies
He uses several analogies: a single knowledge encompassing many knowns, a universal intellectual perception, a dream that contains many images in an instant, the immediate intuition of an answer, and the Quranic grain parable. Each shows unity underlying multiplicity.
Note the progression from abstract (knowledge) to concrete (dream, grain).
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Shahrastani concludes with a qiyas al-awla: if human intellect and soul can unify many meanings, then the eternal divine word is far more singular. Reflect on how this rebuts the objection.
The final sentence 'فكيف الظن' is a rhetorical question affirming transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم سواء، وأنه إذا تعلق بالمأمور فيكون تعلقه مشروطًا بشرائط، كما كان تعلق سائر الصفات مشروطًا بشرائط، وقد عرفت الفرق بين تعلق الصلاحية والصحة، وبين تعلق الفعل والحقيقة واندفع الإلزام بهذا الفرق.
وقولهم : إن الاختلاف والكثرة في الأخبار والأوامر لا يرجع إلى العبارات فقط؛ إذ العبارات لا بد وأن تطابق المعنى، صحيح لكن تلك المعاني المختلفة كالمعلومات المختلفة التي يحيط بها علم واحد، وأن تلك المعلومات المختلفة إن فرضت في الشاهد استدعت علومًا مختلفة وإن شملها اسم العلمية، كذلك الإخبار المختلفة والأوامر المختلفة، وإن استدعت في الشاهد كلمات ومعاني مختلفة فقد أحاط بها معنى واحد هو القول الحق الأزلي، واختلاف الزمان بالماضي والمستقبل والحاضر لا يؤثر في نفس القول ( وما يبدل القول لدي ) كاختلاف الحال في المعلومات التي وجدت وستوجد لا يؤثر في نفس العلم، وهذا المعنى عسير الإدراك جدا لتكرر عهدنا بخلاف ذلك في الشاهد.
ولو لاحظنا جانب العقل وإدراكه المعقول وجردناه عن المواد الجسمانية والأمثلة الخيالية، وصادفنا إدراكًا كليا عقليا لا يختلف باختلاف الأزمنة، ولا يتغير بتغاير الحوادث، وكذا لو استيقظنا من نوم يقظتنا هذه بمنام، وطلعت نفوسنا على مشرق المعاني والحقائق رأت عاما من المعقولات وأشخاصاً من الروحانيات تحدثه بأخبار وتكلمه بأحاديث، لو عبر المعبر عنها بعبارات لسانه لما وسعه يوم واحد لشرحها وبيانها ولو كتبها بقلمه لم تسعه مجلدة واحدة لنظمها وبيانها. وكل ذلك قد يراه في منامه في أقل من لحظة واحدة، ويعلم يقينا أنه رآه حين رآه كأنه شيء واحد ومعنى واحد، لكن التعبير عنه استدعى أوراقاً وسمع ما سمع وصحائف طباقا، وكيف لا والمرء يجد من نفسه وجدانًا ضروريًا أنه إذا سئل عن إشكال في مسألة اعتراه جوابه وحله جملة في أقل من لحظة، ثم يأتي في شرح ذلك بلسانه بعبارات حتى يمتلئ آذان وأسماع كثيرة إن سمعها ووعاها، أو يستثبته بقلمه حتى يسود بياضاً كثيرًا إن سطرها وزيرها والمعنى في الأصل كان واحدا، والشرح منبسطًا والحب يكون واحدا والسنبلة متكثرة ( كَمَثَلِ حَيَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ وَاللَّهُ يُضَعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٦١].
ومن وجد بمشام صدقه شمائم الروحانيات ووقع في أطراف رياض المعقولات، وإن كان محومًا عليها غير واغل في خميلتها علم قطعا أن العقل إحدى الإدراك والنفس وحداني القول، وإنما التكثر في عالم الحس يتصور والاختلاف في عالم العبارات يتحقق، وإذا كانت عقولنا ونفوسنا على هذا النمط من التوحيد، فكيف الظن بقدس الإحاطة الأزلية ووحدانية الكلمة السرمدية.
Knowledge is the same, and that when it attaches to the commanded, its attachment is conditional upon conditions, just as the attachment of the other attributes was conditional upon conditions, and you have known the difference between the attachment of permissibility and validity, and between the attachment of action and reality, and the objection is repelled by this distinction. Their saying: that difference and multiplicity in reports and commands do not return only to expressions; since expressions must correspond to meaning, is correct, but those different meanings are like the different objects of knowledge encompassed by one knowledge, and that those different objects of knowledge if assumed in the witnessed realm necessitate different knowledges even if the name of science encompasses them, similarly the different reports and different commands, even if they necessitate in the witnessed realm words and different meanings, yet they are encompassed by a single meaning which is the eternal truthful speech (al-qawl al-ḥaqq al-azalī), and the difference of time in the past, future, and present does not affect the speech itself — 'Nothing changes the word before Me' — just as the difference of states in the objects of knowledge that have existed and will exist does not affect knowledge itself, and this meaning is very difficult to grasp because of our constant habit contrary to that in the witnessed realm. And if we consider the aspect of intellect and its perception of the intelligible and strip it from physical materials and imaginary examples, and we happen upon a universal intellectual perception that does not differ with the difference of times, nor change with the variation of events, and likewise if we awaken from our sleep of this wakefulness in a dream, and our souls rise to the horizon of meanings and realities, they see a multitude of intelligibles and individuals of spiritual things, which it recounts with reports and speaks with discourses; if the expresser were to express them with the expressions of his tongue, one day would not suffice him for their explanation and exposition, and if he were to write them with his pen, one volume would not contain them for their organization and explanation. And all of that he may see in his dream in less than a single moment, and he knows with certainty that he saw it when he saw it as if it were one thing and one meaning, but expression from it necessitated papers and what he heard and overlapping pages. How could it not be, and a person finds from himself an immediate intuition that when he is asked about a difficulty in a matter, its answer and solution come to him entire in less than a moment, then he proceeds to explain that with his tongue in expressions until ears and many hearings are filled if they hear and comprehend it, or he records it with his pen until he blackens much whiteness if he writes it and arranges it, while the meaning originally was one, and the explanation spread out, and the grain is one and the ear multiplied. 'Like a grain that grows seven ears, in every ear a hundred grains, and Allah multiplies for whom He wills.' [Surah Al-Baqarah 2:261]. And whoever finds with the nostrils of his sincerity the fragrances of spiritualities and alights at the borders of the meadows of intelligibles, even if hovering over them without plunging into their thickets, knows with certainty that the intellect is one of perception and the soul is one of speech; multiplicity is only conceived in the world of sense and difference is realized in the world of expressions. And when our intellects and souls are of this pattern of unity, then what expectation concerning the sanctity of the eternal encompassing and the oneness of the everlasting word?
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a proof defending the unity of divine speech against the objection that multiplicity of commands and reports implies multiplicity in God's speech. The author, al-Shahrastani, first establishes a distinction: divine knowledge is one yet encompasses many known things; similarly, divine speech is one despite encompassing many commands and reports. He then uses analogies from human experience: a person can grasp a complex idea in a moment but requires lengthy expression; this shows that the inner meaning is one while expression is many. He concludes that if human intellect and soul can be unified in this way, then God's eternal word is far more transcendent and singular. The argument follows a pattern: initial distinction, analogy from divine knowledge, analogy from dreams and intuition, and final qiyas al-awla (greater analogy) from human to divine unity.

Correction: Al-Shahrastani clarifies that multiplicity is in the expressions and their temporal manifestations, not in the eternal attribute of speech itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your own life have you experienced this—a moment of sudden clarity that you then struggled to articulate? If your own knowledge can be one while expressed in many words, what does that tell you about the nature of God's speech, which is infinitely higher?
?

What is one command you have been postponing, and will you commit to acting on it today as if it were spoken directly to you in this moment?

Unity of Meaning and Multiplicity of Expression in Divine Speech
Illustrates how a single meaning can be expressed in many words, using analogies and a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single divine meaning can be expressed in multiple ways, as illustrated by human experience and a Quranic verse.

Core Idea
A single meaning can be expressed in many words – this mirrors divine speech.

This passage uses powerful analogies from human experience to explain a key theological point: that divine speech is one in essence but multiple in expression. The author invites us to reflect on how we instantly grasp a complex idea yet need many words to express it. This sets the stage for understanding the unity of the eternal Word.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The Arabic may have unusual terms like 'يستثبته' – focus on the overall meaning.
2
Identify the key analogies
Find the three main analogies: the dream, the instant answer to a question, and the grain verse. Reflect on how each shows a unified meaning that requires many words to express.
The dream analogy is especially vivid: a whole scene seen in a moment but requiring many pages to describe.
3
Connect to divine speech
Realize that if human limited intellect and soul can have unity with multiplicity, then Allah's eternal speech is even more perfectly one in meaning despite multiple expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحدثه بأخبار وتكلمه بأحاديث، لو عبر المعبر عنها بعبارات لسانه لما وسعه يوم واحد لشرحها وبيانها ولو كتبها بقلمه لم تسعه مجلدة واحدة لنظمها وبيانها. وكل ذلك قد يراه في منامه في أقل من لحظة واحدة، ويعلم يقينا أنه رآه حين رآه كأنه شيء واحد ومعنى واحد، لكن التعبير عنه استدعى أوراقا وسمع ما سمع وصحائف طباقا. وكيف لا والمرء يجد من نفسه وجدانا ضروريا أنه إذا سئل عن إشكال في مسألة اعتراه جوابه وحله جملة في أقل من لحظة، ثم يأتي في شرح ذلك بلسانه بعبارات حتى يمتلئ آذان وأسماع كثيرة إن سمعها ووعاها، أو يستثبته بقلمه حتى يسود بياضا كثيرا إن سطرها وزيرها والمعنى في الأصل كان واحدا، والشرح منبسطا والحب يكون واحدا والسنبلة متكثرة ﴿كَمَثَلِ حَيَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ وَاللَّهُ يُضَعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾. ومن وجد بمشام صدقه شمائم الروحانيات ووقع في أطراف رياض المعقولات، وإن كان محومًا عليها غير واغل في خميلتها علم قطعا أن العقل إحدى الإدراك والنفس وحداني القول، وإنما التكثر في عالم الحس يتصور والاختلاف في عالم العبارات يتحقق، وإذا كانت عقولنا ونفوسنا على هذا النمط من التوحيد، فكيف الظن بقدس الإحاطة الأزلية ووحدانية الكلمة السرمدية.
He tells news and speaks traditions; if the one who expresses them through the phrases of his tongue were to express them, one day would not suffice him to explain and clarify them, and if he wrote them with his pen, one volume would not suffice him to arrange and clarify them. And all of that he may see in his sleep in less than one moment, and he knows with certainty that he saw it when he saw it as if it were one thing and one meaning, but its expression required papers and what he heard and stacked pages. How not, and a person finds from himself necessary intuition that if he is asked about a difficulty in a matter, its answer and solution occur to him all at once in less than a moment, then he comes to explain that with his tongue in phrases until many ears and hearings are filled if they hear and comprehend them, or he writes it with his pen until he blackens much whiteness if he writes and records them, while the meaning originally was one and the explanation expansive; the grain is one and the ear multiplies: {Like a grain that grows seven ears, in each ear a hundred grains, and Allah multiplies for whom He wills.} [Al-Baqarah: 261]. And whoever finds with the sense of his truth the fragrances of spiritual things and falls within the boundaries of the meadows of rational things, even if he hovers over them without entering their thicket, knows decisively that the intellect is one of perception and the soul is unique in speech, and multiplicity is only conceivable in the sensible world and difference is realized in the world of expressions. Since our intellects and souls are in this pattern of unity, how then is the thought concerning the sanctity of eternal encompassing and the unity of the eternal Word?
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an analogy and proof that a single meaning can be expressed in multiple ways, applied to divine speech. The author begins by describing how a person can relate many news and traditions; if he tried to express them all verbally, one day would not be enough, and if written, one volume would not contain them. Yet, all of this content can be perceived in a dream in less than a moment as one unified thing and meaning. The expression of that meaning, however, requires many pages. Similarly, when a person is asked a difficult question, the whole answer comes to mind instantly, but its verbal or written explanation requires much time and space. The original meaning was one, but the explanation expands. The author cites the Quranic verse of the grain that multiplies into seven ears with many grains as a metaphor. The second paragraph extends the analogy: one who truly perceives spiritual realities and rational knowledge knows that the intellect is a single faculty of perception and the soul is a single source of speech; multiplicity only occurs in the sensory world and in expressions. Since our own limited intellects and souls exhibit this unity amidst multiplicity, the divine eternal encompassing and the unity of the eternal Word are far more profound.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one in meaning, even though it is expressed in many revealed texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what area of your life have you been inconsistent, speaking differently from what you truly believe? The Mu'tazila Argument for the Created Qur'an
The Mu'tazila present their case that the Qur'an is created based on consensus and sensible attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's rational and textual evidence for their claim that the Qur'an is created.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that the Qur'an's physical composition proves it is created.

The Mu'tazila were known for their rational theology. In this passage, they present their case for the createdness of the Qur'an by appealing to consensus, the tangible nature of the Qur'anic text, and the impossibility of eternal speech having parts. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a sense of the Mu'tazila's flow of thought.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the main premises
Find the key claims: consensus that the Qur'an is speech, its composition of letters and words, its existence as a recited miracle, and its physical presence among us.
3
Examine the etymological argument
The Mu'tazila derive 'Qur'an' from 'gathering', implying it is composed of separate parts. Consider why this is a problem if the Qur'an is eternal.
Eternal speech cannot have parts.
4
Consider the sensory evidence
They claim the Qur'an is heard, touched, and seen, quoting Quranic verses. Think about how these sensory attributes indicate creation.
Created things are sensible.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a rhetorical question: how can the eternal attribute be described with created attributes? Consider the apparent contradiction.

قَالَتِ الْمُعْتَزِلَةُ: أَجْمَعَ الْمُسْلِمُونَ قَبْلَ ظُهُورِ هَذَا الْخِلَافِ عَلَى أَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ كَلَامٌ، وَاتَّفَقُوا عَلَى أَنَّهُ سُوَرٌ وَآيَاتٌ وَحُرُوفٌ مُنْتَظِمَةٌ وَكَلِمَاتٌ مَجْمُوعَةٌ، وَهِيَ مَقْرُوءَةٌ مَسْمُوعَةٌ عَلَى التَّحْقِيقِ، وَلَهَا مُفْتَتَحٌ وَمُخْتَتَمٌ، وَأَنَّهُ مُعْجِزَةُ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ دَالَّةٌ عَلَى صِدْقِهِ، وَلَا تَكُونُ الْمُعْجِزَاتُ إِلَّا فِعْلًا خَارِقًا لِلْعَادَةِ، وَكَانَ السَّلَفُ يَقُولُونَ: يَا رَبَّ الْقُرْآنِ الْعَظِيمِ، يَا رَبَّ طه، يَا رَبَّ يس، وَإِنَّمَا الْقُرْآنُ قُرْآنًا لِلْجَمْعِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِمْ: قَرَأَتِ النَّاقَةُ لَبَنَهَا فِي ضَرْعِهَا، وَالْجَمْعُ إِنَّمَا يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي الْمُفْتَرِقِ، وَالْكَلَامُ الْأَزَلِيُّ لَا يُوصَفُ بِمِثْلِ هَذِهِ الْأَوْصَافِ، وَمِمَّا أَجْمَعَتْ عَلَيْهِ الْأُمَّةُ أَنَّ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بَيْنَ أَظْهُرِنَا نَقْرَؤُهُ بِأَلْسِنَتِنَا وَنَمَسُّهُ بِأَيْدِينَا، وَنُبْصِرُهُ بِأَعْيُنِنَا، وَنَسْمَعُهُ بِآذَانِنَا، وَعَلَيْهِ دَلَّتِ النُّصُوصُ: {وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَأَجِرْهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ} [التوبة: 6]، {لَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ} [الواقعة: 79]، فَالصِّفَةُ الْأَزَلِيَّةُ كَيْفَ تُوصَفُ بِمَا وَصَفْنَا؟
The Mu‘tazila said: The Muslims were unanimous before the emergence of this dispute that the Qur’an is speech, and they agreed that it consists of surahs, verses, ordered letters, and collected words, and it is truly recited and heard, and it has an opening and a closing, and that it is the miracle of the Messenger of Allah – Allah bless him and grant him peace – indicating his truthfulness, and miracles are only an act breaking the customary. The predecessors used to say: 'O Lord of the Mighty Qur’an, O Lord of Ṭā Hā, O Lord of Yā Sīn.' The Qur’an is only called 'Qur’an' for the meaning of gathering, from their saying: 'The she-camel gathered her milk in her udder,' and gathering is only actualized in what is separate. The eternal speech cannot be described with such attributes. Among what the community has agreed upon is that the speech of Allah Most High is among us: we recite it with our tongues, touch it with our hands, see it with our eyes, and hear it with our ears. The texts have indicated this: {And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the speech of Allah} [At-Tawba: 6], {None touch it except the purified} [Al-Wāqi‘a: 79]. So how can the eternal attribute be described with what we have described?
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu‘tazila argument for the createdness of the Qur’an. They begin by claiming a pre-existing consensus that the Qur’an is speech, composed of surahs, verses, letters, and words, and that it is recited and heard, has a beginning and end, and serves as the Prophet’s miracle. They assert that miracles must be created acts breaking natural custom. They then note that the Salaf addressed the Qur’an as something created, e.g., 'O Lord of the Mighty Qur’an,' implying the Qur’an is a creature. They etymologically explain that 'Qur’an' derives from 'jama‘' (gathering), and gathering applies only to separate parts; the eternal speech cannot be composed of parts. They cite an alleged consensus that the Qur’an is physically present among us, experienced through the senses, and support this with verses where the Qur’an is heard and touched. The conclusion is a rhetorical question: how can the eternal attribute be described with such created attributes? The Mu‘tazila thus argue that the Qur’an must be created.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny the Qur'an is speech; they affirm it but say it is created.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that the Qur'an is speech, but they argue it is created speech, not coeternal with Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the Mu‘tazila use to argue that the Qur’an is created?
A) The Qur’an is eternal because it is speech of Allah.
B) The Qur’an has sensible attributes like letters and recitation, which cannot be eternal.
C) The Prophet said the Qur’an is created.
D) The Salaf never addressed the Qur’an as created.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the Qur'an's composite nature and sensory qualities prove it is created, not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur’an, do you experience it as merely letters and sounds, or as the living, eternal speech of Allah? What difference does that make in your heart?
?
The Mu‘tazila argued that because the Qur’an is composed of parts and can be sensed, it must be created. Does your own belief about the Qur’an’s nature affect how you approach it? Are you more focused on its physical form or its divine origin?
?
Imagine you were present during the early debate on the Qur’an’s createdness. After hearing the Mu‘tazila’s argument, would your faith be shaken, or would you seek a deeper understanding of Allah’s speech? What would you say to your own soul to remain firm?

Ash'ari Rebuttal of the Mu'tazila on the Qur'an
The Ash'ariyya refute the Mu'tazili position on the createdness of the Qur'an and expose the impossibility of al-Jubba'i's theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazili view that the Qur'an consists solely of created letters and words, and why they consider the alternative position logically impossible.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari argue that if the Qur'an is created anew at each recitation, two letters cannot coexist in one locus.

In this passage, the Ash'ariyya launch a two-pronged attack against the Mu'tazila. First, they accuse them of breaking consensus by reducing the Qur'an to mere human acts of writing and reciting. Then they turn to Abu 'Ali al-Jubba’i’s specific claim that Allah creates speech at each reading, showing it leads to an absurdity: two letters occupying the same point of articulation simultaneously. This sets the stage for the author’s own corrective view.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusation
Read the opening lines where the Ash'ari accuse the Mu'tazila of violating consensus. Note the consequences they draw: if the Qur'an is created and perishing, then what we recite is not Allah's speech.
2
Understand al-Jubba'i's position
Identify Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i's specific theory: Allah creates speech at each reading or writing. The Ash'ari consider this a denial of necessity.
3
Grasp the logical refutation
Focus on the argument: if Allah created speech simultaneously with the reader's letters, then two letters would exist at the same point of articulation at once, which is impossible because letters only exist successively.
This is the core of the Ash'ari refutation.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why the Ash'ari conclude that al-Jubba'i's position is rationally inconceivable and impossible. This prepares for the author's own distinction in the next lesson.

قَالَتِ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةُ: أَنْتُمْ أَوَّلُ مَنْ خَالَفَ هَذَا الْإِجْمَاعَ، فَإِنَّ عِنْدَكُمْ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ تَبَارَكَ وَتَعَالَى حُرُوفٌ وَكَلِمَاتٌ أَحْدَثَهَا فِي مَحَلٍّ، وَكَمَا وُجِدَتْ فَنِيَتْ، وَالَّذِي كَتَبْنَاهُ بِأَيْدِينَا فِعْلُنَا، وَالَّذِي قَرَأْنَاهُ بِأَلْسِنَتِنَا كَسْبُنَا وَكَذَلِكَ نُثَابُ عَلَى فِعْلِ ذَلِكَ وَنُعَاقَبُ عَلَى تَرْكِهِ، فَلَيْسَ الْكَلَامُ الَّذِي بَيْنَ أَظْهُرِنَا كَلَامَ اللَّهِ، وَمَا كَانَ كَلَامًا لِلَّهِ فَلَيْسَ بَيْنَ أَظْهُرِنَا، وَلَا كَانَ دَلِيلًا وَمُعْجِزَةً، وَلَا قَرَأْنَاهُ وَلَا سَمِعْنَاهُ، بَلِ الَّذِي نَقْرَؤُهُ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ، كَمَنْ يَرْوِي شِعْرَ امْرِئِ الْقَيْسِ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ، وَعَنْ حِكَايَةٍ عَنِ الشَّنَعَةِ؟ صَارَ أَبُو عَلِيٍّ الْجُبَّائِيُّ إِلَى أَنَّهُ يُحْدِثُ كَلَامًا لِنَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ قِرَاءَةِ كُلِّ قَارِئٍ، وَعِنْدَ كِتَابَةِ كُلِّ كَاتِبٍ، وَقَدْ جَحَدَ الضَّرُورَةَ وَكَابَرَ الْعَقْلَ، فَإِنَّ الْعَاقِلَ لَا يَشُكُّ أَنَّ الَّذِي يَسْمَعُهُ مِنَ الْقَارِئِ حُرُوفٌ وَكَلِمَاتٌ تَخْرُجُ عَنْ مَخَارِجِهَا عَلَى اخْتِيَارِهِ، وَلَيْسَ يُقَارِنُهَا أَمْثَالُهَا حَتَّى يَكُونَ كُلُّ حَرْفٍ حَرْفَيْنِ، وَكُلُّ كَلِمَةٍ كَلِمَتَيْنِ، وَكُلُّ آيَةٍ آيَتَيْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فَمَا مَحَلُّهَا وَقَدِ اشْتَغَلَتِ الْمَخَارِجُ بِحُرُوفِهَا؟ وَمِنَ الْمُحَالِ اجْتِمَاعُ حَرْفَيْنِ وَكَلِمَتَيْنِ فِي مَحَلٍّ وَاحِدٍ فِي حَالَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَالْحُرُوفُ لَا وُجُودَ لَهَا إِلَّا عَلَى التَّعَاقُبِ، وَاجْتِمَاعُ حَرْفَيْنِ وَكَلِمَتَيْنِ فِي مَحَلٍّ وَاحِدٍ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولٍ وَلَا مَسْمُوعٍ وَلَا مَحْسُوسٍ مِنْ جِهَةِ الْقَارِئِ، فَهُوَ مُحَالٌ.

ثُمَّ نَقُولُ: الْقَوْلُ الْحَقُّ أَنَّا لَا نُنْكِرُ وُجُودَ الْكَلِمَاتِ الَّتِي لَهَا مُفْتَتَحٌ وَمُخْتَتَمٌ، وَهِيَ آيَاتٌ وَأَعْشَارٌ وَسُوَرٌ، وَيُسَمَّى الْكُلُّ قُرْآنًا، وَمَا لَهُ مُبْتَدَأٌ وَمُنْتَهَى لَا يَكُونُ أَزَلِيًّا، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا الْوَجْهِ مُعْجِزَةُ الرَّسُولِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، وَيُسَمَّى مَا يُقْرَأُ بِاللِّسَانِ قُرْآنًا، وَمَا يُكْتَبُ بِالْيَدِ مُصْحَفًا، لَكِنَّ كَلَامَنَا فِي مَدْلُولِ هَذِهِ الْكَلِمَاتِ وَمُقْرِئِ هَذِهِ الْقِرَاءَةِ: أَهِيَ صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ لَا حَادِثَةٌ وَوَاحِدَةٌ لَا كَثِيرَةٌ، أَمْ هِيَ هَذِهِ فَقَطْ وَلَا مَدْلُولَ لَهَا وَلَا أَمَّ مَقْرُوءٍ وَلَا مَكْتُوبٍ؟ وَبِالِاتِّفَاقِ بَيْنَنَا وَبَيْنَ الْخَصْمِ: كَلَامُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى غَيْرُ مَا حَلَّ فِي اللِّسَانِ مِنْ تَحْرِيكِ الشَّفَتَيْنِ وَاللِّسَانِ وَالْحَلَقِ، بَلْ هُوَ مَعْنًى آخَرُ وَرَاءَ ذَلِكَ، فَنَحْنُ...
The Ash‘ariyya said: You are the first to violate this consensus, for according to you the speech of Allah – blessed and exalted – is letters and words that He originated in a locus, and as they came to be they perished. What we write with our hands is our own act, and what we recite with our tongues is our acquisition; similarly we are rewarded for doing that and punished for neglecting it. Thus the speech that is among us is not the speech of Allah, and what is the speech of Allah is not among us, nor was it a proof or a miracle, nor did we recite it or hear it. Rather what we recite is like that, like someone who recites the poetry of Imru’ al-Qays after his death. And concerning a story about the repulsive thing? Abu ‘Ali al-Jubba’i concluded that He creates speech for Himself at the reading of every reader, and at the writing of every writer. He has denied the necessary and opposed reason, for the rational person does not doubt that what he hears from the reader are letters and words that exit from their points of articulation by his choice, and their like do not accompany them such that every letter becomes two letters, every word two words, and every verse two verses. If so, what would be their locus when the points of articulation are occupied by their own letters? It is impossible for two letters and two words to coexist in one locus at one moment. Letters exist only successively, and the coexistence of two letters and two words in one locus is rationally inconceivable, not heard, not sensed from the reader. Thus it is impossible.

Then we say: The true statement is that we do not deny the existence of words that have an opening and a closing, and they are verses, tenths, and surahs, and the whole is called Qur’an. Whatever has a beginning and an end cannot be eternal, and in this aspect it is the miracle of the Messenger – Allah bless him and grant him peace. What is recited by the tongue is called Qur’an, and what is written by hand is called Mushaf. However, our discussion concerns the meaning (madlūl) of these words and the reciter of this recitation: is it an eternal attribute, not originated, one not many, or is it only these, with no meaning behind them and no 'umm' of something recited or written? By agreement between us and the opponent: the speech of Allah Most High is other than what enters the tongue from the movement of the lips, tongue, and throat; rather it is another meaning beyond that. So we...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage contains the Ash‘ari response to the Mu‘tazila and the author’s own doctrinal clarification. It is an objection-and-answer followed by a positive thesis.

The Ash‘ariyya begin by accusing the Mu‘tazila of breaking the consensus they claimed. They point out that in the Mu‘tazila view, Allah’s speech consists of letters and words created in a locus and then perishing. Consequently, what we write or recite is our own act and acquisition, not Allah’s speech. This implies that the Qur’an among us is not Allah’s speech, and Allah’s speech is not among us, nor is it a miracle or something we recite. They compare this to reciting the poetry of a dead poet.

They then refute the position of Abu ‘Ali al-Jubba’i, who held that Allah creates speech anew at each reading or writing. The Ash‘ari argue this is against rational necessity: the sounds heard are single letters and words; if they were created by Allah, there would be two simultaneous letters in the same locus, which is impossible. Letters only exist in succession, so coexistence is rationally and sensibly impossible.

Then the author (likely Ash‘ari himself) states the correct view: he does not deny the existence of the recited words that have beginnings and ends – these are indeed the Qur’an as miracle. However, the real issue is whether there is a meaning (madlūl) behind these words that is an eternal attribute. By mutual agreement between Ash‘aris and Mu‘tazila, Allah’s speech is something other than the physical movements of the speech organs; it is a meaning beyond that. The passage ends with 'fa-naḥnu...' (so we...), likely leading to the conclusion that this meaning is the eternal speech of Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what is the main logical problem with al-Jubba’i’s position?
A) It denies that the Qur'an is a miracle.
B) It implies that two letters or words cannot coexist at the same instant from the same reader.
C) It claims the Qur'an is eternal.
D) It relies on consensus that does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argue that if the Qur'an were created at each recitation, it would entail the impossible coexistence of two letters at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel the weight of it being the very speech of Allah, or do you treat it as a human recitation of an ancient text? What would change in your heart if you truly believed it was impossible for two letters to coexist—meaning every letter you utter is solely from Allah's eternal speech?
?
The Ash'ari argue that rational necessity demands we accept the impossibility of two letters in one locus. Have you ever examined your own beliefs about the Qur'an to see if they hold up to rational scrutiny? What would it cost you to discover that your current belief rests on a logical fallacy?
?
If you encountered a Muslim who denied the Qur'an is the uncreated speech of Allah, could you clearly articulate why their position leads to absurdity? Would your argument be born from conviction or from rote memorization? Commit to learning one rational proof for the Qur'an's divine origin so that your faith is grounded, not borrowed.

Divine Attributes

The Author's True Position: Recited Qur'an and Eternal Speech
The author clarifies that the recited words are created, but the meaning they convey is the eternal attribute of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's doctrinal clarification that the recited words of the Qur'an are created and serve as a miracle, while the speech of Allah is an eternal attribute underlying them.

Core Idea
The recited Qur'an is created, but it points to an eternal, uncreated attribute of Allah.

After refuting the Mu'tazila, the author (apparently Ash'ari) now states his own position. He does not deny that the words we recite are indeed the Qur'an and a miracle, but he insists that behind these created words lies an eternal meaning – the divine attribute of speech. Both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agree that Allah's speech is distinct from the physical acts of reading, but they differ on whether that speech is eternal or originated. The author here affirms its eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening affirmation
Read the first part where the author affirms that words with beginnings and ends (verses, surahs) exist and are called Qur'an, and as created things they are the miracle.
2
Identify the real issue
The author says the real question is about the meaning (madlūl) behind these words: is it an eternal attribute or just these words with no deeper reference?
3
Note the agreed premise
Both parties agree that Allah's speech is other than the physical movements of lips and tongue; it is a meaning beyond that. This is a common ground.
4
Consider the author's implied conclusion
While the passage trails off, the context makes clear that the author affirms the eternal attribute as the true speech of Allah, which is one and uncreated.

قَالَتِ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةُ: أَنْتُمْ أَوَّلُ مَنْ خَالَفَ هَذَا الْإِجْمَاعَ، فَإِنَّ عِنْدَكُمْ كَلَامَ اللَّهِ تَبَارَكَ وَتَعَالَى حُرُوفٌ وَكَلِمَاتٌ أَحْدَثَهَا فِي مَحَلٍّ، وَكَمَا وُجِدَتْ فَنِيَتْ، وَالَّذِي كَتَبْنَاهُ بِأَيْدِينَا فِعْلُنَا، وَالَّذِي قَرَأْنَاهُ بِأَلْسِنَتِنَا كَسْبُنَا وَكَذَلِكَ نُثَابُ عَلَى فِعْلِ ذَلِكَ وَنُعَاقَبُ عَلَى تَرْكِهِ، فَلَيْسَ الْكَلَامُ الَّذِي بَيْنَ أَظْهُرِنَا كَلَامَ اللَّهِ، وَمَا كَانَ كَلَامًا لِلَّهِ فَلَيْسَ بَيْنَ أَظْهُرِنَا، وَلَا كَانَ دَلِيلًا وَمُعْجِزَةً، وَلَا قَرَأْنَاهُ وَلَا سَمِعْنَاهُ، بَلِ الَّذِي نَقْرَؤُهُ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ، كَمَنْ يَرْوِي شِعْرَ امْرِئِ الْقَيْسِ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ، وَعَنْ حِكَايَةٍ عَنِ الشَّنَعَةِ؟ صَارَ أَبُو عَلِيٍّ الْجُبَّائِيُّ إِلَى أَنَّهُ يُحْدِثُ كَلَامًا لِنَفْسِهِ عِنْدَ قِرَاءَةِ كُلِّ قَارِئٍ، وَعِنْدَ كِتَابَةِ كُلِّ كَاتِبٍ، وَقَدْ جَحَدَ الضَّرُورَةَ وَكَابَرَ الْعَقْلَ، فَإِنَّ الْعَاقِلَ لَا يَشُكُّ أَنَّ الَّذِي يَسْمَعُهُ مِنَ الْقَارِئِ حُرُوفٌ وَكَلِمَاتٌ تَخْرُجُ عَنْ مَخَارِجِهَا عَلَى اخْتِيَارِهِ، وَلَيْسَ يُقَارِنُهَا أَمْثَالُهَا حَتَّى يَكُونَ كُلُّ حَرْفٍ حَرْفَيْنِ، وَكُلُّ كَلِمَةٍ كَلِمَتَيْنِ، وَكُلُّ آيَةٍ آيَتَيْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فَمَا مَحَلُّهَا وَقَدِ اشْتَغَلَتِ الْمَخَارِجُ بِحُرُوفِهَا؟ وَمِنَ الْمُحَالِ اجْتِمَاعُ حَرْفَيْنِ وَكَلِمَتَيْنِ فِي مَحَلٍّ وَاحِدٍ فِي حَالَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَالْحُرُوفُ لَا وُجُودَ لَهَا إِلَّا عَلَى التَّعَاقُبِ، وَاجْتِمَاعُ حَرْفَيْنِ وَكَلِمَتَيْنِ فِي مَحَلٍّ وَاحِدٍ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولٍ وَلَا مَسْمُوعٍ وَلَا مَحْسُوسٍ مِنْ جِهَةِ الْقَارِئِ، فَهُوَ مُحَالٌ.

ثُمَّ نَقُولُ: الْقَوْلُ الْحَقُّ أَنَّا لَا نُنْكِرُ وُجُودَ الْكَلِمَاتِ الَّتِي لَهَا مُفْتَتَحٌ وَمُخْتَتَمٌ، وَهِيَ آيَاتٌ وَأَعْشَارٌ وَسُوَرٌ، وَيُسَمَّى الْكُلُّ قُرْآنًا، وَمَا لَهُ مُبْتَدَأٌ وَمُنْتَهَى لَا يَكُونُ أَزَلِيًّا، وَهُوَ بِهَذَا الْوَجْهِ مُعْجِزَةُ الرَّسُولِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، وَيُسَمَّى مَا يُقْرَأُ بِاللِّسَانِ قُرْآنًا، وَمَا يُكْتَبُ بِالْيَدِ مُصْحَفًا، لَكِنَّ كَلَامَنَا فِي مَدْلُولِ هَذِهِ الْكَلِمَاتِ وَمُقْرِئِ هَذِهِ الْقِرَاءَةِ: أَهِيَ صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ لَا حَادِثَةٌ وَوَاحِدَةٌ لَا كَثِيرَةٌ، أَمْ هِيَ هَذِهِ فَقَطْ وَلَا مَدْلُولَ لَهَا وَلَا أَمَّ مَقْرُوءٍ وَلَا مَكْتُوبٍ؟ وَبِالِاتِّفَاقِ بَيْنَنَا وَبَيْنَ الْخَصْمِ: كَلَامُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى غَيْرُ مَا حَلَّ فِي اللِّسَانِ مِنْ تَحْرِيكِ الشَّفَتَيْنِ وَاللِّسَانِ وَالْحَلَقِ، بَلْ هُوَ مَعْنًى آخَرُ وَرَاءَ ذَلِكَ، فَنَحْنُ...
The Ash‘ariyya said: You are the first to violate this consensus, for according to you the speech of Allah – blessed and exalted – is letters and words that He originated in a locus, and as they came to be they perished. What we write with our hands is our own act, and what we recite with our tongues is our acquisition; similarly we are rewarded for doing that and punished for neglecting it. Thus the speech that is among us is not the speech of Allah, and what is the speech of Allah is not among us, nor was it a proof or a miracle, nor did we recite it or hear it. Rather what we recite is like that, like someone who recites the poetry of Imru’ al-Qays after his death. And concerning a story about the repulsive thing? Abu ‘Ali al-Jubba’i concluded that He creates speech for Himself at the reading of every reader, and at the writing of every writer. He has denied the necessary and opposed reason, for the rational person does not doubt that what he hears from the reader are letters and words that exit from their points of articulation by his choice, and their like do not accompany them such that every letter becomes two letters, every word two words, and every verse two verses. If so, what would be their locus when the points of articulation are occupied by their own letters? It is impossible for two letters and two words to coexist in one locus at one moment. Letters exist only successively, and the coexistence of two letters and two words in one locus is rationally inconceivable, not heard, not sensed from the reader. Thus it is impossible.

Then we say: The true statement is that we do not deny the existence of words that have an opening and a closing, and they are verses, tenths, and surahs, and the whole is called Qur’an. Whatever has a beginning and an end cannot be eternal, and in this aspect it is the miracle of the Messenger – Allah bless him and grant him peace. What is recited by the tongue is called Qur’an, and what is written by hand is called Mushaf. However, our discussion concerns the meaning (madlūl) of these words and the reciter of this recitation: is it an eternal attribute, not originated, one not many, or is it only these, with no meaning behind them and no 'umm' of something recited or written? By agreement between us and the opponent: the speech of Allah Most High is other than what enters the tongue from the movement of the lips, tongue, and throat; rather it is another meaning beyond that. So we...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage contains the Ash‘ari response to the Mu‘tazila and the author’s own doctrinal clarification. It is an objection-and-answer followed by a positive thesis.

The Ash‘ariyya begin by accusing the Mu‘tazila of breaking the consensus they claimed. They point out that in the Mu‘tazila view, Allah’s speech consists of letters and words created in a locus and then perishing. Consequently, what we write or recite is our own act and acquisition, not Allah’s speech. This implies that the Qur’an among us is not Allah’s speech, and Allah’s speech is not among us, nor is it a miracle or something we recite. They compare this to reciting the poetry of a dead poet.

They then refute the position of Abu ‘Ali al-Jubba’i, who held that Allah creates speech anew at each reading or writing. The Ash‘ari argue this is against rational necessity: the sounds heard are single letters and words; if they were created by Allah, there would be two simultaneous letters in the same locus, which is impossible. Letters only exist in succession, so coexistence is rationally and sensibly impossible.

Then the author (likely Ash‘ari himself) states the correct view: he does not deny the existence of the recited words that have beginnings and ends – these are indeed the Qur’an as miracle. However, the real issue is whether there is a meaning (madlūl) behind these words that is an eternal attribute. By mutual agreement between Ash‘aris and Mu‘tazila, Allah’s speech is something other than the physical movements of the speech organs; it is a meaning beyond that. The passage ends with 'fa-naḥnu...' (so we...), likely leading to the conclusion that this meaning is the eternal speech of Allah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'aris deny the createdness of the Qur'an entirely, claiming the entire Qur'an is uncreated.
Correction: The Ash'aris distinguish between the created recited words (which they affirm as a miracle) and the eternal attribute of speech (the madlūl) that is uncreated.

B) The Qur'an is entirely uncreated.
C) Allah's speech is other than the physical movements of the tongue and lips.
D) The recited words are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The author affirms the createdness of the recited Qur'an as a miracle, but insists that the divine speech it signifies is an eternal, singular attribute.

Refuting Created Speech at Every Recitation
Refutes al-Jubbai's claim that recitation creates new speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that each recitation creates new speech is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Two letters cannot occupy the same moment — this simple fact refutes al-Jubbai.

Abu Ali al-Jubbai proposed that every reader creates a new instance of speech when reciting. This seems plausible until we examine what actually happens. The author argues that such a view contradicts both inherent knowledge and reason. Let’s see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the key terms: كلام, قراءة, مخارج, اجتماع, محال.
The passage is a refutation, so it presents a claim and then dismantles it.
2
Identify al-Jubbai's claim
Al-Jubbai held that the reader creates speech for himself at each recitation or writing. Underline this claim in the text.
This is the view being refuted.
3
Understand the argument about letters and locus
The author argues that letters emerge sequentially from points of articulation. If al-Jubbai were correct, two letters would have to exist simultaneously in the same place, which is impossible.
Focus on the phrase 'اجتماع حرفين وكلمتين في محل واحد'.
4
Grasp why simultaneous letters are impossible
Letters only exist in succession (على التعاقب). Their coexistence in one locus is inconceivable, unheard, and unperceived. This logical impossibility disproves al-Jubbai's view.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صار أبو علي الجبائي إلى أنه يحدث كلاما لنفسه عند قراءة كل قارئ، وعند كتابة كل كاتب وقد جحد الضرورة وكابر العقل، فإن العاقل لا يشك أن الذي يسمعه من القارئ حروف وكلمات تخرج عن مخارجها على اختياره وليس يقارنه أمثالها حتى يكون كل حرف حرفين، وكل كلمة كلمتين، وكل آية آيتين وإن كان فما محلها وقد اشتغلت المخارج بحروفها؟ ومن المحال اجتماع حرفين وكلمتين في محل واحد في حالة واحدة، والحروف لا وجود لها إلا على التعاقب و اجتماع حرفين وكلمتين في محل واحد غير معقول ولا مسموع ولا محسوس من جهة القارئ، فهو محال.
Abu Ali al-Jubbai held that the reader creates speech for himself at every recitation and at every writing, and he denied the necessary and opposed reason, for the rational person does not doubt that what he hears from the reader are letters and words that emerge from their points of articulation by his choice, and their like do not accompany him such that every letter becomes two letters, every word two words, and every verse two verses. But if so, what is their locus, when the points of articulation are occupied with their letters? It is impossible for two letters and two words to come together in one locus in a single state, and letters exist only in succession. The gathering of two letters and two words in one locus is inconceivable, unheard, and unperceived from the reader, so it is impossible.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Ali al-Jubbai's position that the reader generates a new instance of speech at each recitation or writing. The author argues that such a claim contradicts both necessity (al-ḍarūra) and reason. He explains that the letters and words we hear are produced by the reader's choice from their specific points of articulation. If, as al-Jubbai implies, each recitation created a wholly new instance, then each single letter would have to be two letters simultaneously—an absurdity. The author emphasizes that letters cannot coexist in the same temporal moment; they occur sequentially (ʿalā al-taʿāqub). The idea of two letters or words occupying the same locus at once is rationally, audibly, and perceptually impossible from the reader's perspective. Thus, al-Jubbai's view is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the reader creates the speech itself, so each recitation is a new creation.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to a logical impossibility: two letters cannot occupy the same locus at the same time, and letters exist only in succession.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is al-Jubbai's view that the reader creates speech at each recitation impossible?
A) Because the reader cannot create speech.
B) Because letters cannot occupy the same locus at the same time.
C) Because the Quran is uncreated.
D) Because the reader is not a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubbai's view that each recitation creates new speech is impossible because letters cannot coexist simultaneously in the same locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever stop to think that the letters you utter are not new creations but manifestations of Allah's eternal speech? This lesson proves that your recitation cannot be a separate speech. How does this truth change the way you approach your recitation? Commit to reciting with the awareness that you are not producing new words but channeling the infinite speech of Allah.
?
Have you ever doubted the Quran's eternity, thinking that each time you recite you create it anew? The author shows this is logically impossible. Search your heart: do you truly believe the Quran is the eternal word of Allah, or do you treat your recitation as your own construction? What will you do to align your belief with this reality?
?
If two letters cannot coexist, then every single letter of your recitation is a direct link to the original divine speech. Does your humility in recitation reflect this? Or do you recite carelessly, as if the words originate from you? Pledge to recite with the certainty that each letter is from Allah, not from you.

The Quran's Words Are Temporal, Its Meaning Eternal
States the correct doctrine: the Quran's words are temporal, its meaning eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the recited Quran is temporal and that the eternal attribute of speech is distinct from its physical expressions.

Core Idea
The Quran's words are created; its meaning is eternal.

In this passage, al-Shahrastani clarifies the correct Sunni position on the Quran. While affirming that the recited words are indeed called Quran and are a miracle, he insists that the real speech of Allah is an eternal attribute beyond the temporal words. This lesson will help you grasp this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two aspects of Quran
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Shahrastani distinguishes between the physical recited words and the eternal meaning they point to.
The words have a beginning and an end, so they cannot be eternal.
2
Understand the agreed-upon point
Both Sunnis and opponents agree that God's speech is not the physical movements of the tongue. The real speech is a meaning beyond that.
This agreement is crucial for the argument.
3
See why the argument is cut off
Al-Shahrastani says the proof is cut off by what the people of consensus know, because the opponent does not accept the same premises.
This highlights the role of shared foundational beliefs in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : القول الحق أنا لا ننكر وجود الكلمات التي لها مفتتح ومختتم، وهي آيات وأعشار وسور، ويسمى الكل قرآنًا، وما له مبتدأ ومنتهى لا يكون أزليا وهو هذا الوجه معجزة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ويسمى ما يقرأ باللسان قرآنا ، وما يكتب باليد مصحفًا، لكن كلامنا في مدلول هذه الكلمات ومقرئ هذه القراءة أهي صفة أزلية لا حادثة وواحدة لا كثيرة أم هي هذه فقط ولا مدلول لها ولا أم مقروء ولا مكتوب ؟ وبالاتفاق بيننا وبين الخصم كلام الله تعالى غير ما حل في اللسان من تحريك الشفتين واللسان والحلق بل هو معنى آخر وراء ذلك، فنحن نعتقد أن ذلك المعنى واحد أزلي، وأنتم تعتقدون أنه مثل هذا كثير حادث فانقطع الاستدلال بما يعرفه أهل الإجماع، وكما أن كلامه أزلي واحد عندنا وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا كذلك هو كلام آخر في محل آخر، وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا عند الخصم، فصار الاتفاق بالمقدمات المشهورة المعهودة لا اليقينية المقبولة المشهودة، وتبين أن إطلاق لفظ القرآن على القراءة والمقروء باشتراك اللفظ، وقد يسمى الدليل باسم المدلول، ويطلق لفظ العلم على المعلوم ؛ كقوله تبارك وتعالى : ( وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِّنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ ) أي : بمعلومه وقال : إن قرآن الفجر كان مشهودا به الصلاة والقراءة فيها.
Then we say: The true statement is that we do not deny the existence of words that have a beginning and an end, which are verses, tenths, and chapters, and all is called Qur'an. What has a beginning and an end cannot be eternal, and this aspect is the miracle of the Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace. What is recited by the tongue is called Qur'an, and what is written by the hand is called mushaf. But our inquiry concerns the reference (madlūl) of these words and the meaning of this recitation (muqri' hādhihi al-qirā'a): Is it an eternal, uncreated attribute, one not many, or is it only these words with no reference, neither recited nor written? By agreement between us and the opponent, the speech of God Almighty is other than what occurs on the tongue—the movement of the lips, tongue, and throat—rather it is another meaning beyond that. So we believe that that meaning is one and eternal, while you (the opponent) believe that it is like this: many and temporal. So the proof is cut off (fā-nqaṭa'a al-istidlāl) by what the people of consensus know. And just as His speech is eternal and one for us, and that is not present among us, likewise it is another speech in another locus, and that is not among us for the opponent. So agreement (al-ittifāq) became based on well-known customary premises (al-muqaddimāt al-mashhūra al-ma'hūda), not certain accepted witnessed ones. And it became clear that the application of the word 'Qur'an' to the recitation (al-qirā'a) and the recited (al-maqrū') is by homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ). Sometimes the name of the proof is applied to the proven (al-dalīl bi-sm al-madlūl), and the word 'knowledge' (‘ilm) is applied to the known (al-ma‘lūm), as His saying, exalted: 'And they encompass not anything of His knowledge except what He wills' (2:255), i.e., of His known thing. And He said: 'Indeed, the Qur'an of dawn is witnessed' (17:78), meaning the prayer and the recitation in it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a positive statement of the correct doctrine regarding the nature of the Qur'an. The author begins by affirming that the verbal words (al-kalimāt) with beginnings and ends—verses, tenths, and chapters—are indeed called Qur'an and constitute the miracle of the Prophet. However, the key question concerns the referent (madlūl) of these words: Is the referent an eternal attribute (one and uncreated) or merely the recited words themselves with no referent? Both parties (Sunni and opponent) agree that God's speech is not the physical movements of the tongue and lips but a meaning beyond that (ma‘nā ākhar warā’ dhālika). The Sunnis hold that meaning to be one and eternal; the opponents hold it to be multiple and temporal. The author then points out that the argument (al-istidlāl) is thus cut off by what the people of consensus (ahl al-ijmā‘) recognize—namely, the opponent does not accept the same premises. Consequently, agreement is based on customary premises, not certain ones. The author clarifies that applying the term 'Qur'an' to both the recitation (al-qirā'a) and the recited (al-maqrū‘) is a case of homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ), like calling the proof by the name of the proven. This is exemplified by the Quranic verse 'nor shall they encompass aught of His knowledge' where 'ilm means the known (ma‘lūm). Another example: 'the Qur'an of dawn is witnessed' refers to the prayer and recitation. Thus, the eternal attribute of speech is distinct from its temporal expressions.

Key Terms
كلمات
قرآن
مصحف
مدلول
صفة أزلية
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believe that the recited Quran (the words and letters) is itself the eternal attribute of Allah.
Correction: The recited words are temporal and created; the eternal attribute of speech is a meaning beyond those words, which they indicate.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, what do both Sunnis and opponents agree upon regarding God's speech?
A) It is the physical sounds produced by the tongue.
B) It is a meaning beyond the physical movements of the lips and tongue.
C) It is both the physical words and the eternal meaning simultaneously.
D) It is only the written text in the mushaf.

Key Takeaway
The recited Quran is temporal and created; the eternal attribute of speech is a single, uncreated meaning distinct from its physical expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says the argument is cut off because the opponent rejects the consensus of the community. Are there areas in your life where you reject the clear consensus of mainstream Islamic scholarship? What is the cost of such stubbornness on your faith?
?
If the eternal meaning of the Quran is beyond your limited grasp, are you humble enough to seek knowledge from those who have deeper understanding? What steps will you take this week to learn the Quran's meanings from reliable sources?

Homonymy of 'Quran' and the Basis of Theological Agreement
Explains homonymy of 'Quran' and the basis of theological agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'Quran' is used homonymously for both the eternal attribute and the recited words, and that theological agreement often relies on customary premises.

Core Idea
'Quran' refers to two different realities by homonymy.

Al-Shahrastani concludes his argument by explaining how the word 'Quran' can apply to both the recitation and the recited without implying that the recited words are eternal. He also notes that the agreement between believers and opponents is based on well-known premises rather than certain ones. This lesson unpacks these final points.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Focus on the comparison between the eternal speech and the recited words.
2
Understand homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ)
The author says the word 'Quran' is applied to both the recitation (qirā'a) and the recited (maqrū') by homonymy. This means the same word is used for two different meanings.
This is like using 'light' for both sunlight and a lamp.
3
Examine the examples given
Al-Shahrastani gives examples: calling the proof by the name of the proven, and using 'ilm (knowledge) for ma'lum (known). These illustrate the principle of homonymy.
The verse 'nor shall they encompass aught of His knowledge' means His known things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : القول الحق أنا لا ننكر وجود الكلمات التي لها مفتتح ومختتم، وهي آيات وأعشار وسور، ويسمى الكل قرآنًا، وما له مبتدأ ومنتهى لا يكون أزليا وهو هذا الوجه معجزة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ويسمى ما يقرأ باللسان قرآنا ، وما يكتب باليد مصحفًا، لكن كلامنا في مدلول هذه الكلمات ومقرئ هذه القراءة أهي صفة أزلية لا حادثة وواحدة لا كثيرة أم هي هذه فقط ولا مدلول لها ولا أم مقروء ولا مكتوب ؟ وبالاتفاق بيننا وبين الخصم كلام الله تعالى غير ما حل في اللسان من تحريك الشفتين واللسان والحلق بل هو معنى آخر وراء ذلك، فنحن نعتقد أن ذلك المعنى واحد أزلي، وأنتم تعتقدون أنه مثل هذا كثير حادث فانقطع الاستدلال بما يعرفه أهل الإجماع، وكما أن كلامه أزلي واحد عندنا وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا كذلك هو كلام آخر في محل آخر، وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا عند الخصم، فصار الاتفاق بالمقدمات المشهورة المعهودة لا اليقينية المقبولة المشهودة، وتبين أن إطلاق لفظ القرآن على القراءة والمقروء باشتراك اللفظ، وقد يسمى الدليل باسم المدلول، ويطلق لفظ العلم على المعلوم ؛ كقوله تبارك وتعالى : ( وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِّنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ ) أي : بمعلومه وقال : إن قرآن الفجر كان مشهودا به الصلاة والقراءة فيها.
Then we say: The true statement is that we do not deny the existence of words that have a beginning and an end, which are verses, tenths, and chapters, and all is called Qur'an. What has a beginning and an end cannot be eternal, and this aspect is the miracle of the Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace. What is recited by the tongue is called Qur'an, and what is written by the hand is called mushaf. But our inquiry concerns the reference (madlūl) of these words and the meaning of this recitation (muqri' hādhihi al-qirā'a): Is it an eternal, uncreated attribute, one not many, or is it only these words with no reference, neither recited nor written? By agreement between us and the opponent, the speech of God Almighty is other than what occurs on the tongue—the movement of the lips, tongue, and throat—rather it is another meaning beyond that. So we believe that that meaning is one and eternal, while you (the opponent) believe that it is like this: many and temporal. So the proof is cut off (fā-nqaṭa'a al-istidlāl) by what the people of consensus know. And just as His speech is eternal and one for us, and that is not present among us, likewise it is another speech in another locus, and that is not among us for the opponent. So agreement (al-ittifāq) became based on well-known customary premises (al-muqaddimāt al-mashhūra al-ma'hūda), not certain accepted witnessed ones. And it became clear that the application of the word 'Qur'an' to the recitation (al-qirā'a) and the recited (al-maqrū') is by homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ). Sometimes the name of the proof is applied to the proven (al-dalīl bi-sm al-madlūl), and the word 'knowledge' (‘ilm) is applied to the known (al-ma‘lūm), as His saying, exalted: 'And they encompass not anything of His knowledge except what He wills' (2:255), i.e., of His known thing. And He said: 'Indeed, the Qur'an of dawn is witnessed' (17:78), meaning the prayer and the recitation in it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a positive statement of the correct doctrine regarding the nature of the Qur'an. The author begins by affirming that the verbal words (al-kalimāt) with beginnings and ends—verses, tenths, and chapters—are indeed called Qur'an and constitute the miracle of the Prophet. However, the key question concerns the referent (madlūl) of these words: Is the referent an eternal attribute (one and uncreated) or merely the recited words themselves with no referent? Both parties (Sunni and opponent) agree that God's speech is not the physical movements of the tongue and lips but a meaning beyond that (ma‘nā ākhar warā’ dhālika). The Sunnis hold that meaning to be one and eternal; the opponents hold it to be multiple and temporal. The author then points out that the argument (al-istidlāl) is thus cut off by what the people of consensus (ahl al-ijmā‘) recognize—namely, the opponent does not accept the same premises. Consequently, agreement is based on customary premises, not certain ones. The author clarifies that applying the term 'Qur'an' to both the recitation (al-qirā'a) and the recited (al-maqrū‘) is a case of homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ), like calling the proof by the name of the proven. This is exemplified by the Quranic verse 'nor shall they encompass aught of His knowledge' where 'ilm means the known (ma‘lūm). Another example: 'the Qur'an of dawn is witnessed' refers to the prayer and recitation. Thus, the eternal attribute of speech is distinct from its temporal expressions.

Key Terms
اشتراك اللفظ
مدلول
قرآن
قراءة
مقروء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believe that the term 'Quran' is used univocally (with a single meaning) for both the eternal attribute of speech and the recited words.
Correction: The term is used homonymously (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) — it refers to two distinct realities: the eternal attribute and the temporal recitation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, how is the word 'Quran' applied to both the recitation (qirā'a) and the recited (maqrū')?
A) By univocal usage.
B) By homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ).
C) By metaphor.
D) By literal extension.

Key Takeaway
The word 'Quran' is applied to both the recitation and the eternal meaning by homonymy, not univocally, and theological agreement often rests on customary premises rather than certain ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider whether you are engaging with the eternal speech of Allah or just reciting words? How would that awareness change the way you recite?
?
The author says theological agreement often rests on customary premises rather than certain ones. In your own understanding of faith, have you accepted a belief simply because it is commonly held? Will you examine that belief now in light of this lesson?
?
If the word 'Quran' can refer to both the eternal attribute and the temporal recitation, which of these two are you truly seeking when you read the Quran? How can you ensure your heart connects with the eternal meaning beyond the act of reading?

How the Verses Brought by Jibril Are God's Speech
The author argues that the verses are God's eternal speech manifested through Jibril's recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran can be both God's eternal speech and the verses brought by Jibril through the distinction between meaning and manifestation.

Core Idea
God's speech is the eternal meaning; Jibril's manifestation is its temporal expression.

A common challenge in kalam is reconciling the eternal nature of God's speech with the fact that the Quran was revealed through Jibril over time. This passage from al-Shahrastani provides a sophisticated answer: the verses are God's speech because Jibril is a manifestation (mazhar) of the divine meaning. Before diving into the text, consider how you would explain that a person's speech can be both their own and expressed through an intermediary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the translation. Notice the key terms: تمثل (tamaththala), مظهر (mazhar), عبارة (expression), معنى (meaning).
The text uses a known logical distinction between 'ibara and ma'na.
2
Analyze Jibril's manifestation analogy
The author says Jibril manifested himself in a human form (tamaththala bihi). The person then points and says 'This is Jibril' and 'This is God's speech.' Understand that both pointings refer to the same entity through its manifestation.
The manifestation is a means, not the object itself.
3
Grasp the distinction between expression and meaning
The author uses the everyday example: when you say 'Your speech is correct,' you refer to the meaning, not the mere words. Similarly, when we say 'These verses are God's speech,' we refer to the eternal meaning manifested in the recited words.
Correctness (sihha) pertains to meaning, not wording.
4
Apply to the theological issue
Conclude that the verses are truly God's speech because they express His eternal attribute of speech through a temporal manifestation. Jibril's role does not create the speech but conveys its manifestation.
This preserves both the uncreatedness of the Quran and the historical revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب الحق أن الآيات التي جاء بها جبريل عليه السلام منزلا على الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم كلام الله ، كما أن الشخص الذي تمثل به جبريل وقراءي وظهر له جبريل حتى يقول هذا كلام الله وهذا جبريل ؛ لأن ما أشير سمي إليه بهذا وهذا هو مظهره، وأنت تقول : كلامك هذا صحيح وغير صحيح، ولا تشير إلى مجرد العبارة دون المعنى ليل تشير إلى العبارة على أنها مظهر المعنى : وإلا فالصحة والغساد إنما يدخلان على المعنى دون اللفظ والصواب
The true answer is that the verses which Jibril, peace be upon him, brought down as a revelation to the Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace, are the speech of God, just as the person in whose form Jibril manifested himself (tamaththala bihi Jibrīl) and appeared to him (waẓahara lahu Jibrīl) such that he says 'This is God's speech' and 'This is Jibril'—because what is pointed to is named by 'this' and 'this' is his manifestation (mazharuhu). And you say: 'Your speech is correct or incorrect,' and you do not refer to the mere expression without the meaning, rather you refer to the expression as being the manifestation of the meaning. Otherwise, correctness and corruption pertain only to the meaning, not to the wording (al-lafẓ) and the correct (al-ṣawāb).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to how the verses of the Quran can be called God's speech when they were brought by Jibril (Gabriel). The author explains that Jibril manifested himself in human form (tamaththala) and recited the verses. The verses are truly God's speech, just as Jibril is truly Jibril, even though he appears in a human form. The pointing (al-ishāra) to the revelation as 'this is God's speech' refers to the meaning expressed through the form, not the mere physical sound. The author draws an analogy: when one says 'your speech is correct,' they do not refer to the mere vocal expression (al-ʿibāra) without the meaning; they refer to the expression as a manifestation (mazhar) of the meaning. If that were not the case, then correctness and corruption would apply to the wording itself, but in reality they pertain to the meaning. Thus, the verses are God's eternal speech manifested through temporal expressions.

جبريل

مظهر
عبارة

صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because Jibril brought the verses, they must be created or not entirely God's speech.
Correction: Jibril's manifestation does not create the speech; it conveys the manifestation of God's eternal speech.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the phrase 'your speech is correct' refer to?
A) The mere words and phonetic sounds.
B) The meaning expressed through the words.
C) The speaker's intention alone.
D) The physical act of speaking.

Key Takeaway
The verses of the Quran are God's speech because they manifest the eternal meaning, just as Jibril's human form manifests his true identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you treat it merely as words on a page, or do you experience it as the eternal speech of God manifesting to you in this moment? What would change if you truly believed that the meaning is from God, even while the words are recited by your tongue?
?
The author argues that when you say 'your speech is correct,' you refer to meaning, not wording. Reflect: In your daily conversations, do you focus more on the form of what people say or the essence? How does this habit affect your relationship with Allah's speech?
?
Jibril's manifestation in human form allowed the Prophet to point and say 'This is Jibril.' Similarly, the Quran's words point to God's speech. Are you content to stop at the words, or do you let them point you to the eternal meaning? What would need to change in your recitation for you to truly listen to the meaning?

The Eternal One Meaning and the Consensus Argument
Author states the core disagreement and analyzes the consensus argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement about God's speech being one eternal meaning versus many created instances, and why the consensus argument cannot resolve it.

Core Idea
God's speech is one eternal meaning, not multiple created words.

The author begins by stating his foundational belief: the meaning of God's speech is one and eternal. He contrasts this with the opposing view that it is multiple and created. He then explains why the inference from the consensus of scholars fails due to differing premises. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the contrast between 'one eternal' and 'many created'.
2
Identify the two positions
The author states his belief and then the opponent's belief. What are they?
The author holds that the meaning is one and eternal; the opponent holds that it is many and created like the recited words.
3
Understand the consensus argument failure
Why does the author say the inference from the people of consensus came to an end? Reflect on the role of shared premises.
The opponents do not accept the same premises, so the consensus argument is inconclusive.
4
Analyze the analogy
The author draws an analogy: just as our view of eternal speech is not physically present, the opponent's created speech is in another locus. How does this show both sides rely on different premises?
Both sides have different foundational assumptions about the locus of speech.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The agreement reduces to customary premises, not certain ones. Why is this important for theological debate?
It shows that deep disagreements cannot be resolved by appeal to common authority alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتقاد أن ذلك المعنى واحد أزلي، وأنتم تعتقدون أنه مثل هذا كثير حادث فانقطع الاستدلال بما يعرفه أهل الإجماع، وكما أن كلامه أزلي واحد عندنا وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا كذلك هو كلام آخر في محل آخر، وليس ذلك بين أظهرنا عند الخصم، فصار الاتفاق بالمقدمات المشهورة المعهودة لا اليقينية المقبولة المشهودة، وتبين أن إطلاق لفظ القرآن على القراءة والمقروء باشتراك اللفظ، وقد يسمى الدليل باسم المدلول، ويطلق لفظ العلم على المعلوم؛ كقوله تبارك وتعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِّنْ عِلْمِهِ إِلَّا بِمَا شَاءَ ﴾ أي: بمعلومه وقال: ﴿ إِنَّ قُرْآنَ الْفَجْرِ كَانَ مَشْهُودًا ﴾ به الصلاة والقراءة فيها.
Belief that that meaning is one eternal, and you believe that it is like this many and created, so the inference by what the people of consensus know came to an end. And just as His speech is eternal one with us and that is not among us, so too it is another speech in another locus, and that is not among us according to the opponent. So the agreement became based on well-known customary premises, not certain accepted witnessed premises. And it became clear that the application of the word 'Quran' to the recitation and the recited is by homonymy. And sometimes the proof is called by the name of the thing proven, and the word 'knowledge' is applied to the known; like His saying, Blessed and Most High: 'And they encompass not a thing of His knowledge except what He wills' meaning His known thing. And He said: 'Indeed the Quran of dawn is witnessed' by it [i.e., by the Quran] the prayer and the recitation in it.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the nature of God's speech. The author states his belief that the meaning (ma'na) of God's speech is one and eternal, while the opponents hold that it is multiple and created like this [i.e., the recited words]. He then notes that the proof based on the consensus of the scholars (ahl al-ijma') becomes inconclusive because the opponents do not accept the same premises. Drawing an analogy: just as the author's school holds that God's speech is one and eternal and is not physically present among us in this locus, similarly, for the opponent, the [created] speech is in another locus (i.e., the physical words) and is not present among us in the eternal sense. Thus the agreement between the two sides reduces to commonly accepted (mashhurah) premises, not certain (yaqiniyyah) ones. The author then clarifies that the term 'Quran' is used homonymously (ishtirak al-lafz) for both the act of recitation (qira'ah) and the recited text (maqru'). He supports this by noting that sometimes the proof (dalil) is called by the name of the thing proven (madlul), just as 'knowledge' ('ilm) is applied to the known (ma'lum) in the verse: 'They encompass not a thing of His knowledge except what He wills'—explaining 'knowledge' here means 'known thing'. Similarly, the verse 'Indeed the Quran of dawn is witnessed' is interpreted as referring to the prayer and the recitation (qira'ah) within it, showing that 'Quran' can refer to the recited act.

Key Terms
معنى واحد أزلي
كثير حادث
أهل الإجماع
مقدمات مشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the consensus of scholars can definitively prove that God's speech is created or eternal.
Correction: The passage argues that consensus only works if both sides share the same premises; here they do not, so the consensus argument is inconclusive.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the inference from the people of consensus came to an end?
A) Because the opponents do not accept the same premises.
B) Because the consensus is not valid.
C) Because the Quran is created.
D) Because the author does not believe in consensus.

Key Takeaway
Because the two sides disagree on foundational premises, consensus cannot be used as a proof for the nature of God's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a theological disagreement with another Muslim, do you tend to appeal to what 'everyone knows' or to consensus without first examining the foundational premises of each side? What do you risk by doing that?
?
The author says the agreement between the two sides is only based on 'customary premises' not 'certain ones'. Which of your own beliefs about God do you hold merely because they are commonly accepted, rather than because you have examined their foundation? How would you test them?
?
This passage shows that a seemingly shared consensus can mask deep disagreement about first principles. Is there any belief you hold that you have never critically examined because you assumed 'everyone agrees'? What would it take for you to question that assumption?

The Homonymous Use of 'Quran' and Scriptural Evidence
Author explains the homonymous use of 'Quran' and cites Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'Quran' is used homonymously for both the recitation and the recited text, supported by Quranic verses.

Core Idea
'Quran' refers to multiple realities: the eternal speech and the recited words.

The author now clarifies a potential confusion: the word 'Quran' is applied both to the act of recitation (qira'ah) and the recited text (maqru'). He supports this by noting that in language, the proof (dalil) is sometimes called by the name of the thing proven (madlul), and the word 'knowledge' is used for the known. He cites two Quranic verses: one where 'knowledge' means 'known thing', and another where 'Quran of dawn' is interpreted as the prayer and recitation. This lesson examines these examples and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of this passage. Notice the examples.
This passage is about linguistic usage.
2
Identify the claim
The author claims that 'Quran' is used homonymously for recitation and recited text. What does homonymy mean here?
Homonymy: the same word for different meanings.
3
Understand the analogy
The author gives examples where 'proof' is called by the name of the thing proven, and 'knowledge' is used for the known. How do these support his claim?
They show that it is common in language to name one thing by another related thing.
4
Examine the verses
Look at the two Quranic verses. How does the author interpret them to support homonymy?
Verse 2:255 interprets 'knowledge' as 'known'; Verse 17:78 interprets 'Quran of dawn' as prayer and recitation.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
How does this clarification help the earlier argument about God's speech?
It shows that the term 'Quran' can refer to the recited words without implying that the eternal speech is created.

The author states his belief that the meaning (ma'na) of God's speech is one and eternal, while the opponents hold that it is multiple and created like this [i.e., the recited words]. He then notes that the proof based on the consensus of the scholars (ahl al-ijma') becomes inconclusive because the opponents do not accept the same premises.
