Ash'ari's Position and Proof for Divine Hearing and Seeing
al-Ash'ari states his position and gives a proof from the necessity of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's position that God's hearing and seeing are real attributes, and his argument from the necessity of opposites.

Core Idea
Ash'ari argued that if God were not hearing and seeing, He would have defects.

In this lesson, we examine al-Ash'ari's specific doctrine on God's attributes of hearing and seeing. He asserts that these are real, eternal attributes subsisting in God's essence, additional to His knowledge. Then he provides a rational proof: a living being must either have an attribute or its opposite; since God is perfect, He must have the positive attributes of hearing and seeing, not their opposites (deafness and blindness), which are defects. However, the author notes that the premises of this proof require demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read Ash'ari's statement
Read the part of the passage starting with 'وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري...' and its translation. Notice his claim that hearing and seeing are two attributes (سمع and بصر) subsisting in God's essence.
This is the Ash'ari position distinct from others.
2
Understand the proof
Follow the logical argument: if a living being accepts a meaning and has an opposite with no intermediate, it cannot be free from that opposite. Apply to God: if not hearing/seeing, then opposite (deafness/blindness) which is a defect. Since God is perfect, He must be hearing and seeing.
This is a classical kalam argument.
3
Note the caveat
The author says the premises need proof. This indicates that the argument is not complete within this passage; further demonstration is required.
This shows the rigor of theological reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: Ash'ari is establishing that divine attributes are real and distinct from the essence, which is a cornerstone of Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة عشرة في العلم يكون (1) الباري تعالى سميعاً بصيراً (†) لذاته ذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي ومن تابعه من البغداديين إلى أن معنى كونه سميعاً بصير (۳) أنه عالم بالمسموعات والمبصرات لا زائد (۳) على كونه عالما بالمعلومات، (٤) ووافقه جماعة من النجارية (4) ، ومن قال من المعتزلة : إنه سميع بصير فمذهبه مذهب الكعبي لا غير، ومن قال منهم : إن المعنى بكونه سميعاً بصيراً أنه حي لا آفة به فمذهبه بخلاف مذهب الكعبي، وهو الذي صار إليه الجبائي وابنه ، ومنهم من صار إلى أن معنى كونه سميعاً بصيراً أنه مدرك للمسموعات والمبصرات وذلك (٧) زائد على كونه عالما. (۷) (٦) وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله إلى أنه تعالى سميع بسمع، بصير يبصر ، وهما صفتان قائمتان بذاته، زائدتان على كونه عالما. عنه أو ودليله في ذلك أن الحي إذا قبل معنى وله ضد ولا واسطة بين الضدين لم يخل ضده، فلو لم يتصف بكونه سميعاً بصيراً لاتصف بضدهما، وذلك آفة عن ونقص، وهذه المقدمات تحتاج إلى إثبات، فلا بد من البرهان على كل واحدة منهما.

And among them is one who held that the meaning of His being hearing and seeing is that He is perceiver of audibles and visibles, and that is additional to His being knower. Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what would be the consequence if God were not described as hearing and seeing?

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari insists that God's hearing and seeing are real attributes, and his proof relies on the necessity of avoiding defects, though its premises require further proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall that God's hearing and seeing are perfect and free from any defect, do you truly believe that every word you speak and every action you do in private is heard and seen by Him? How would your behavior change if you lived with constant awareness of that reality?
?
Ash'ari's proof argues that God cannot have opposites like deafness or blindness because they are defects. Are there spiritual defects in your own life that you have accepted as 'just the way I am'? What would it mean for you to strive to embody the perfection of God's attributes in your own character, even in small ways?
?
The passage emphasizes that God's attributes of hearing and seeing are real and subsisting in His essence. Do you relate to God as if His attributes are real and personal, or do you sometimes treat them as abstract concepts? What step can you take today to deepen your personal connection to a God who genuinely hears your duas and sees your struggles?

Contrasting Views on Allah's Hearing and Sight
Al-Shahrastani enumerates three views on Allah's hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main theological positions on Allah's attributes of hearing and sight: the reductionist view of al-Ka'bi, the 'living without defect' view of al-Jubba'i, and the affirmation of real attributes by al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
For al-Ash'ari, Allah's hearing and sight are real attributes, not reducible to His knowledge.

Al-Shahrastani presents a survey of different theological schools' views on Allah's hearing and sight. This comparative approach helps clarify why the Ash'ari school insists on affirming distinct attributes. The passage shows the spectrum from reducing hearing and sight to knowledge (al-Ka'bi) to affirming them as real and additional (al-Ash'ari).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to grasp the flow of contrasting positions. Let the author's own sequence—from al-Ka'bi to al-Ash'ari—guide your initial understanding.
Notice the structure: it starts with al-Ka'bi, then Mu'tazilite variations, then al-Ash'ari.
2
Identify each position
List the three positions: al-Ka'bi's reduction to knowledge, al-Jubba'i's 'living without defect', and al-Ash'ari's real attributes. Pay attention to the subtle differences among the Mu'tazilite views.
3
Compare and contrast
Notice what each view adds or denies about divine attributes. Ask: why does it matter whether hearing and sight are attributes or not? How does each school preserve God's unity?
This connects to the broader debate about God's simplicity and the danger of anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on al-Ash'ari's approach
Al-Ash'ari affirms attributes without compromising tawhid. Reflect on how this balances transcendence and immanence, and why the Ash'ari school sees this as the middle path.
This is central to Ash'ari theology and distinguishes it from both reductionism and anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة عشرة في العلم يكون الباري تعالى سميعاً بصيراً لذاته ذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي ومن تابعه من البغداديين إلى أن معنى كونه سميعاً بصيراً أنه عالم بالمسموعات والمبصرات لا زائد على كونه عالما بالمعلومات، ووافقه جماعة من النجارية، ومن قال من المعتزلة : إنه سميع بصير فمذهبه مذهب الكعبي لا غير، ومن قال منهم : إن المعنى بكونه سميعاً بصيراً أنه حي لا آفة به فمذهبه بخلاف مذهب الكعبي، وهو الذي صار إليه الجبائي وابنه، ومنهم من صار إلى أن معنى كونه سميعاً بصيراً أنه مدرك للمسموعات والمبصرات وذلك زائد على كونه عالما. وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله إلى أنه تعالى سميع بسمع، بصير ببصر، وهما صفتان قائمتان بذاته، زائدتان على كونه عالما.
The fifteenth principle concerning knowledge: The Exalted Creator is Hearing, Seeing by His essence. Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi and those who followed him from the Baghdadis held that the meaning of His being Hearing, Seeing is that He is Knower of audible things and visible things, nothing additional to His being Knower of known things. A group of the Najjārīs agreed with him. Whoever among the Mu'tazila says that He is Hearing, Seeing, his school is the school of al-Ka'bi, no other. And whoever among them says that the meaning of His being Hearing, Seeing is that He is Living without defect, his school is contrary to the school of al-Ka'bi, and this is the position adopted by al-Jubbā'ī and his son. And among them are those who adopted that the meaning of His being Hearing, Seeing is that He is perceiver of audible things and visible things, and that is additional to His being Knower. And Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ash'arī, may God have mercy on him, held that He, exalted, is Hearing by hearing, Seeing by sight, and they are two attributes subsisting in His essence, additional to His being Knower.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth principle concerning God's attributes of hearing and sight. It first lists the view of al-Ka'bi and his Baghdad followers, who reduce hearing and sight to knowledge—that God knows audible and visible things without any additional attribute. The Najjārīs agreed. Then it distinguishes two Mu'tazilite positions: one equates the terms with knowledge (like al-Ka'bi); another, adopted by al-Jubbā'ī and his son, interprets them as meaning 'living without defect,' thus denying any real attribute. A third Mu'tazilite view holds that hearing and sight are perceptions additional to knowledge. Finally, al-Ash'ari's position is stated: God is Hearing by a hearing attribute and Seeing by a sight attribute, both subsisting in His essence and additional to His knowledge. This sets the stage for the proof that follows.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's affirmation of hearing and sight as attributes contradicts divine simplicity and implies multiplicity in God.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari affirms attributes as real but not separate from the essence; they are not parts or components but eternal qualities that do not compromise God's unity. The attributes are not identical to the essence nor other than it in a way that implies composition.

Al-Ash'ari affirms Allah's hearing and sight as real, subsisting attributes, distinguishing his school from Mu'tazilite reductionism.

The Proof Structure: God Must Have Hearing and Sight
Al-Shahrastani presents the conditional argument and calls for proving its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Shahrastani's proof that Allah must have the attributes of hearing and sight.

Core Idea
If a living being cannot lack both a quality and its defect, then God must have perfect qualities.

Al-Shahrastani begins his proof by establishing a conditional principle: a living being that can accept a quality with an opposite cannot be free from either the quality or its opposite, and the opposite is a defect. This leads to the conclusion that if God were not Hearing and Seeing, He would be deaf and blind, which is impossible. The proof then requires establishing several premises. This lesson examines the overall argument and its need for supporting premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if X then Y. Understand that the conclusion is that God must have hearing and sight.
Pay attention to the terms 'ضد' (opposite) and 'آفة' (defect).
2
Identify the conditional
The proof starts with 'إذا قبل معنى... لم يخل ضده'. This is the key conditional. Understand that it says if a living being can accept a quality with an opposite, it cannot be free from the opposite.
3
Apply to God
Apply the conditional to God: if He is living (which is established), then He must have either hearing/sight or their opposites. Since opposites are defects and God is transcendent, He must have hearing and sight.
4
Recognize the need for premises
The author states that this proof requires establishing several premises. This sets up the subsequent sections of the argument.
The first premise is discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليله في ذلك أن الحي إذا قبل معنى وله ضد ولا واسطة بين الضدين لم يخل ضده، فلو لم يتصف بكونه سميعاً بصيراً لاتصف بضدهما، وذلك آفة ونقص، وهذه المقدمات تحتاج إلى إثبات، فلا بد من البرهان على كل واحدة منهما. أما المقدمة الأولى فالدليل عليها أن المصحح لقبول السمع والبصر شاهدًا كون الإنسان حيا أنا عرفنا ذلك بطريق السبر؛ إذ لو كان المصحح وجوده أو حدوثه أو قيامه بالنفس، أو غير ذلك من الأوصاف كان منتقضا على الفور، فبقي كونه حيا، والباري تعالى حي فلزم القضاء بكونه موصوفا بالسمع والبصر لتعاليه عن قبول الآفات والنقائص، وليس منكر صحة قبول السمع والبصر أسعد ممن يزعم أن الباري تعالى لا يتصف بالعلم وضده مصيرا إلى استحالة اتصافه حالا بحكميهما.
And his proof for that is that when a living being accepts a meaning and it has an opposite and there is no intermediary between the two opposites, it cannot be free from its opposite. So if He were not described as Hearing, Seeing, He would be described by their opposites, and that is a defect and deficiency. And these premises require proof, so there must be a demonstration for each one of them. As for the first premise, the evidence for it is that the factor that validates the acceptance of hearing and sight—observed—is that the human is living. We know that by the method of examination (sabr); because if the factor were its existence or its origination or its subsistence in itself or other descriptions, it would be invalidated immediately. So it remains that it is being alive. And the Exalted Creator is Living, so it is necessary to judge that He is described with hearing and sight because of His transcendence above accepting defects and deficiencies. And no denier of the validity of accepting hearing and sight is more unfortunate than the one who claims that the Exalted Creator is not described with knowledge and its opposite, adopting the impossibility of His being described with both states simultaneously.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage contains the core proof for affirming hearing and sight as attributes. The proof is structured as a conditional: if a living being (here, God) cannot be free from either a quality or its opposite when the quality has an opposite and no middle ground, then if God is not Hearing/Seeing, He must be deaf and blind, which are defects. The author then notes that this proof requires establishing several premises, and he begins with the first premise: that the basis for a living being's capacity to have hearing and sight is its being alive. He argues by elimination (sabr): the factor cannot be existence, temporality, or self-subsistence, because those would be inconsistent; only 'being alive' remains. Since God is alive, He must have hearing and sight. The passage ends with a rhetorical point: no one who denies hearing and sight is worse off than one who denies both knowledge and its opposite, implying the absurdity of denying all attributes.

Key Terms
الحي
الضد
الآفة
النقص
المقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof assumes God must have all human attributes indiscriminately.
Correction: The proof only applies to attributes where the opposite is a clear defect, not all human traits.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if God is not described as Hearing and Seeing?
A) He would still have hearing and sight potentially.
B) He would be described by their opposites, which are defects.
C) He would be free from all attributes.
D) He would be described as knowing their opposites.

Key Takeaway
The proof that Allah has hearing and sight rests on the principle that a living being cannot lack both a quality and its defect, and since God is living and perfect, He must possess these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not Hearing, He would be deaf. Do you truly worship Allah as the All-Hearing, or do you act as if He does not hear your whispered sins? How would your secret actions change if you fully believed He hears every word?
?
The proof says God cannot have defects. Yet you often attribute to Allah limitations in your heart when things don't go your way. Where have you doubted His perfect attributes? Will you repent and affirm His perfection?
?
This argument relies on the impossibility of a living being lacking both a quality and its opposite. Are there areas of your faith where you try to remain neutral or indifferent, thinking you can avoid both commitment and rejection? What does this passage demand from you?

Proving the First Premise: The Basis for Accepting Hearing and Sight
Al-Shahrastani proves the first premise by eliminating other possible factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani proves that the factor enabling a living being to have hearing and sight is its being alive.

Core Idea
Only being alive makes hearing and sight possible, not existence or temporality.

Having established that the proof requires several premises, al-Shahrastani now demonstrates the first one: the cause that allows a living being to have hearing and sight is its life. He uses a method of elimination (sabr), ruling out other possible factors like existence, origination, or self-subsistence. This lesson examines his reasoning and its application to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that it begins with 'أما المقدمة الأولى' (As for the first premise). It provides evidence using the method of 'sabr' (examination).
2
Identify the method of elimination
The author lists several candidates for the factor (musahhih) that enables hearing/sight: existence, origination (huduth), self-subsistence (qiyam bil-nafs), etc. He argues that each would be invalidated immediately (muntagidan 'ala al-fawr).
3
Understand 'sabr'
Sabr is a method of examining possibilities and eliminating those that lead to inconsistency. The only remaining factor is being alive (kawnuhu hayyan).
4
Apply to God
Since God is Living (Hayy), He must have hearing and sight, because He cannot have defects. The passage ends with a rhetorical jab: denying hearing/sight is worse than denying all attributes like knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Defending Divine Attributes: Opposites and Defects
Al-Shahrastani answers two objections: why must God have opposites if not Hearing/Seeing, and why are those opposites defects?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments that require God to have attributes of perfection and why their opposites are considered defects.

Core Idea
God cannot be free from both a perfection and its opposite; He must have perfections.

Al-Shahrastani addresses two key objections that challenge the necessity of divine attributes. The first objection asks why lack of hearing and sight would imply their opposites; the second asks why those opposites are considered defects. The answers draw on established principles about substances and the nature of praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: two objections and their answers.
2
Identify each objection and its answer
Note the first objection: 'What is the evidence that if He is not described with hearing and sight, He must be described with their opposites?' and its answer. Then the second objection and its answer.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
The first answer relies on the principle that a substance cannot be free from contradictories. Ask yourself: Why is this principle relevant to God?
This connects to earlier discussions about jawhar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أنه إذا لم يتصف بالسمع والبصر يجب أن يتصف بضدهما ؟ قيل : كل ما دل على استحالة عرو الجوهر عن المتضادات فهو دليل على ذلك، وقد سبق القول فيه. فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أن الاتصاف بضد السمع والبصر من النقائص ؟ قيل : لما علم أن السمع والبصر من صفات المدح، فذلك دليل على أن الاتصاف بأضداد ذلك نقائص وآفات ويجب تعاليه عنها، وقد وصف الرب تعالى
If it is said: What is the evidence that if He is not described with hearing and sight, He must be described with their opposites? It is said: Everything that indicates the impossibility of a substance being free from contradictories is evidence for that, and the discussion has preceded concerning it. If it is said: What is the evidence that being described with the opposite of hearing and sight is among the defects? It is said: Because it is known that hearing and sight are attributes of praise, so that is evidence that being described with their opposites are defects and deficiencies, and He must be transcendent above them. And the Lord, the Exalted, has described...
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their answers. First objection: what proves that if God lacks hearing and sight, He must have their opposites (deafness and blindness)? Answer: the principle that a substance (jawhar) cannot be free from contradictories—already established earlier. Second objection: what proves that the opposites of hearing and sight are defects? Answer: since hearing and sight are praiseworthy, their opposites must be blameworthy defects, and God is free from them. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'and the Lord, the Exalted, has described...' indicating further discussion on the page but incomplete in this window.

Key Terms

المتضادات
صفات المدح
النقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies on a human analogy or that hearing and sight are necessary because of some human standard.
Correction: The proof is grounded in logical principles about substances and contradictories, not human analogy. The defectiveness of opposites follows from the praiseworthiness of hearing and sight, which is known by reason and revelation.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is impossible for a substance to be free from contradictories.
C) Because hearing and sight are necessary attributes.
D) Because God's essence is simple.

Key Takeaway
God's perfection requires that He possess praiseworthy attributes like hearing and sight; lacking them would entail defects, which is impossible for God.

?

Proving God's Hearing and Sight Through His Life
Sanusi proves that God has hearing and sight based on His being alive via elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of life necessitates the attributes of hearing and sight for God, based on a rational proof by elimination.

Core Idea
God's life is the basis for His hearing and sight.

Sanusi now moves to a specific rational argument: if God is alive, then He must also have hearing and sight. The argument is built on a careful examination of what makes a being capable of hearing and sight in the created world. Through a process of elimination (السبر), only life remains as the valid basis. This lesson walks through that argument and shows how it applies to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first premise, proof, application, and refutation.
Pay attention to the term المصحح (the corrector).
2
Identify the method of elimination
The author lists possible candidates for the corrector (existence, temporality, etc.) and eliminates them. Only 'being alive' remains.
This method is called السبر (examination/elimination).
3
Apply the conclusion to God
Since God is alive and transcends defects, He must possess hearing and sight.
4
Consider the refutation
The author turns against a denier who claims God has no attributes at all, showing that this position is even weaker.
This is a classic argument against those who deny all attributes (like the Jahmis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى فالدليل عليها أن المصحح لقبول السمع والبصر شاهدًا أن عرفنا ذلك بطريق السبر؛ إذ لو كان المصحح وجوده أو حدوثه أو قيامه بالنفس، أو غير ذلك من الأوصاف كان منتقضا على الفور، فبقي كونه حيا، والباري تعالى حي فلزم القضاء بكونه موصوفا بالسمع والبصر لتعاليه عن قبول الآفات والنقائص، وليس منكر صحة قبول السمع والبصر أسعد ممن يزعم أن الباري تعالى لا يتصف بالعلم وضده مصيرا إلى استحالة اتصافه حالا بحكميهما.
As for the first premise, the proof for it is that the corrector (المصحح) for the acceptance of hearing and sight in the witnessed [world] is that the human is alive. We know this by way of examination (السبر); for if the corrector were its existence, or its temporality, or its subsisting by the soul, or something else among the attributes, it would be instantly contradicted. Thus it remains [that it is] his being alive. And God Almighty is alive, so it is necessarily decreed that He is described with hearing and sight because He is exalted above accepting defects and deficiencies. And no denier of the validity of accepting hearing and sight is more fortunate than one who claims that God Almighty is not described with knowledge and its opposite, proceeding to the impossibility of His being described with them at all.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage states the first premise of the argument for God's possession of hearing and sight. The author begins by identifying the valid basis (المصحح) for attributing hearing and sight to a person in the witnessed world: it is the fact that the person is alive. He explains that this is known through the method of examination (السبر), eliminating alternative possibilities such as existence, temporality, or subsistence by the soul. Since only 'being alive' survives the elimination, it must be the corrector. He then applies this to God: since God is alive and transcendent above defects, He must possess hearing and sight. Finally, he turns the argument against an opponent who denies that God has any attributes at all, claiming that such a position is even less fortunate than denying the validity of the empirical basis for the attributes.

السبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that the proof for God's hearing and sight is based solely on analogy with humans without a valid basis.
Correction: The author uses a rational method of elimination (السبر) to identify 'being alive' as the corrector, which is shared by humans and God, but God's attributes are understood as transcendent and free from defects.

Objection and Answer on Necessity of Opposites for Divine Attributes
Objection and answer about the necessity of opposite attributes when hearing and sight are absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if God lacks hearing and sight He must have their opposites, and the reasoning based on the impossibility of a substance being free of opposites.

Core Idea
Absence of an attribute implies its opposite for any substance.

This short passage presents a crucial objection in kalam: if we deny that God has hearing and sight, does that force us to attribute deafness and blindness to Him? The author answers by invoking a metaphysical principle about substances and opposites, which has been established earlier. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector asks: What is the proof that if God is not described with hearing and sight, He must be described with their opposites? This challenges the necessity of affirming these attributes.
The objector is questioning the logical consequence of denying attributes.
2
Understand the answer
The answer states that the proof is the previously established principle: it is impossible for a substance to be empty of opposites. This principle applies here.
The author refers to a prior discussion about substances and opposites.
3
Connect to the principle
The principle 'no substance can be free of opposites' means that any entity that is a substance must have either one attribute or its opposite. Since God is not a substance but the argument treats Him as such for the sake of objection, the absence of hearing implies the presence of deafness.
This principle was argued earlier in the text.
4
Reflect on the implication
If accepted, this forces us to either affirm hearing and sight for God or accept their opposites, which are imperfections. Thus, the objection strengthens the case for affirming these attributes.
The answer leads to a defense of God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أنه إذا لم يتصف بالسمع والبصر يجب أن يتصف بضدهما ؟ قيل : كل ما دل على استحالة عرو الجوهر عن المتضادات فهو دليل على ذلك، وقد سبق القول فيه.
If it is said: What is the proof that if He is not described with hearing and sight, He must be described with their two opposites? It is said: Everything that indicates the impossibility of a substance (الجوهر) being empty of opposites is the proof for that, and the discussion on it has preceded.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector asks: If God lacks hearing and sight, does He necessarily have their opposites (deafness and blindness)? The answer is yes, based on a previously established principle: it is impossible for a substance (الجوهر) to be free of opposite attributes. Therefore, if God does not have hearing, He must have its opposite, which is deficiency. This answer leads into the next objection.

Key Terms
الجوهر
المتضادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of an attribute does not require its opposite to be present.
Correction: The author argues that for any substance (جوهر), it is impossible to be empty of opposites, so absence of one attribute necessitates the presence of its opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why must God have either hearing and sight or their opposites?
A) Because God cannot lack all attributes
B) Because a substance cannot be free of opposites
C) Because hearing and sight are essential
D) Because of divine perfection

Key Takeaway
The absence of an attribute in God would necessitate its opposite, based on the principle that a substance cannot be free of opposites; this forces us to affirm perfect attributes for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you deny God an attribute like hearing, do you realize you are forced to admit its opposite, deafness? How does that make you feel about the necessity of affirming all of God's perfect attributes?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever casually negate something without considering the implication of ascribing its opposite? What would it mean for your faith if you were wrong?
?
This lesson shows that absence of an attribute implies its opposite for any substance. How does this logical truth deepen your awe of God's perfection, and what will you do today to keep these attributes alive in your mind?

Proving the Deficiency of Opposite Attributes
An objection and answer proving that the opposite of hearing and sight is a deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposite of attributes of praise like hearing and sight are considered deficiencies and how that relates to God's perfection.

Core Idea
Opposites of God's praise-worthy attributes are deficiencies that imply need and temporality.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: how do we know that having the opposite of hearing and sight is a deficiency? The answer lies in the fact that God praises Himself with these attributes, so their opposites must be blameworthy. This leads to a deeper point: deficiency indicates temporal origination and need for a completer, which cannot apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection asks for a proof that being described with the opposite of hearing and sight is a deficiency. Read the Arabic and translation to understand the question.
2
Understand the author's proof
The author argues that hearing and sight are attributes of praise (صفات المدح) because God praises Himself with them in the Quran. Therefore, their opposites must be attributes of blame and deficiency.
Notice the logic: if X is praiseworthy, then not-X is blameworthy.
3
Reflect on the connection to temporal origination
The author states that deficiency is proof of temporal origination (الحدوث) because a deficient being needs a completer (مكمل). Since God is perfect and beyond possibility, He cannot have any deficiency.
This connects divine attributes to God's necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أن الاتصاف بضد السمع والبصر من النقائص ؟ قيل : لما علم أن السمع والبصر من صفات المدح، فذلك دليل على أن الاتصاف بأضداد ذلك نقائص وآفات ويجب تعاليه عنها، وقد وصف الرب تعالى نفسه بالسمع والبصر على وجه التمدح، كما وصف نفسه عالما قادرًا حيا على وجه التمدح ، فلولا أنهما صفتا مدح لما تمدح بهما، فيلزم أن يكون ضدهما صفتا ذم ونقص، والنقص دليل الحدوث، ولو قدرنا الإدراك صفة زائدة على العلم، أو قلنا : إنه العلم ففي نفيه نقص وقصور، فلن يتحقق نقص إلا ممكنا محتاجًا إلى مكمل لتعود الذات إلى الكمال، وذات الباري تعالى وصفاته منزهة عن شوائب الإمكان ولا نقص في ذاته وصفاته.
If it is said: What is the proof that being described with the opposite of hearing and sight is among the deficiencies? It is said: Since it is known that hearing and sight are among the attributes of praise (صفات المدح), that is proof that being described with their opposites are deficiencies and defects, and He must be transcendent above them. And God Almighty described Himself with hearing and sight in the way of self-praise, just as He described Himself as knowing, powerful, and living in the way of self-praise. If they were not attributes of praise, He would not have praised Himself with them. So it necessarily follows that their opposites are attributes of blame and deficiency. And deficiency is proof of temporal origination (الحدوث). And if we suppose that perception is an attribute additional to knowledge, or we say that it is knowledge, then in its negation there is deficiency and shortcoming. And deficiency is only verified for a possible being that needs a completer (مكمل) to return the essence to perfection. And the essence of God Almighty and His attributes are free from the taints of possibility, and there is no deficiency in His essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage continues the objection-and-answer format with a deeper question: What proves that being described with the opposite of hearing and sight is a deficiency? The author replies that hearing and sight are attributes of praise (صفات المدح); God praises Himself with them in the Qur'an. If they were not praiseworthy, He would not have done so. Therefore, their opposites must be attributes of blame and deficiency. Deficiency, in turn, indicates temporal origination (الحدوث), because a deficient being needs a completer to achieve perfection. Since God is perfect and beyond possibility, He cannot have any deficiency. The author also addresses the case where perception is considered either additional to knowledge or identical with it; in either case, its negation implies deficiency.

Key Terms

النقص
الحدوث
المكمل
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that deficiency is just a negative attribute with no further implications.
Correction: The author shows that deficiency is linked to temporal origination and need for a completer, which cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say deficiency is proof of temporal origination (الحدوث)?
A) Because God is eternal
B) Because a deficient being needs a completer, which is characteristic of possible beings
C) Because deficiency is an attribute
D) Because opposites imply deficiency

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are perfect; any opposite of a praise-worthy attribute is a deficiency that implies need and temporality, which cannot apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Do you truly believe that God’s hearing and sight are perfections that He praises Himself for? Or do you sometimes secretly think they are 'just' metaphors or unimportant? How would your prayer change if you meditated on the fact that the opposite of these attributes would be a deficiency?
?
2. This lesson teaches that any deficiency implies need for a completer. Where in your life are you seeking completion from something other than God? What would it look like to rely on Him alone as the only one free from all deficiency?
?
3. If you really internalized that God is free from all deficiency, how would that affect your trust in Him when you feel incomplete or lacking? Would you still seek human approval or material things to 'complete' you?

Al-Ka'bī's Denial of Hearing and Sight as Additional Attributes
Al-Ka'bī argues that hearing and sight are not additional perceptions but are knowledge itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bī's argument that hearing and sight are not additional attributes but are identical to knowledge, and his critique of proving attributes by analogy from the witnessed to the absent.

Core Idea
Perception is knowledge, not a separate attribute.

Al-Ka'bī, a Mu'tazilī theologian, presents a radical challenge to the Ashʿarī method of proving divine attributes. He argues that what we call 'hearing' and 'sight' are not additional perceptions beyond knowledge; rather, they are knowledge itself obtained through sensory instruments. This passage introduces his view and his critique of the proof by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation to grasp al-Ka'bī's core claim that perception is knowledge.
Pay attention to the terms 'الإدراك' and 'العلم'.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'الإدراك', 'العلم', 'الحاسة', and 'الوسائط'. Understand how al-Ka'bī redefines them to reduce perception to knowledge.
Note that 'الحاسة' is simply the instrument, not the perception itself.
3
Analyze the argument structure
The passage has two parts: first, al-Ka'bī rejects the analogy from witnessed to absent; second, he gives his positive account that perception is knowledge. Notice how he distinguishes between the perceiver, the instrument, and the act.
This argument challenges the need for separate attributes of hearing and sight in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الكعبي : إنما يصح لكم إثبات الإدراك غائبًا بعد إثباته شاهدًا، ونحن لا نساعدكم أن السمع والبصر إدراكان زائدان على العلم. أما الكعبي فقد قال : الذي يجده الإنسان من نفسه إدراكه للمسموع والمبصر بقلبه () وعقله، ولا يحس بصره بالمبصر بل يحس المبصر ويسمع السامع لا الأذان، وذلك هو العلم حقيقة ولكن لما لم يحصل له ذلك العلم إلا بوسائط بصره سمي البصر حاسة، وإلا فالمدرك هو العالم وإدراكه ليس زائدا على علمه.
Al-Ka'bī said: It is only valid for you to prove perception in the absent after proving it in the witnessed, and we do not agree with you that hearing and sight are two perceptions additional to knowledge. As for al-Ka'bī, he said: What a human finds in himself is his perception of the heard and seen by his heart and intellect, and his sight does not perceive the visible object, but rather he perceives the visible object and he hears the audibly perceived, not the ears. That is in reality knowledge. But because that knowledge is only obtained through the mediation of his sight, it is called the sense of sight. Otherwise, the perceiver is the knower, and his perception is not additional to his knowledge.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage introduces the view of al-Ka'bī, a Mu'tazilī theologian. He challenges the author's method of proving attributes by analogy from the witnessed (شاهدًا) to the absent (غائبًا). Al-Ka'bī denies that hearing and sight are perceptions additional to knowledge. He argues that what humans experience as perception is actually knowledge obtained through the senses; the senses are merely instruments. The real perceiver is the intellect, and perception is not separate from knowledge. Therefore, for God, there is no additional attribute of hearing and sight beyond knowledge.

Key Terms
الإدراك
العلم
الحاسة
الوسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bī denies that sense organs exist or that perception occurs.
Correction: Al-Ka'bī does not deny sense organs; he argues that perception is not additional to knowledge, but is knowledge itself mediated by the senses.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bī, what is the relationship between perception and knowledge?
A) Perception is additional to knowledge
B) Perception is identical to knowledge
C) Perception is a type of knowledge
D) Perception and knowledge are unrelated

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bī reduces hearing and sight to knowledge, denying them as separate attributes and challenging the analogy-based proof for divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe your senses give you direct knowledge, or have you ever considered that they are just mediators? How does that change your certainty in what you 'see' and 'hear' about God?
?
If al-Ka'bī is right that perception is just knowledge, what does that say about your own reliance on sensory experiences in your faith? Are you willing to admit that your 'knowing' may be skewed by the instruments?
?
Al-Ka'bī challenges the very method of proving God's attributes by analogy from humans. How comfortable are you with the idea that your own mind might be projecting human limits onto God? Will you let this unsettle your assumptions, or will you push it aside?

Distinguishing Knowledge by Report from Sight: Refuting al-Ka'bī
Proof refuting al-Ka'bī via distinction between knowledge by report and sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between knowledge acquired through report and direct sight, and how this distinguishes perceived knowledge from mere belief.

Core Idea
Direct sight adds a holistic distinction beyond mere knowledge by report.

Al-Ka'bī argued that perception does not add any new knowledge beyond simple belief. The author of this text counters by appealing to direct human experience: when we know something by report and then see it, we unmistakably sense a difference. This passage explores the nature of that difference, arguing it is not a simple categorical change but a holistic transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage is a direct response to al-Ka'bī's view.
2
Identify the three key elements
The passage presents a proof: (1) a person experiences a distinction when seeing after report, (2) this distinction is not generic or specific, (3) it is a holistic distinction. Identify each element.
3
Reflect on the holistic distinction
Ask yourself what 'holistic distinction' (تفرقة جملة) means. How does it differ from a distinction of genus or species? Consider an example from your own experience.
This distinction is central to the argument against al-Ka'bī.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This argument refutes the idea that perception adds no new knowledge. Understand how proving a distinction supports the addition of knowledge through sight.
The argument is cut off, but the point is clear: sight provides a unique form of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على ذلك أن من علم شيئًا بالخبر ثم رأه بالبصر وجد تفرقة بين الحالتين، إلا أن تلك التفرقة ليست تفرقة جنس وجنس ونوع ونوع، بل تفرقة جملة
And the proof of that is that one who knows something by report then sees it with sight finds a distinction between the two states, except that that distinction is not a distinction of genus from genus or species from species, but rather a distinction of totality (جملة).
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage provides a proof refuting al-Ka'bī's view. The author points out that a person who knows something by report (الخبر) and then sees it directly with sight finds a clear distinction between the two states. This distinction is not merely generic or specific; it is a holistic distinction (تفرقة جملة). This demonstrates that seeing provides knowledge that is different from mere knowledge by report, implying that perception adds something beyond knowledge. The argument is cut off, but the point is clear.

Key Terms

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

God's Hearing and Sight as Attributes of Praise
Ash'ari proof that hearing and sight are real attributes of divine perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians affirm that God's attributes of hearing and sight are real and necessary for divine perfection, based on God's self-praise and the implications of deficiency.

Core Idea
God's self-praise proves that His hearing and sight are real attributes of perfection.

This passage presents an argument from divine self-description. Because God praises Himself with hearing and sight, just as He does with knowledge and power, these attributes cannot be mere metaphors or reducible to knowledge. Denying them would imply deficiency, which is incompatible with God's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how the argument unfolds.
Pay attention to the logical structure: praise → reality → opposite is deficiency.
2
Identify the two key premises
Find where the argument states that God describes Himself with hearing/sight in a praiseworthy manner, and that the opposite would be deficiency. How does deficiency imply temporality?
The passage connects deficiency to contingency (imkan).
3
Consider the objection about perception
The passage addresses a possible objection: even if perception is additional to knowledge or identical to it, denying it still implies deficiency. Reflect on why this strengthens the argument.
This shows the necessity of these attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفسه بالسمع والبصر على وجه التمدح، كما وصف نفسه عالما قادرًا حيا على وجه التمدح، فلولا أنهما صفتا مدح لما تمدح بهما، فيلزم أن يكون ضدهما صفتا ذم ونقص، والنقص دليل الحدوث، ولو قدرنا الإدراك صفة زائدة على العلم، أو قلنا : إنه العلم ففي نفيه نقص وقصور، فلن يتحقق نقص إلا ممكنا محتاجًا إلى مكمل لتعود الذات إلى الكمال، وذات الباري تعالى وصفاته منزهة عن شوائب الإمكان ولا نقص في ذاته وصفاته.
Himself with hearing and sight in a praiseworthy manner, just as He described Himself as knowing, powerful, living in a praiseworthy manner. Had they not been attributes of praise, He would not have praised Himself with them. So it follows that their opposites are attributes of blame and deficiency. And deficiency is evidence of temporality. And if we suppose that perception is an attribute additional to knowledge, or we say that it is knowledge, then in its negation there is deficiency and shortcoming. For deficiency only occurs to a contingent being in need of a completer to return the essence to perfection. And the essence of the Exalted Creator and His attributes are transcendent of the stains of contingency, and there is no deficiency in His essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's attributes of hearing and sight are real and necessary for divine perfection. The argument proceeds in steps: First, God describes Himself as having hearing and sight in a praiseworthy manner, just as He describes Himself as knowing, powerful, and living. This self-praise shows that these attributes are inherently praiseworthy; otherwise, God would not have praised Himself with them. Consequently, their opposites—deafness and blindness—are attributes of blame and deficiency. Deficiency is a sign of contingency (hadath), which cannot apply to God. The passage then addresses a possible objection: if one says perception is an attribute additional to knowledge, or if one says it is identical to knowledge, denying it would still imply deficiency. Any deficiency only exists in a contingent being that needs something else to achieve perfection. But God's essence and attributes are free from any stain of contingency or deficiency.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
مدح
نقص
حدوث
إدراك
علم
إمكان
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's hearing and sight are metaphorical or reducible to His knowledge.
Correction: The passage argues they are real attributes of praise, because God praises Himself with them, and denying them would imply deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence of denying that hearing and sight are attributes of praise for God?
A) It would imply that God is imperfect
B) It would imply deficiency, which is evidence of contingency
C) It would imply that God does not exist
D) It would imply that God is limited in power

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are real attributes of praise, and denying them would imply deficiency, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God praises Himself with hearing and sight, do you truly believe that He hears every word you speak and sees every action you do? How does that certainty change what you say when no one is listening, or what you do when no one is watching?
?
The passage proves that denying God's hearing and sight would imply deficiency in Him. Yet we often live as if He does not see us in our private moments. What one area of your life would you change today if you truly believed He sees and hears all?
?
Deficiency belongs only to contingent beings in need of a completer. In what ways have you been living as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your very existence depends on the Perfect One? What practical step can you take this week to acknowledge your need for Him?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection: Perception is Knowledge
Al-Ka'bi argues that hearing and sight are not additional attributes but merely knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ka'bi's argument that hearing and sight are not separate attributes but forms of knowledge, and his reductio ad absurdum against additional perception.

Core Idea
Perception is not a separate attribute; it is just knowledge through a medium.

In this passage, Al-Ka'bi (a prominent Mu'tazilite) challenges the Ash'ari position that hearing and sight are attributes additional to knowledge. He insists that what we call perception is simply knowledge obtained through sensory means, and he provides both a positive argument and a reductio ad absurdum to support his view. Understanding this objection is crucial for grasping the Mu'tazilite stance on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice Al-Ka'bi's direct challenge to the Ash'ari position.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'إدراك', 'علم', 'شاهد', 'غائب'.
2
Identify Al-Ka'bi's main claim
Al-Ka'bi asserts that hearing and sight are not additional perceptions beyond knowledge. He redefines perception as knowledge gained through sensory means (like the eyes or ears).
He says: 'that is truly knowledge' – perception is not a separate attribute.
3
Understand his proof from the two states
He argues that knowing something by report and then seeing it yields a difference, but it is not a difference in genus or species—only in detail or specificity. The underlying awareness is the same.
This shows that the difference is not due to a separate perception but to the manner of knowledge.
4
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Ka'bi argues that if perception were a separate attribute, a person with sound senses would necessarily perceive all that is present (like a singing girl, cattle, drums, trumpets) – but we know this is false. Also, it would allow absurd possibilities like seeing distant but not near objects.
The reductio shows that the Ash'ari position leads to contradictions with necessary knowledge.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument fits into the Mu'tazilite project: they deny distinct attributes to preserve God's unity and avoid multiplicity in the divine essence.
Al-Ka'bi is not denying that God hears and sees; he reinterprets these as aspects of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الكعبي : إنما يصح لكم إثبات الإدراك غائبًا بعد إثباته شاهدًا، ونحن لا نساعدكم أن السمع والبصر إدراكان زائدان على العلم. أما الكعبي فقد قال : الذي يجده الإنسان من نفسه إدراكه للمسموع والمبصر بقلبه وعقله، ولا يحس بصره بالمبصر بل يحس المبصر ويسمع السامع لا الأذان، وذلك هو العلم حقيقة ولكن لما لم يحصل له ذلك العلم إلا بوسائط بصره سمي البصر حاسة، وإلا فالمدرك هو العالم وإدراكه ليس زائدا على علمه. والدليل على ذلك أن من علم شيئًا بالخبر ثم رأه بالبصر وجد تفرقة بين الحالتين، إلا أن تلك التفرقة ليست تفرقة جنس وجنس ونوع ونوع، بل تفرقة جملة وتفصيل، وعموم وخصوص وإطلاق وتعيين، أوإلا فشعور النفس بهما في الحالتين واحد. قال : ولو كان المدرك مدركًا بإدراك للزم أن يحضر عند الحاسة السليمة قينة تلعب، وأنعام تسرح، وطبول تضرب، وصور تنفخ فيه، وهو لا يراها ولا يسمعها؛ إذ لم يخلق له إدراك ذلك، وكذلك يجوز أن يرى الشخص البعيد ولا يرى القريب؛ لأنه خلق له إدراك البعيد دون القريب، وقد علمنا ضرورة أن الأمر على خلاف ذلك.
Al-Ka'bi said: 'It is only valid for you to prove perception in the unseen after proving it in the witnessed, and we do not agree with you that hearing and sight are two perceptions additional to knowledge. As for Al-Ka'bi, he said: What a person finds in himself is his perception of the heard and seen by his heart and mind, and his sight does not sense the seen object, rather he senses the seen object and he hears the hearer not the ears, and that is truly knowledge, but since that knowledge is not obtained except through the means of his sight, it was called the sense of sight, otherwise the perceiver is the knower and his perception is not additional to his knowledge. The proof of that is that someone who knows a thing by report then sees it with his eyes finds a difference between the two states, but that difference is not a difference of genus and genus or species and species, but a difference of summary and detail, generality and specificity, absoluteness and determinacy, otherwise the self's awareness of them in both states is one. He said: And if the perceiver were perceiving by a perception, it would necessitate that in the presence of a sound sense, there would be a singing girl playing, cattle grazing, drums beating, and trumpets blown into, and he does not see them nor hear them, since perception of that was not created for him. Likewise, it would be possible for a person to see the distant and not see the near because he was created with perception of the distant without the near, whereas we know by necessity that the matter is contrary to that.'
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Ka'bi's objection to the Ash'ari claim that hearing and sight are additional attributes beyond knowledge. Al-Ka'bi begins by challenging the methodology: he argues that one must first establish perception in the witnessed (shahid) world before extrapolating to the unseen (gha'ib). He states that the Mu'tazila do not accept that hearing and sight are two perceptions separate from knowledge. He then explains his own view: what a person finds within himself is perception of the heard and seen by his heart and mind; the sight does not actively sense objects, but rather the person senses objects and hears the hearer (the sound) not the ears. This is truly knowledge ('ilm). It is only called 'sight' because it occurs through the medium of the eyes, but the perceiver is the knower ('alim), and his perception is not additional to his knowledge. He provides a proof: a person who knows something by report and then sees it with his eyes finds a difference, but that difference is not one of genus or species; it is merely a difference of detail, generality, etc. Otherwise, the self's awareness would be identical. He then presents a reductio ad absurdum: if perception were a separate attribute, then a person with sound senses would necessarily perceive everything present (like a singing girl, cattle, drums, trumpets) which is false. It would also allow for seeing distant but not near objects, which contradicts necessary experience.

شاهد

سمع

وسائط
حاسة
تفرقة
جنس
نوع
شعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi denies that God hears and sees at all.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi does not deny that God hears and sees; he reinterprets these as aspects of God's knowledge. He only denies that they are additional attributes separate from knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ka'bi, what is the true nature of perception?
A) It is an attribute additional to knowledge
B) It is the same as knowledge
C) It is a physical sense independent of the mind
D) It is a purely mental concept with no reality

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi argues that hearing and sight are not separate attributes but merely knowledge obtained through sensory means, using both a positive proof and a reductio ad absurdum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something beautiful or hear a moving verse, do you realize that according to Al-Ka'bi, what is happening is that your heart is knowing through a medium? Does your own experience confirm that the perceiver is the knower, not your eyes or ears? How would this awareness change how you value your senses in your worship and daily life?
?
Al-Ka'bi says the difference between knowing by report and direct seeing is only in detail, not essence. Reflect on a time you learned about a truth of Islam (like the reality of the Hereafter) through teaching, and then felt it deeply in your heart during reflection. Was your awareness of the truth essentially one? What does that imply about the nature of faith? Are you content with secondhand knowledge, or are you striving for firsthand experience of divine truths?
?
If perception is knowledge, then every sensory experience is an act of knowing. In your daily life, you are constantly bombarded with sights and sounds—yet you ignore most. Are you controlling what you 'know' through your senses, or are your senses distracting you from what truly matters? How can you train your attention to use your senses as instruments for knowing Allah and drawing closer to Him?

Al-Jubba'i on Perception as Soundness of the Self
Al-Jubba'i defines perception as the soundness of the self and provides a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jubba'i's definition of perception as the soundness of the self and his argument that perception is not an extra attribute.

Core Idea
For Al-Jubba'i, perception is simply the state of a sound self, not a separate attribute.

Al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazilite theologian, offered a distinctive view on divine perception. Rather than treating perception as an additional attribute alongside God's essence, he argued that it is merely the condition of a sound self, free from defect. This passage presents his definition and a proof from the universality of perception when the self is sound. Understanding this view is key to early Islamic debates on the nature of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Jubba'i's definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Jubba'i equates perception with the soundness of the self, not with any extra entity.
He uses the phrase 'there is no meaning for perception other than that' to emphasize this.
2
Identify the key terms
Pay special attention to 'nafs' (self) and 'afa' (defect). Also note 'samī' (hearing) and 'basīr' (seeing) as examples.
3
Examine the proof
Al-Jubba'i argues that a sound self perceives all things equally, including opposites like black and white. If perception were a separate attribute, it could vary, but it doesn't.
The proof relies on the idea that an extra attribute could be selective.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this view means for divine attributes: if perception is just soundness, then God's hearing and seeing are not distinct entities but aspects of His perfect self.
This stands in contrast to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجبائي : إن الحي إذا سلمت نفسه عن الآفة سمي سميعاً بصيراً، ولا معنى للإدراك شاهدًا وغائبًا إلا ذلك. والدليل عليه : أن الذات إذا سلمت أدركت كل معروض عليها من المتماثلات والمختلفات، وإدراكها السواد كإدراكها البياض، ولو كان مدركا بإدراك لجاز أن يدرك بعض الأشياء دون بعض مما يقابل المدرك، فكان يجوز أن يدرك بياضا ولا يدرك سوادا؟
Al-Jubba'i said: 'The living being, when his self is free from defect, is called hearing and seeing, and there is no meaning for perception, witnessed or unseen, other than that. The proof of it is that when the self is sound, it perceives every thing presented to it from similar and different things, and its perception of black is like its perception of white. And if it were perceiving by a perception, it would be possible for it to perceive some things without others among what confronts the perceiver; so it would be possible for it to perceive whiteness without perceiving blackness?'
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Jubba'i's view on perception. He defines perception simply as the state of a living being whose self is free from defect (afa). When that condition holds, the being is described as 'hearing' and 'seeing', and perception has no reality beyond that. In other words, it is not an additional attribute but a holistic property of a sound self. He offers a proof: when the self is sound (sullimat), it perceives every object presented to it, whether similar (mutamathilat) or different (mukhtalifat), and its perception of black equals its perception of white. If perception were a separate attribute (bi-idrak), it would be possible for it to perceive some things and not others, but this is not the case. Therefore, perception is not an extra attribute. The passage ends with a rhetorical question highlighting the absurdity of such a possibility.

Key Terms
إدراك
نفس
آفة
سميع
بصير
شاهد
غائب
متماثلات
مختلفات
سواد
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Jubba'i entirely denies that God has attributes like hearing and seeing.
Correction: He reinterprets them as the state of a sound self, not as separate entities; he does not deny that God hears and sees, but redefines what that means.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jubba'i, what is the meaning of perception?
A) It is an additional attribute
B) It is the soundness of the self from defect
C) It is an act of the sense organs
D) It is a mental representation of objects

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i redefines perception as the soundness of the self, arguing that it is not an extra attribute by showing that a sound self perceives all opposites equally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i claims that a sound self perceives opposites equally. When you evaluate your own judgments—about people, ideas, or actions—do you find that you perceive both sides with equal clarity? What 'defects' in your self might be causing imbalance?
?
If perception is not an added quality but simply the state of your self, then how you perceive God's commands and prohibitions reveals the condition of your soul. What does your current perception of right and wrong tell you about the soundness of your own self?
?
The absurdity Al-Jubba'i highlights is the possibility of perceiving whiteness without blackness. In your life, are there truths you selectively perceive while ignoring their opposites? What would it take for you to allow your self to be sound enough to see both?

Objection: Perception Requires More Than Life
An objection arguing that perception requires additional structural conditions besides life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that perception demands additional conditions beyond life, and why this challenges proving Allah's attributes from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Perception demands structure and mediums — life alone is not enough.

In this passage, an opponent grants that perception occurs in the witnessed world but denies that life is the sole condition. They list structural and environmental conditions for the senses, arguing that if some senses require contact, then sight and hearing must require other conditions. This poses a challenge to those who argue from witnessed perception to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the opponent's concession and their list of conditions.
The opponent starts by granting perception exists but rejects life as the sole ground.
2
Understand the analogy
The opponent compares three senses (smell, touch, taste) that require contact to the remaining two (sight, hearing) that require mediums. Grasp the logic: if some senses need extra conditions, all might.
The analogy uses 'by consensus' to strengthen the claim.
3
Analyze the challenge
The opponent forces the other side to either prove life suffices or treat all conditions as customary. Reflect on why this is problematic for arguments from the witnessed to the unseen.
This is a classic kalām difficulty: moving from observable to unobservable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وإن سلمنا ثبوت الإدراك في الشاهد فلم نسلم أن المصحح مجرد كون الحيوان حيًّا فقط، وهاهنا شرط آخر وهو حصول البنية، وشرط للشرط وهو توسط الهوى المضيء بين المبصر والمبصر، والهوى الصافي بين السمع والمسموع، كما في الشم واللمس والذوق اتصال الأجرام ومماستها بالاتفاق، وإذا استدعت الثلاث من الحواس شرطًا حتى يتحقق الإدراك، ولأجل ذلك امتنع إثباته غائبا، كذلك الحاستان الباقيتان استدعتا شرائط أخر سوى كونه حيا حتى يتحقق الإدراك، فأنتم مدفوعون إلى إقامة الدليل على حصر الشرائط في كونه حيًّا فقط، وإلا فارفعوا كون الحياة شرطًا كما رفعتم كون البنية وتوسط الهوى شرطًا، واجعلوا وجود الحياة شرطًا من حيث العادة لا من حيث الضرورة، كما جعلتم البنية شرطاً من حيث العادة، وهذا الموضع من مشكلات مسائل الإدراكات.
They said: And if we grant the existence of perception in the witnessed world, we do not grant that the ground is merely the animal being alive; rather there is another condition here, namely the possession of structure, and a condition for the condition, namely the mediation of luminous air between the seer and the seen, and pure air between hearing and the heard, just as in smell, touch, and taste there is contact of bodies and their touching by consensus. And if three of the senses require a condition for perception to occur, and for that reason it is impossible to affirm it in the unseen, likewise the remaining two senses require other conditions besides being alive for perception to occur. So you are forced to produce a proof that the conditions are limited to being alive only; otherwise, remove life as a condition just as you removed structure and the mediation of air as conditions, and make existence of life a condition by way of habit not by way of necessity, as you made structure a condition by way of habit. And this is a problematic point in the issues of perceptions.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent (likely a Mu'tazilite or philosopher) grants that perception occurs in the witnessed world (shāhid) but denies that the sole enabler is life (ḥayāt). They list additional conditions: physical structure (binya) and a medium (hawā')—specifically luminous air for sight and pure air for hearing. By analogy, they argue that if three senses (smell, touch, taste) require contact of bodies, then the remaining two (sight and hearing) must require other conditions besides life. The opponent then challenges the other side to either prove that life alone suffices or abandon life as a necessary condition and treat all conditions as merely customary (ʿāda), not necessitating when transposed to the unseen (ghā'ib). The author (Imām al-Ḥaramayn) notes this is a problematic point in discussions of perception.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrāk)
شرط (sharṭ)
بنية (binya)
هوى (hawā')
حيوان (ḥayawān)
عادة (ʿāda)
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
شاهد (shāhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent denies perception entirely.
Correction: The opponent actually concedes perception occurs in the witnessed world; they only dispute the conditions necessary for it.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what additional condition do smell, touch, and taste require?
A) Life
B) Structure
C) Contact and touching of bodies
D) A medium of air

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that sensory perception requires conditions beyond life, challenging the extrapolation from witnessed to divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that sensory perception in the created world requires specific physical conditions, does it ever cause you to doubt that Allah hears and sees all things without any medium? How does this affect your awareness of His constant witness over your life?
?
The opponent argues that life alone is not enough for perception—have you ever unconsciously limited Allah's attributes to what you can imagine from your own experience? Which of your actions today would be different if you truly believed that Allah sees you without any barrier?
?
This passage forces you to choose: either accept that life alone suffices for divine perception or reduce it to a mere custom. Which choice do you make in your heart, and does your daily practice reflect a firm belief that Allah perceives every detail of your existence without needing any condition?

Al-Ash'ari's First View on Perception
Al-Ash'ari enumerates his two views on perception and elaborates the first view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's first position on the nature of perception, how it differs from knowledge, and how it contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view of al-Ka'bi.

Core Idea
Perception is like knowledge but requires a determinate object.

Al-Ash'ari presents two views on perception. In this passage, he explains the first view, which classifies perception as a kind of knowledge, differing only in that perception demands a specific object of perception. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite position of al-Ka'bi, who denied any real attribute of perception. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari theory of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's flow.
Notice the two statements mentioned at the start.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage explains that perception and knowledge are alike except for one thing: perception requires determination of the object (taʿyīn al-mudrak), while knowledge does not. This means perception cannot relate to non-existent things.
Think about why this matters for divine attributes.
3
Distinguish from al-Ka'bi
Al-Ash'ari explicitly says his first view is not the same as al-Ka'bi's. Al-Ka'bi denied any real meaning (maʿnā) for perception, while al-Ash'ari affirms it and says it is of the genus of knowledge.
This clarifies that Ash'ari affirms a real attribute.
4
Consider the implication for God's attributes
The passage concludes that on this view, God's Hearing and Seeing are two perceptions that are two particular knowledges, additional to His being Knower.
This connects perception to the broader theology of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الأشعري: الإدراكات من قبيل العلوم على قول وهي جنس آخر على قول، فمن قال بالقول الأول فيقول: هو مماثل للعلوم ولا يفترقان إلا في أن أحد العلمين يستدعي تعيين المدرك، والعلم من حيث هو علم لا يستدعي تعيين المدرك فلا جرم بقول العلم يتعلق بالمعدوم والإدراك لا يتعلق به؛ إذ المعدوم لا يتعين، ولا تظنن أن هذا الرأي هو مذهب الكعبي، فإنه لم يثبت لإدراك معنى أصلا، والأشعري أثبت له معنى وقال هو من جنس العلوم، فعلى هذا القول الرب تعالى سميع بصير بإدراكين؛ هما علمان مخصوصان وراء كونه عالما هو مدلول الإحكام والإتقان.
Al-Ashʿarī said: Perceptions are of the genus of knowledge according to one statement, and they are another genus according to another statement. Whoever holds the first statement says: it is similar to knowledges, and they do not differ except in that one of the two knowledges requires determination of the object of perception, while knowledge as such does not require determination of the object of perception. Hence, knowledge relates to the non-existent and perception does not relate to it, because the non-existent is not determined. And do not think that this opinion is the doctrine of al-Kaʿbī, for he did not affirm any meaning for perception at all, while al-Ashʿarī affirmed a meaning for it and said it is of the genus of knowledge. So according to this statement, the Lord, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing by two perceptions; they are two particular knowledges beyond His being Knower, which is the meaning indicated by (His) perfect ordination and precision.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage classifies al-Ash'ari's two positions on perception (idrākāt). The first position holds that perception is of the same genus (jins) as knowledge (ʿilm). The difference is only that perception requires a determinate object (taʿyīn al-mudrak) — it only applies to existent, specific entities — while knowledge can relate to non-existent (maʿdūm) things because the non-existent lacks determination. Al-Ash'ari distinguishes his view from the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi, who denied any real attribute (maʿnā) for perception. Al-Ash'ari affirms that perception has a real meaning and is a kind of knowledge. Consequently, God's attributes of Hearing and Seeing are two perceptions that are two specific knowledges, additional to His being Knower, which is indicated by the perfection of His acts (iḥkām and itqān).

Key Terms
إدراكات (idrākāt)
علوم (ʿulūm)
جنس (jins)
تعيين (taʿyīn)
مدرك (mudrak)
معدوم (maʿdūm)
معنى (maʿnā)
إحكام (iḥkām)
إتقان (itqān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari's first view is the same as al-Ka'bi's denial of perception as a distinct attribute.
Correction: Ash'ari explicitly distinguishes his view: he affirms that perception has a real meaning (maʿnā) and is a type of knowledge, unlike al-Ka'bi who denied any meaning for perception.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's first view, what distinguishes perception from knowledge?
A) Perception relates only to existents because it requires determination of the object
B) Perception is a different genus
C) Perception does not require any conditions
D) Perception is identical to knowledge

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's first view holds that perception is a kind of knowledge that requires a determinate object, and this is distinct from the Mu'tazilite denial of any real attribute of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If perception requires a specific, determined object, then God perceives every detail of your life as a distinct reality. Are you living as if your every thought and action is fully and specifically known and perceived by Him? What would change if you truly internalized that He perceives even the most hidden parts of your being?
?
Al-Ashʿarī warns against confusing his view with those who deny real attributes to God. Have you ever, in your heart, reduced God's attributes — like His hearing or seeing — to mere metaphors or intellectual concepts? How would your worship change if you genuinely believed that His perception is a real and distinct attribute beyond mere knowledge?
?
This passage distinguishes between what God knows (including non-existents) and what He perceives (actual, determinate realities). Do you treat God's knowledge of your potential sins the same as His perception of your actual sins? How does that distinction affect your sense of accountability for what you actually do versus what you only think?
