Proof that Perception Differs from Knowledge
A rational proof for the distinction between knowledge and perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that perception is a distinct genus from knowledge, based on the experience of difference and the blind person example.

Core Idea
Perception adds something beyond knowledge, as shown by the blind person's lack.

Sanusi presents the second position with its proof. The argument relies on our direct awareness of a difference between knowing something by report and perceiving it. He also uses the illuminating example of a blind person who can know everything a sighted person knows except the perception itself. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a premise about knowledge, an observation of difference, and an example.
The proof is based on comparing two states: knowledge vs. perception.
2
Understand the key premise
The author states that if two instances of knowledge have the same object and are equal in inner attributes, they cannot differ. This is a logical principle.
This sets up the argument that since we find a difference between knowledge and perception, they must be different genera.
3
Analyze the blind person example
The blind person can know everything about an object that a sighted person knows, but lacks the visual perception. This shows perception is additional to knowledge.
This is a concrete way to see that perception is not reducible to knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال بالقول الثاني استدل بأن العلم بالشيء إذا أضيف إلى علم آخر به، واتحد المتعلق واستوى العلمان في صفات النفس لم يتصور اختلافهما، فلما وجدنا في أنفسنا اختلافا بين العلم والإدراك، وأحسسنا تفرقة بين المعلوم خبرا وبين المرئي مع اتحاد المتعلق واستواء الأمر في الحدوث، علمنا أن الجنسين مختلفان، وأيضا فإن الأكمه إذا أحاط علما بما أحاط به البصير حصل له كل علم سوى الإدراك، فدل أنه زائد على العلم على أن هذا يشكل بقاعدة من لم يجعل البنية شرطًا، فإنه جوز أن يخلق الله تبارك وتعالى الروية في القلب والعلم في البصر، فحينئذ يلتبس العلم بالإدراك ويلزم أن يسمع السامع بالبصر، ويبصر بالسمع، ويشم بحاسة الذوق، ويذوق بحاسة الشم، ويلزم أن تكون حاسة واحدة هي سامعة مبصرة شامة ذائقة لامسة، وإنما اختص كل إدراك بحاسة خاصة في خلقه.
And whoever holds the second statement argues that knowledge, when added to another knowledge of the same thing, and the object is one, and the two knowledges are equal in attributes of the soul, it is inconceivable that they differ. So when we find in ourselves a difference between knowledge and perception, and we sense a distinction between the known as an object of report and the seen, despite unity of object and equality in temporality, we know that the two genera are different. Also, the blind person when he encompasses knowledge of everything that the sighted person encompasses, he obtains every knowledge except perception. So it proves that (perception) is additional to knowledge. However, this is problematic according to the principle of one who does not make structure a condition, for he allows that God, blessed and exalted, may create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye; then knowledge and perception become confused, and it would necessitate that the hearer hears with the eye, sees with hearing, smells with the sense of taste, tastes with the sense of smell, and it would necessitate that one sense is hearing, seeing, smelling, tasting, touching. And each perception is only distinguished by a specific sense in its creation.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the second view that perception is a separate genus from knowledge. The argument: if two instances of knowledge have the same object and are equal in soul's attributes, they cannot differ. But we experience a difference between knowing something by report and perceiving it directly (e.g., seeing). Also, a blind person (akmah) can know everything a sighted person knows except the visual perception itself, showing perception is additional to knowledge. However, this proof faces a difficulty for those who deny that physical structure (binya) is a necessary condition for perception. They allow that God could create vision (ru'ya) in the heart and knowledge in the eye, blurring the distinction. This would lead to absurd consequences: mixing of senses, so that one sense could perform all functions. The author concludes that each perception is assigned to a specific sense by God's creation, preserving distinction.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm - knowledge)
إدراك (idrāk - perception)
جنس (jins - genus)
متعلق (mutaʿallaq - object)
أكمه (akmah - blind person)
بصير (baṣīr - sighted person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that perception is just a kind of knowledge, and the difference is only in intensity.
Correction: The proof shows that perception adds something beyond knowledge, as the blind person has full knowledge but lacks perception.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what does the blind person example demonstrate?
A) The blind person has no knowledge at all
B) Perception is additional to knowledge
C) Knowledge and perception are identical
D) The blind person lacks all knowledge except perception

Key Takeaway
Perception is a distinct genus from knowledge, proven by our internal experience and the blind person example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that a blind person can know everything about an object except seeing it. How does this distinction between knowledge and perception affect the way you value the knowledge you have of Allah through your senses? Are there things you know but have not truly perceived? What does that mean for your faith?
?
The proof shows that perception adds something to knowledge. Have you ever experienced a moment when your perception of a truth (like seeing a beautiful creation) gave you more than just knowing it? How can you seek such perception in your spiritual life?
?
If perception is a separate genus, then how should you prioritize seeking 'ilm (knowledge) versus idrak (perception) in your worship? Could you be content with just knowing without truly perceiving? When has that been a problem for you?

The Difficulty Regarding Conditions for Perception
A difficulty for the proof when conditions are denied

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of perception's distinctness faces a difficulty for those who deny that physical structure is a necessary condition for perception.

Core Idea
Without a structural condition, perception and knowledge could mix, leading to absurd consequences.

Sanusi raises a problem with the previous proof. For those who do not accept that physical structure (binya) is a necessary condition for perception, the proof's conclusion is undermined. They allow that God could create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye, blurring the distinction. This lesson examines that challenge and its absurd implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the difficulty passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the shift from proof to problem: 'however, this is problematic...'
The difficulty is raised for a specific group: those who do not make structure a condition.
2
Understand the alternative scenario
They allow God to create vision (ru'ya) in the heart and knowledge in the eye. This inverts the normal association.
This challenges the assumption that perception is tied to specific senses.
3
Recognize the absurd consequences
If this is possible, then hearing could happen with the eye, seeing with the ear, etc. All senses could be performed by one sense organ.
This shows the necessity of a condition: each perception is assigned to a specific sense by creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال بالقول الثاني استدل بأن العلم بالشيء إذا أضيف إلى علم آخر به، واتحد المتعلق واستوى العلمان في صفات النفس لم يتصور اختلافهما، فلما وجدنا في أنفسنا اختلافا بين العلم والإدراك، وأحسسنا تفرقة بين المعلوم خبرا وبين المرئي مع اتحاد المتعلق واستواء الأمر في الحدوث، علمنا أن الجنسين مختلفان، وأيضا فإن الأكمه إذا أحاط علما بما أحاط به البصير حصل له كل علم سوى الإدراك، فدل أنه زائد على العلم على أن هذا يشكل بقاعدة من لم يجعل البنية شرطًا، فإنه جوز أن يخلق الله تبارك وتعالى الروية في القلب والعلم في البصر، فحينئذ يلتبس العلم بالإدراك ويلزم أن يسمع السامع بالبصر، ويبصر بالسمع، ويشم بحاسة الذوق، ويذوق بحاسة الشم، ويلزم أن تكون حاسة واحدة هي سامعة مبصرة شامة ذائقة لامسة، وإنما اختص كل إدراك بحاسة خاصة في خلقه.
And whoever holds the second statement argues that knowledge, when added to another knowledge of the same thing, and the object is one, and the two knowledges are equal in attributes of the soul, it is inconceivable that they differ. So when we find in ourselves a difference between knowledge and perception, and we sense a distinction between the known as an object of report and the seen, despite unity of object and equality in temporality, we know that the two genera are different. Also, the blind person when he encompasses knowledge of everything that the sighted person encompasses, he obtains every knowledge except perception. So it proves that (perception) is additional to knowledge. However, this is problematic according to the principle of one who does not make structure a condition, for he allows that God, blessed and exalted, may create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye; then knowledge and perception become confused, and it would necessitate that the hearer hears with the eye, sees with hearing, smells with the sense of taste, tastes with the sense of smell, and it would necessitate that one sense is hearing, seeing, smelling, tasting, touching. And each perception is only distinguished by a specific sense in its creation.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the second view that perception is a separate genus from knowledge. The argument: if two instances of knowledge have the same object and are equal in soul's attributes, they cannot differ. But we experience a difference between knowing something by report and perceiving it directly (e.g., seeing). Also, a blind person (akmah) can know everything a sighted person knows except the visual perception itself, showing perception is additional to knowledge. However, this proof faces a difficulty for those who deny that physical structure (binya) is a necessary condition for perception. They allow that God could create vision (ru'ya) in the heart and knowledge in the eye, blurring the distinction. This would lead to absurd consequences: mixing of senses, so that one sense could perform all functions. The author concludes that each perception is assigned to a specific sense by God's creation, preserving distinction.

Key Terms
روية (ru'ya - vision)
قلب (qalb - heart)
بنية (binya - structure)
حاسة (ḥāssa - sense)
بصير (baṣīr - sighted person)
سمع (samʿ - hearing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof is absolutely conclusive and faces no counterarguments.
Correction: The author himself points out a difficulty based on the denial of structural conditions, showing the proof is not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author mention if one denies structure as a condition?
A) The blind person could see
B) Hearing could occur with the eye
C) Knowledge would be impossible
D) Perception would be identical to knowledge

Key Takeaway
The proof of perception's distinctness depends on the assumption that perception requires specific physical structure; without it, the distinction collapses and absurdities arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your physical senses are the only reliable means of perceiving truth? How does the possibility that God could create perception anywhere challenge the certainty you place in your own senses?
?
If the distinctions between knowledge and perception can collapse without a proper structure, what does that reveal about the order and wisdom in God's creation? How should this order affect your trust in the natural means He has given you?
?
When you seek to know God, do you rely more on sensory experiences (e.g., seeing signs) or on rational proofs? This lesson warns that mixing perceptions leads to absurdity—are you guilty of mixing different ways of knowing in a way that confuses your faith?

The Ash'ari View: Perception as a Distinct Attribute
Ash'ari doctrine: perception is a distinct meaning, God has two perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that perception (idrak) is a distinct attribute from knowledge (ilm), and that God is Hearing and Seeing by two perceptions that are specific sciences.

Core Idea
Perception is a distinct meaning, not identical to knowledge.

This passage clarifies the Ash'ari stance on the nature of perception as a divine attribute. It distinguishes perception from knowledge, explains its relationship to the non-existent, and warns against confusing it with the view of al-Ka'bi. The passage then affirms that God’s attributes of Hearing and Seeing are two perceptions that are specific sciences beyond His knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: idrak, ilm, ma'na, sami', basir, ma'dum.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the distinction between knowledge and perception
The passage states that knowledge relates to the non-existent, but perception does not because the non-existent is not determinate. This is a key difference.
3
Contrast al-Ash'ari with al-Ka'bi
Al-Ash'ari affirms that perception has a distinct meaning (ma'na) and is of the genus of sciences. al-Ka'bi denied any meaning for perception. Understand the difference.
This protects against a common misunderstanding.
4
Apply to divine attributes
Based on this, God is Hearing and Seeing by two perceptions, which are two specific sciences beyond His being Knowing. Reflect on how this preserves the distinction of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدرك فلا جرم بقول العلم يتعلق بالمعدوم والإدراك لا يتعلق به إذ المعدوم لا يتعين ولا تظنن أن هذا الرأي هو مذهب الكعبي فإنه لم يثبت لإدراك معنى أصلا والأشعري أثبت له معنى وقال هو من جنس العلوم فعلى هذا القول الرب تعالى سميع بصير بإدراكين هما علمان مخصوصان وراء كونه عالما هو مدلول الإحكام والإتقان.
Thus the percept is not; it is correct that knowledge relates to the non-existent and perception does not relate to it, because the non-existent is not determined. And do not think that this view is the doctrine of al-Ka'bi, for he did not establish a meaning for perception at all, while al-Ash'ari established a meaning for it and said it is of the genus of sciences. So according to this statement, the Lord Almighty is Hearing, Seeing with two perceptions, which are two specific sciences beyond His being Knowing, which is indicated by the perfection and precision (of creation).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on the nature of perception (idrak) and its relation to knowledge (ilm) in the context of divine attributes. The author begins by stating a general principle: knowledge relates to the non-existent (ma'dum) while perception does not, because the non-existent is not determinate. He then warns against confusing this view with that of al-Ka'bi, who denied any additional meaning for perception. Al-Ash'ari, by contrast, affirmed that perception is a distinct meaning (ma'na) and classified it as a type of science (ilm). Based on this, the author concludes that God Almighty is Hearing and Seeing by virtue of two perceptions, which are two specific sciences (ilman makhsusan) beyond His being Knowing (alim), the latter being indicated by the perfection and precision of creation (ihkam wa itqan). Thus, perception is an additional attribute distinct from knowledge.

Key Terms
إدراك
علم
معنى
سميع
بصير
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Ash'ari equates perception with knowledge or denies it as a distinct attribute.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly affirms perception as a distinct meaning (ma'na) and classifies it as a type of science, different from al-Ka'bi who denied any meaning.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relation between perception (idrak) and knowledge (ilm) in the Ash'ari view?
A) Perception is identical to knowledge.
B) Perception is a distinct meaning (ma'na) of the genus of sciences.
C) Perception is a type of non-existence.
D) Perception is only for created beings.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari affirms that perception is a distinct attribute of the genus of sciences, and God is Hearing and Seeing by two perceptions that are specific sciences beyond His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah hears and sees me,' do you truly believe that His perception is an additional, specific science beyond His general knowledge? How would your actions change if you internalized that He perceives every detail of your life with a distinct attribute?
?
The passage warns against confusing al-Ash'ari's view with a simpler one. Have you ever settled for a vague, comfortable understanding of God's attributes to avoid deeper reflection? What would it cost you to dig into the precise meanings of His names?
?
If perception relates only to what is determined, and God's knowledge encompasses even the non-determined, how does this challenge your own certainty about what is real? In what area of your life are you living as if something is 'non-determined' when God has already determined it?

Proof that Perception is Distinct from Knowledge
Proof from experience and the blind person that perception differs from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs showing that perception (idrak) is not identical to knowledge ('ilm) but an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Perception is not mere knowledge; blind people disprove that.

This passage presents two arguments for the view that perception is a distinct genus from knowledge. The first argument relies on our inner experience of a difference between knowing and perceiving, even when the object is the same. The second argument uses the example of a blind person who can have full knowledge but lacks perception, showing that perception is additional. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the logical flow: first a general principle, then an experiential observation, then a concrete example.
2
Understand the first argument
The argument states that if two knowledges have the same object and are equal in psychological attributes, they cannot differ. Yet we find a clear difference between knowledge and perception in ourselves. Therefore they are different genera.
The key premise is that identical knowledges cannot be conceived as different.
3
Understand the second argument
The blind person (akmah) can know everything about an object that a sighted person knows, but lacks perception (vision). This shows perception is something additional to knowledge.
This is a concrete illustration of the general principle.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments converge: perception is not reducible to knowledge; it is a distinct attribute (za'id) that may be present or absent independently.
This has implications for divine attributes—Allah's knowledge and perception are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال بالقول الثاني استدل بأن العلم بالشيء إذا أضيف إلى علم آخر به واتحد المتعلق واستوى العلمان في صفات النفس لم يتصور اختلافهما فلما وجدنا في أنفسنا اختلافا بين العلم والإدراك وأحسسنا تفرقة بين المعلوم خبرا وبين المرئي مع اتحاد المتعلق واستواء الأمر في الحدوث علمنا أن الجنسين مختلفان وأيضا فإن الأكمه أو أحاط علما بما أحاط به البصير حصل له كل علم سوى الإدراك فدل أنه زائد على العلم.
And one who adopts the second position argued that knowledge of a thing, when added to another knowledge of it, while the object is one and the two knowledges are equal in the attributes of the soul, their difference cannot be conceived. Since we find in ourselves a difference between knowledge and perception, and we sense a distinction between what is known by report and what is seen, despite the unity of the object and the equality in terms of contingency, we know that the two genera are different. Also, the blind person, if he comprehends in knowledge everything that the sighted person comprehends, obtains all knowledge except perception, which indicates that it is additional to knowledge.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for those who hold the second position (that perception is distinct from knowledge). The argument proceeds in two steps. First, it notes that if two knowledges have the same object and are equal in psychological attributes, they cannot be conceived as different. Yet we find in ourselves a clear distinction between knowing something (e.g., by report) and perceiving it (e.g., seeing it), even when the object is the same and both are contingent. This shows that knowledge and perception belong to different genera. Second, the example of the blind person (akmah) is given: a blind person can attain all the knowledge that a sighted person has about an object, but lacks the perception (sight). Thus, perception is something additional (za'id) to knowledge, not identical to it.

Key Terms
علم
إدراك
الأكمه
بصير
زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is simply a type of knowledge.
Correction: The proof shows that perception and knowledge are generically different; perception is an additional attribute that can be absent while knowledge remains.

Checkpoint
What example does the argument use to show that perception is additional to knowledge?
A) A person with full knowledge but lacking perception.
B) A person who perceives without knowledge.
C) Two knowledges of different objects.
D) The difference between hearing and seeing.

Key Takeaway
Perception is an attribute additional to knowledge, not reducible to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the realities of faith but do not perceive them in your heart, are you content with mere knowledge? What will you do today to move from knowledge to perception?
?
The blind person has all knowledge except sight. Do you treat your perception of Allah's signs as a gift or a given? How can you awaken your inner perception?
?
Do you live as if knowledge alone is sufficient, neglecting the perception that leads to certainty? What step can you take this week to truly perceive what you already know?

Objection on Reversing Senses and the Response with Unseen Beings
Objection and response about possible reversal of senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God could reverse sensory functions is refuted by appealing to the habitual order of creation and the examples of unseen beings (angels, jinn, Iblis).

Core Idea
Sensory functions are fixed by divine habit, and unseen beings show that perception is not just knowledge.

This lesson presents an objection against a previous argument that relied on the specificity of senses. The objector suggests that God could create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye, blurring the distinction. The response, however, affirms that the actual course of creation assigns each perception to a specific sense, and further demonstrates through the examples of angels and jinn that the absence of perception does not indicate non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection: if physical structure is not a condition, God could create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye, leading to confusion.
2
Identify the absurd consequences
Note the list of absurdities: hearing with the eye, seeing with hearing, etc. Recognise that the objector uses these to argue against the specificity of senses.
The enumeration shows the logical extreme of the premise.
3
Examine the response: habit and unseen examples
The response first invokes the 'prevailing habit' (al-'ada al-jariya) that each sense is specific. Then it provides examples: angels unseen at the Prophet's gathering, devils and jinn roaming unseen, and Iblis seeing us while we do not see him. These examples show that lack of perception does not mean non-existence, refuting the objection's force.
The examples are from the unseen world (ghayb), demonstrating that sensory limitations do not imply absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذا الدليل يشكل بقاعدة من لم يجعل البنية شرطًا فإنه جوز أن يخلق الله تبارك وتعالى الروية في القلب والعلم في البصر فحينئذ يلتبس العلم بالإدراك ويلزم أن يسمع السامع بالبصر ويبصر بالسمع ويشم بحاسة الذوق ويذوق بحاسة الشم ويلزم أن تكون حاسة واحدة هي سامعة مبصرة شامة ذائقة لامسة وإنما اختص كل إدراك بحاسة خاصة في خلقه. وكذلك يجوز أن تكون أمور حاضرة لا نحسها وأمور غائبة نبصرها ولكن العادة الجارية أمنتنا من ذلك أليست الملائكة كانت تحضر مجلس النبوة والقوم حضور والأعين سليمة وهم لا يبصرون أليست الشياطين والجن يطوفون في البلاد ويسرحون في الأرض وإن إبليس هو يرانا وقبيله من حيث لا نراه فبطلت تلك التهويلات والتشنيعات التي ألزمها الخصم.
However, this argument is problematic based on the principle of one who does not make physical structure a condition, for he allows that God Almighty may create vision in the heart and knowledge in the eye; then knowledge would be confused with perception, and it would be necessary that the hearer hears with the eye, sees with hearing, smells with the sense of taste, tastes with the sense of smell, and it would be necessary that one sense be hearing, seeing, smelling, tasting, touching, whereas each perception is specifically attached to a particular sense in its creation. Similarly, it is possible that there are present things we do not sense and absent things we see, but the prevailing habit has made us safe from that. Did not the angels attend the assembly of prophethood while the people were present with sound eyes yet did not see them? Did not the devils and jinn roam the lands and travel the earth, and indeed Iblis sees us and his tribe from where we do not see him? Thus those intimidations and denunciations that the opponent presented are refuted.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the previous proof and then provides a response. The objection is based on the position of one who does not make the physical structure (al-binya) a condition for perception. Such a person allows that God could create vision (al-ru'ya? possibly al-ru'ya) in the heart and knowledge in the eye, thus confusing knowledge and perception. This would lead to absurdities like hearing with the eye, seeing with hearing, etc., implying one sense could do all functions, contrary to the established specificity of each sense. The response, however, counters that such a possibility does not undermine the distinction because even if God could reverse functions, the actual habitual course (al-'ada al-jariya) assigns each perception to a specific sense. Moreover, the objection itself is refuted by examples from the unseen: angels were present at the gathering of prophethood but people did not see them; devils and jinn roam around but are not seen; Iblis sees us while we do not see him. These examples show that lack of perception does not imply non-existence, and the claimed absurdities (tahwilat wa tashni'at) are invalid.

Key Terms
بنية
حاسة
ملائكة
شياطين
جن
إبليس
عادة جارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If senses could be reversed, then perception and knowledge are identical, and the distinction between them collapses.
Correction: The response shows that perception is tied to specific senses by the habitual order of creation, and the examples of unseen beings confirm that perception is not simply knowledge—perception involves a specific sensory faculty, whereas knowledge can exist without sensory input.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, what empirical or theological evidence is used to refute the objection about reversing senses?
A) The fact that people who are blind still have knowledge.
B) The habit that each sense has a specific function and the examples of angels and jinn.
C) The impossibility of creation by God.
D) The argument from divine simplicity.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God could reverse senses fails because the habitual order of creation assigns specific functions to each sense, and unseen beings confirm that perception is not simply knowledge.

?
The objection in this lesson imagines a scenario where God could have reversed your senses, but He did not. Your ability to perceive the world is not random but deliberately structured by His wisdom. If you truly believed that your sight and hearing are gifts from a wise and purposeful Creator, how would you treat your eyes and ears differently in the next hour? Commit to one concrete action before you close this lesson.

Refutation of al-Jubba'i's Definition of Hearing and Seeing
Refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition that 'hearing' and 'seeing' mean 'alive without defect'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of hearing and seeing cannot be defined merely as the absence of defects but are positive attributes.

Core Idea
Hearing and seeing are positive realities, not just the absence of defects.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, defined divine attributes like hearing and seeing in a way that reduces them to mere negations. The author refutes this by showing that a negation alone cannot distinguish between perception and its absence. This passage exposes the logical flaw in defining positive attributes by negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the tight argumentative structure.
Pay attention to how the author builds the refutation step by step.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's definition
Al-Jubba'i defines 'hearing' and 'seeing' as 'the living being who has no defect'. Understand this as a purely negative definition.
3
Follow the refutation argument
The author points out that an absolute negation of defect is insufficient because one can have defects and still perceive. The definition must specify the locus and that the defect prevents perception, which implicitly affirms perception as a positive state.
Notice the logical move: negating a negator (defect) does not give a positive content.
4
Reflect on the positive nature of perception
The author concludes that perception is a positive meaning (ma'na mawjud) that one senses, and a pure negation cannot be sensed. Thus, a distinction based on pure non-existence collapses.
This is the key takeaway: positive attributes require positive definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جواب الجبائي عن القاعدة التي صار إليها من حد السميع أو البصير أنه الحي الذي لا آفة به قيل أطلقت القول بنفي الآفة وذلك بالاتفاق ليس بشرط فإن السميع والبصير قد يكون ذا آفة وذا آفات كثيرة فلا بد وأن يخص نفي الآفة بمحل الإدراك ثم تلك الآفة يجب أن تكون مانعة من الإدراك فقد أثبت الإدراك من حيث نفاه ثم الذي يحسه الإنسان من نفسه معنى موجود لا نفي محض وقولهم لا آفة به نفي محض فلا يتصور الإحساس به ويستحيل أن ترجع التفرقة بين حالتي الإدراك وعدم الإدراك إلى عدم محض فحينئذ تنعدم التفرقة فإن التفرقة بالعدم وعدم التفرقة سواء.
As for the response of al-Jubba'i regarding the principle he adopted, that the definition of the Hearing or Seeing is 'the living being who has no defect,' it was said: you made an absolute statement negating defect, and that by consensus is not a condition, for the Hearing and Seeing may be with a defect and with many defects. So it is necessary to specify the negation of defect to the locus of perception, and then that defect must be a preventer of perception. Thus you have affirmed perception from where you negated it. Then what a person senses from himself is an existing meaning, not a pure negation. And their statement 'no defect in it' is a pure negation, so sensation of it cannot be conceived, and it is impossible for the distinction between the states of perception and non-perception to be reduced to a pure negation, for then the distinction would be eliminated, because the distinction by non-existence and the absence of distinction are equal.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Jubba'i's definition of 'Hearing' (sami') and 'Seeing' (basir) as 'the living being who has no defect' (al-hayy alladhi la afa bihi). The refutation points out that the negation of defect is not a sufficient condition because a person may be 'Hearing' or 'Seeing' despite having defects (afa), as long as the defect does not prevent perception. Therefore, the definition must specify that the defect is in the locus of perception (mahall al-idrak) and that it prevents perception. But by stating that, al-Jubba'i implicitly affirms perception as something positive (ma'na mawjud) even while negating defect. The author argues that what a person senses from himself is a positive meaning, not a mere negation. Moreover, if 'no defect' were a pure negation (nafy mahd), it could not be sensed, and the distinction between perception and non-perception would collapse, because a distinction based on pure non-existence is no distinction at all. Thus, the definition fails.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
آفة
نفي
إحساس
محل الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attributes of hearing and seeing are merely the absence of defects or imperfections.
Correction: The passage shows that perception is a positive attribute (ma'na mawjud) that one senses, and cannot be reduced to a negation. Defining it as a pure negation fails to capture its reality and leads to logical collapse.

Checkpoint

A) It includes too many conditions.
B) It reduces perception to a pure negation and fails to account for the positive sensation of perception.
C) It confuses hearing with seeing.
D) It denies the existence of defects.

Key Takeaway
Defining hearing and seeing as mere absence of defects fails because perception is a positive reality that cannot be reduced to a negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'Allah is Hearing,' do you mentally default to 'He is not deaf' rather than affirming a positive, active quality? You just learned that perception is an existing meaning, not a mere absence of defect. Al-Jubba'i's definition failed because it reduced a positive attribute to a negation. Refuting the Reduction of Hearing and Seeing to 'Living Without Defect'
Refutes al-Jubba'i's definition of hearing and seeing as 'living without defect'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's definition of hearing and seeing as 'living without defect' fails, because it reduces a positive attribute to a mere negation and cannot account for the distinction between different perceptions.

Core Idea
Negative definitions cannot explain the real difference between hearing and seeing.

Al-Jubba'i, a Mu'tazilite theologian, defined hearing and seeing simply as 'being alive without any defect'. But can a mere absence of defect really distinguish between two distinct perceptions? The author of Nihayat al-Aqdam exposes the flaw in this reduction, showing that a pure negation cannot capture the positive reality of perception or differentiate hearing from seeing. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Jubba'i's claim
Read the passage to identify al-Jubba'i's definition: 'hearing' and 'seeing' mean 'the living being without defect'.
Notice that he reduces attributes to a single negation.
2
First criticism: absolute negation not a condition
The author argues that negating defect absolutely is not a condition; a being with defects can still hear or see. So negation must be specified to the locus of perception.
3
Core argument: pure negation cannot be sensed or distinguish
The author points out that 'without defect' is a pure negation, but perception is a positive, sensed meaning. A pure negation cannot account for the necessary distinction between hearing and seeing, which are experienced as distinct.
This is the central logical flaw.
4
Extension to all attributes
If al-Jubba'i's logic is applied to all attributes (knowledge, power), they would all reduce to 'living without defect', making them indistinguishable. This shows the definition is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جواب الجبائي عن القاعدة التي صار إليها من حد السميع أو البصير أنه الحي الذي لا آفة به، قيل: أطلقت القول بنفي الآفة وذلك بالاتفاق ليس بشرط، فإن السميع والبصير قد يكون ذا آفة وذا آفات كثيرة، فلا بد وأن يخص نفي الآفة بمحل الإدراك، ثم تلك الآفة يجب أن تكون مانعة من الإدراك، فقد أثبت الإدراك من حيث نفاه ثم الذي يحسه الإنسان من نفسه معنى موجود لا نفي محض. وقولهم: لا آفة به نفي محض فلا يتصور الإحساس به، ويستحيل أن ترجع التفرقة بين حالتي الإدراك وعدم الإدراك إلى عدم محض، فحينئذ تنعدم التفرقة، فإن التفرقة بالعدم وعدم التفرقة سوي. ثم نقول: نحن نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين كون الإنسان سميعا وبين كونه بصيرا، وهما متفقان في أن معنى كل واحد منهما أنه حي لا آفة به، فهذه التفرقة ترجع إلى ماذا؟ فلا بد من أمرين زائدين على كونه حيا لا آفة به حتى يكون بأحدهما سميعا وبالثاني بصيرا، وإلا فتبطل التفرقة الضرورية، فالذي انفصل به السمع عن البصر وهو وراء كونه حيا لا آفة به فكذلك الذي انفصل به السمع والبصر عن العلم وسائر الصفات وراء كونه حيا لا آفة به، ولئن ألزم الجبائي بأن يقال: معنى كونه عالما قادرا أنه لا آفة به، حتى يرد الصفات كلها إلى كونه حيا لا آفة به لم يجد هذا الإلزام مخلصا.
As for al-Jubba'i's answer regarding the principle he adopted, that the definition of 'hearing' or 'seeing' is 'the living being without defect', it is said: You have made the statement of negating defect absolute, and that by consensus is not a condition, for the hearing and seeing one may have a defect and many defects, so it is necessary that the negation of defect be specified to the locus of perception. Then that defect must be a prevention of perception, so you have affirmed perception while negating it. Then what a person senses from himself is an existing meaning, not a pure negation. And their saying 'without defect' is a pure negation, so sensation of it cannot be conceived, and it is impossible that the distinction between the states of perception and non-perception be reduced to a pure non-existence; then the distinction would cease, for distinction by non-existence and non-distinction are equal. Then we say: We find a necessary distinction between a person being hearing and being seeing, and they both agree that the meaning of each is that he is living without defect, so to what does this distinction reduce? So there must be two additional things beyond his being living without defect, so that by one he is hearing and by the second seeing; otherwise the necessary distinction would be invalidated. That by which hearing is separated from sight is beyond being living without defect, and likewise that by which hearing and sight are separated from knowledge and the rest of the attributes is beyond being living without defect. And if al-Jubba'i is compelled to say that the meaning of his being knowing and powerful is that he is without defect, so that all attributes reduce to his being living without defect, he will not find an escape from this compulsion.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of hearing and seeing. It begins by stating al-Jubba'i's position: hearing/seeing mean 'living without defect'. The reply first points out that negating defect absolutely is not a condition; a being with defects can still hear/see, so the negation must be specified to the locus of perception. Then it argues that if 'without defect' is pure negation, it cannot account for the sensation of perception, which is an actual positive meaning. The author then presents a key argument: we find a necessary distinction between hearing and seeing, yet they share the definition of 'living without defect'. Therefore, there must be additional attributes beyond that shared meaning to account for the difference. This reasoning is extended to all attributes: if the opponent tries to reduce knowledge and power to 'living without defect', he will be unable to escape the same problem of distinguishing them.

The Necessary Distinction Between Hearing and Seeing Proves Multiple Attributes
Argument from necessary distinction between hearing and seeing for distinct attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary distinction between hearing and seeing demonstrates that divine attributes are distinct realities beyond the attribute of life.

Core Idea
The necessary distinction between hearing and seeing proves attributes are more than just 'being alive without defect.'

The author places us in a thought experiment. We know intuitively that hearing and seeing are different. Yet both share a common core: the person is alive and functioning. If both reduce to that, they'd be identical. But they aren't. So there must be something extra — a distinct attribute for each. This reasoning forces us to accept that attributes are not mere negations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow: observation, question, answer, extension, and refutation of al-Jubba'i.
Pay attention to the term 'تفرقة ضرورية' (necessary distinction).
2
Identify the core argument
The author notes a necessary distinction between hearing and seeing despite both meaning 'alive without defect.' Ask yourself: why must there be two additional matters?
If the distinction were merely apparent, the argument would collapse.
3
Consider the extension
The same reasoning is extended to hearing and sight versus knowledge and other attributes. See how the argument generalizes.
This shows the principle applies to all attributes.
4
Refute the objection of al-Jubba'i
Al-Jubba'i reduced all attributes to 'alive without defect.' The author claims this compulsion has no escape. Understand why the reduction fails.
The necessary distinction between hearing and seeing disproves the reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : نحن نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين كون الإنسان سميعا وبين كونه بصيرا ، وهما متفقان في أن معنى كل واحد منهما أنه حي لا آفة به، فهذه التفرقة ترجع إلى ماذا ؟ فلا بد من أمرين زائدين على كونه حيًّا لا آفة به حتى يكون بأحدهما سميعا وبالثاني بصيراً، وإلا فتبطل التفرقة الضرورية، فالذي انفصل به السمع عن البصر وهو وراء كونه حيًّا لا آفة به فكذلك الذي انفصل به السمع والبصر عن العلم وسائر الصفات وراء كونه حيا لا آفة به، ولئن ألزم الجبائي بأن يُقال : معنى كونه عالما قادرًا أنه لا آفة به ، حتى يرد الصفات كلها إلى كونه حيا لا آفة به لم يجد هذا الإلزام مخلصاً.
Then we say: We find a necessary distinction between a human being hearing and being seeing, and they agree that the meaning of each one of them is that he is alive without defect. So to what does this distinction refer? There must be two additional matters beyond his being alive without defect so that by one he is hearing and by the second he is seeing; otherwise the necessary distinction becomes invalid. So that by which hearing is separated from sight, being beyond his being alive without defect, likewise that by which hearing and sight are separated from knowledge and the rest of the attributes is beyond his being alive without defect. And if al-Jubba'i is compelled by the statement: 'The meaning of his being knowing and powerful is that he is without defect,' so that all attributes are reduced to his being alive without defect, he will find no escape from this compulsion.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a proof from necessary distinction between two attributes (hearing and seeing) to establish that attributes are additional to the essence. The author begins by noting that we find a necessary distinction between a person being hearing and being seeing. Both share the common meaning that the person is 'alive without defect.' But if that were the only meaning, there would be no distinction between hearing and seeing. Therefore, there must be two additional realities (أمرين زائدين) beyond mere life, so that one makes him hearing and the other makes him seeing. Otherwise, the necessary distinction we perceive would be invalid. The same reasoning is then extended: just as hearing and sight are distinguished from each other by something beyond life, so too hearing and sight are distinguished from knowledge and other attributes by something beyond life. The author then considers an objection from al-Jubba'i, who argued that the meaning of 'knowing' and 'powerful' is simply 'without defect,' thereby reducing all attributes to life without defect. The author claims this compulsion has no escape, implying that the necessary distinction between attributes forces the admission that attributes are distinct realities.

أمرين زائدين
الجبائي
صفات

Misconception: The attributes like hearing and seeing are just ways of saying that God is alive without defect.
Correction: If that were the case, hearing and seeing would be identical, but we find a necessary distinction between them. Therefore, attributes must be distinct realities beyond mere life.

Key Takeaway
The necessary distinction between hearing and seeing proves that divine attributes are additional realities beyond the attribute of life.

Refutation of Bodily Structure as a Condition for Perception
Refutation of the condition of bodily structure for perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument against the claim that perception requires a bodily structure, and why such a condition cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Perception does not require a composite body; it can subsist in a simple substance.

Some rationalists argued that perception requires a bodily structure (al-binya) similar to how life requires it. In this passage, the author refutes this by analyzing the nature of perception and atoms. He shows that perception can subsist in a single atom, and that the condition of bodily structure is neither necessary nor consistent when applied to the unseen (al-gha'ib).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the claim being made by some rationalists and the author's response.
Notice that the passage starts by presenting the view and then immediately refutes the condition of bodily structure.
2
Understand the atomistic argument
The author argues that perception subsists in one part of the whole (a single atom), not requiring the entire composite. Surrounding atoms have no effect because each atom is independent.
Key phrase: 'الإدراك الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد من الجملة'
3
See the application to God
If bodily structure were a necessary condition like life, it would have to be consistently applied to the unseen (God), implying God has a body. This is absurd.
The author uses the principle: 'الشروط يجب طردها شاهدًا وغائبًا' (conditions must be applied uniformly).
4
Reflect on the key conclusion
The refutation shows that perception is intrinsic to the atom and does not require combination or relation. Therefore, God's perfections do not require a bodily structure.
Perception is not a relational accident (عرض إضافي).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال بعض العقلاء: وقرر أن جميع صفات الكمال تجتمع في كونه حيا، وتنتفي صفات النقص بقولنا : لا آفة به، وأما اشتراطهم البنية حسب اشتراط الحياة غير صحيح، فإن الإدراك الواحد لا يقوم إلا بجزء واحد من الجملة، وإذا قام به أفاد حكما له، ولا أثر للجواهر المحيطة، فإن كل جوهر مختص بحيزه موصوف بأعراضه، وكما لا يؤثر جوهر في جوهر لا يؤثر حكم جوهر في جوهر آخر، ولا يختلف حكم جوهر بعينه في تفرده وانضمامه، وإذا جاز قيام الإدراك به مع اتصاله بالجواهر جاز قيامه به مع تفرده، فإنه لا يتحول عن صفته تفرد أو انضم إلى غيره، خصوصاً إذا كان الإدراك في صفة نفسه لا يقتضي جمعاً وإضافة ونسبة ولم يكن من الأعراض الإضافية بخلاف الاجتماع والافتراق والمماسة، ثم لو كانت البنية شرطًا كاشتراط كونه حيًا لوجب درده في الغائب، حتى يكون الباري تعالى في علمه وقدرته ذا بنية كما كان حيًّا ؛ لأن الشروط يجب طردها شاهدًا وغائبًا.
And some rationalists have said and asserted that all perfect attributes are encompassed in His being alive, and defects are negated by our statement: 'without defect.' As for their condition of bodily structure (al-binya) like the condition of life, it is not correct. For a single perception subsists only in one part of the whole, and if it subsists in it, it gives a ruling for it, and the surrounding atoms have no effect. For each atom is specific to its place and described by its accidents, and just as one atom does not affect another, the ruling of one atom does not affect another atom. The ruling of a particular atom does not differ whether it is alone or joined. And if it is possible for perception to subsist in it while it is connected with atoms, then it is possible for it to subsist in it while it is alone, for it does not change from its attribute whether alone or joined to another, especially since perception in its own nature does not require combination, addition, or relation, and is not among the relational accidents, unlike conjunction, separation, and contact. Then if bodily structure were a condition like the condition of being alive, it would have to be applied to the unseen, so that the Most High Creator would have a bodily structure in His knowledge and power just as He is alive, because conditions must be applied consistently to the witnessed and the unseen.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two related ideas: (1) that all perfect attributes are reducible to life, and (2) that perception requires a bodily structure (بنية). The author first cites the view of some rationalists who claimed that all perfections are combined in being alive, and defects negated by 'without defect.' Then he turns to their condition of bodily structure for perception, arguing it is false. The proof proceeds by examining the nature of perception and atoms. Perception, he argues, subsists in one indivisible part (جزء واحد) of the whole, not requiring the entire composite. Surrounding atoms have no effect because each atom is independent and its accidents are specific to it. If perception can occur in an atom when connected, it can occur when alone, since the atom's ruling does not change whether separate or joined. Perception is not a relational accident that requires combination; it is intrinsic. Therefore, the condition of bodily structure is unnecessary. Moreover, if bodily structure were a necessary condition like life, then it would have to be applied to God (الغائب), making Him have a body, which is absurd. Conditions must be applied uniformly to the witnessed and the unseen. Thus the condition is refuted.

Key Terms
بنية
جوهر
جزء واحد
أعراض إضافية
شاهد وغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception requires a bodily structure or physical organ.
Correction: The passage shows that perception can subsist in a single atom without needing a composite body, and that such a condition would incorrectly imply God has a body.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the condition of bodily structure (al-binya) for perception?
A) Because bodily structure is only needed for compound bodies
B) Because perception can subsist in a single atom and does not require a composite
C) Because the author denies perception to God
D) Because bodily structure is a condition in the witnessed but not in the unseen

Key Takeaway
Bodily structure is not a necessary condition for perception; perception can subsist in a simple substance, and such a condition cannot be applied to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If perception does not require a physical organ, what does this mean for your reliance on your physical senses in matters of faith? Are you prioritizing spiritual perception over physical evidence in your relationship with Allah?
?
This lesson argues that conditions must be consistent between the witnessed and unseen. Are you applying the same standards of justice and mercy you expect from Allah in your own treatment of others?
?
The atom does not change its attribute whether alone or joined. How does your own identity and character remain consistent whether you are alone or with others? Does your faith change based on your company?

Refutation of the Ray Theory of Vision
Refutation of the theory of vision via rays emitted from the eye

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theory that vision occurs via rays emitted from the eye is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
The eye cannot contain the vast bodies required for ray emission.

This passage critiques a common ancient theory of vision, showing its internal contradiction. The author presents the ray theory in detail before delivering a concise refutation based on physical impossibility. We'll explore both the theory's conditions and its decisive rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's astonishment at the theory's assumptions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the ray theory conditions
Identify the conditions for vision according to the ray theory: connection of rays, straightness, proper distance, no excessive distance or proximity.
The theory explains why very distant objects and the inside of eyelids are not seen.
3
Identify the two false opinions
The author declares both the emission theory (rays exiting the eye) and the intromission theory (forms entering the eye) as false.
4
Grasp the refutation's argument
Focus on the final sentence: the eye is too small to contain bodies that would spread over half the celestial sphere. This is the key physical impossibility.
This is a clear logical refutation based on size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أنهم كما شرطوا البنية شرطوا في الرؤية اتصال الأشعة وهي أجسام مضيئة تنبعث عن البصر عند فتح الأجفان، وهي أجسام تتشكل وتتحرك وتتعوج وتستقيم، فإذا اتصل الشعاع المنبعث من الناظر بالقاعدة على حد معلوم مع الاستقامة ولم يكن ثم بعد مفرط ولا قرب مفرط أدركه الرائي ، فلذلك لا يرى الشيء البعيد على غاية البعد ، ولا يرى باطن الأجفان، وإن كانت القاعدة صقيلة ارتدت الأشعة إلى الرائي ، فرأى عند ذلك نفسه، وربما يظن أنه ينفصل عن المتلون صورة وتمتد إلى العين فينطبع فيها ، وكلا المذهبين باطل. أما القول بالأشعة التي تنبعث من العين فبطلانه بأنا نعلم قطعا أن العين على صغرها لا تتسع لأجسام تنبسط على نصف كرة العالم.
And it is strange that just as they conditioned bodily structure, they conditioned in vision the connection of rays—which are luminous bodies emitted from the eye when the eyelids open, and they are bodies that take shape, move, bend, and straighten. So when the ray emitted from the observer connects with the object on a known limit with straightness, and there is no excessive distance or excessive proximity, the seer perceives it. For that reason, he does not see a very distant thing, nor does he see the inside of the eyelids. And if the object is polished, the rays reflect back to the observer, and he then sees himself. And perhaps one thinks that a form detaches from the colored object and extends to the eye and becomes imprinted in it—both opinions are false. As for the claim of rays that are emitted from the eye, its falsity is that we know with certainty that the eye, despite its smallness, cannot contain bodies that spread over half the celestial sphere.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ray theory (theory of emission) of vision. The author begins by expressing astonishment that those who condition bodily structure for perception also condition vision upon the connection of rays (اتصال الأشعة). He describes the theory: rays are luminous bodies emitted from the eye upon opening the eyelids; they can take shape, move, bend, and straighten. When the emitted ray from the observer connects with the object (القاعدة) under specific conditions—on a known limit, with straightness, and without excessive distance or proximity—the seer perceives it. This explains why very distant things are not seen and why the inside of the eyelids is not seen. If the object is polished, the rays reflect back, and the observer sees himself. The author then mentions an alternative theory (intromission) that a form detaches from the object and enters the eye. He declares both opinions false. Then he specifically refutes the emission theory by arguing that the eye, despite its small size, cannot contain bodies that spread over half the celestial sphere, as would be required for vision of the heavens. This is a decisive refutation based on physical impossibility.

Key Terms

اتصال الشعاع
بعد مفرط
قرب مفرط
القاعدة الصقيلة
المذهبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision occurs by rays exiting the eye and extending to the object.
Correction: The eye cannot contain the vast bodies required to emit rays that spread over half the celestial sphere.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the ray theory of vision false?
A) Because rays cannot bend or straighten
B) Because the eye cannot contain bodies that spread over half the celestial sphere
C) Because we cannot see the inside of our eyelids
D) Because the author prefers the intromission theory

Key Takeaway
The ray theory of vision is false because the eye cannot physically contain the bodies needed to emit rays across large distances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your eyes every moment. This lesson shows that a seemingly plausible theory about vision collapses because of a simple physical impossibility. When in your life have you accepted an idea that 'sounds right' without checking if it is even possible? What is one assumption you hold today that you need to test against physical or logical reality? Write down one step you will take this week to challenge an assumption you have never questioned.
?
The author argues that the eye cannot contain bodies spread over half the sky. This is a definitive refutation. How often do you cling to beliefs or habits despite clear evidence that they are impossible or harmful? What is one thing you know is impossible for you to continue doing, yet you still do? Resolve today to abandon it, and write how you will replace it with something true and beneficial.
?
The passage contrasts two false theories and declares both false. Sometimes you may think the only options are two flawed views. Have you ever settled for a 'lesser of two evils' in your understanding of Allah's attributes or in your daily choices without seeking the truth that transcends both? Commit to studying the revealed sources—Quran and Sunnah—to find the correct view, rather than accepting false dichotomies.

Why God is Hearing and Seeing but Not Smelling or Tasting
Mutakallimun argue five perceptions differ in reality, allowing God to be Hearing and Seeing only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument for limiting certain sense attributes to God based on the nature of sensory perceptions.

Core Idea
God can be Hearing and Seeing, but not Smelling, Tasting, or Touching.

The mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) often used human sensory experience as a foundation for theological reasoning. In this passage, they argue that the five senses differ in their requirements—some need physical contact, others need spatial orientation—and these differences determine which attributes can be applied to God. Since God is transcendent, only senses free from physical limitations are permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the main claim about which attributes are permissible for God and why.
The claim is that God is Hearing and Seeing, but not Smelling, Tasting, or Touching.
2
Understand the self-evident distinction
The mutakallimun rely on human experience: we know we see with our eyes and hear with our ears, distinct from knowing with our heart. Notice how they use this as evidence.
They contrast the locus of vision (eye) and locus of hearing (ear) with the locus of knowledge (heart).
3
Compare the requirements of each sense
The passage explains that sight requires facing and orientation (for colors/shapes), hearing does not; smell, taste, and touch require contact of bodies. List these differences.
Sight: needs facing. Hearing: no facing. Smell/taste/touch: need contact.
4
Apply to God's transcendence
Since God is not a body and is transcendent, He cannot be described with senses requiring contact or facing. Therefore, only Sami' and Basir are permissible.
This is the core theological conclusion.
5
Consider the caveat on perception vs. knowledge
The author notes that this conclusion holds only if one distinguishes between idrak (perception) and ilm (knowledge). If they are equated, the loci and realities become unified.
This caveat shows the argument's dependence on a specific definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون: الحواس الخمس مشتملة على إدراكات خمس تختلف أنواعها وحقائقها، والإنسان يجد من نفسه أنه يرى ببصره ويسمع بأذنه، كما يجد أنه يعلم بقلبه، فالبصر محل الرؤية، والأذن محل السمع كالقلب هو محل العلم، وكما يجد التفرقة بين العلم والإدراك يجد التفرقة بين محل العلم ومحل الإدراك، ولأن الذي يدرك ببصره من المرئي هو الألوان والأشكال فيستدعي ذلك مقابلة ومواجهة، والذي يدرك بسمعه من المسموع هو الأصوات والحروف ولا يستدعي ذلك مقابلة، وكذلك المشموم والمذوق والملموس يستدعي اتصال الأجسام ولا يستدعيه السمع والبصر، فلذلك يجوز أن يوصف الباري تعالى بأنه سميع بصير، ولا يوصف بأنه شام ذائق لامس، وكذلك لا يجوز أن يسمع الشيء من حيث يبصر، ويبصر من حيث يسمع، فدل ذلك على أن الإدراكات مختلفة الحقائق والمحال جميعا، وهذا كله على رأي من يفرق بين الإدراك والعلم، فأما من قضى بأن الإدراك علم قضى باتحاد المحل والحقيقة.
The mutakallimun said: The five senses contain five perceptions that differ in their types and realities. Man finds in himself that he sees with his eyesight and hears with his ear, just as he finds that he knows with his heart. So the eye is the locus of vision, and the ear is the locus of hearing, just as the heart is the locus of knowledge. And just as he finds the distinction between knowledge and perception, he finds the distinction between the locus of knowledge and the locus of perception. Because that which is perceived by his eyesight from the visible is colors and shapes, which require facing and orientation; that which is perceived by his hearing from the audible is sounds and letters, which do not require facing. Likewise, the smelled, tasted, and felt require contact of bodies, while hearing and sight do not. Therefore it is permissible to describe the Exalted Creator as Hearing and Seeing, and not to describe Him as Smelling, Tasting, or Touching. Likewise, it is not permissible that He should hear a thing from where He sees, and see from where He hears. That indicates that perceptions differ in realities and loci altogether. And all this is according to the one who distinguishes between perception and knowledge. As for the one who judges that perception is knowledge, he judges the unity of locus and reality.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the nature of the five senses and its implications for divine attributes. The argument is based on self-evident experience: a person knows directly that he sees with his eyes and hears with his ears, just as he knows with his heart. Each sense organ has its own locus (mahall) and the perceptions differ in reality. The key distinction is that vision requires facing and orientation (because it perceives colors and shapes from a distance in a certain direction), while hearing does not require facing (sounds can be heard from any direction). Smell, taste, and touch require physical contact of bodies (the object must touch the sense organ). Because God is transcendent and not a body, He cannot be described with senses that require contact or facing. Therefore, it is permissible to describe God as Seeing and Hearing (since they do not require contact), but not as Smelling, Tasting, or Touching. Furthermore, the author notes that this conclusion holds only for those who distinguish between perception (idrak) and knowledge (ilm); if one equates them (i.e., says perception is knowledge), then the locus and reality become one for all perceptions, which would lead to different conclusions. This argument integrates physical observations about the senses with theological principles about God's transcendence.

Key Terms
المتكلمون (mutakallimun)
إدراك (idrak)
علم (ilm)
محل (mahall)
سميع (Sami')
بصير (Basir)
لمس (lams)
ذوق (dhawq)
شم (shamm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be described with all five sense attributes (seeing, hearing, smelling, tasting, touching).
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that only hearing and sight are permissible because they do not require physical contact or spatial orientation, contrary to smell, taste, and touch.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun argue that God cannot be described as smelling (Shamm)?
A) Because smell is a lower sense
B) Because smelling requires contact of bodies
C) Because God has no nose
D) Because smell is not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that only hearing and sight are attributable to Allah because they do not require physical contact or spatial orientation, unlike smell, taste, and touch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah hearing your du'a, do you unconsciously imagine Him 'listening' like a human? How does this passage challenge that mental image, and what will you do to purify your concept of Allah?
?
The mutakallimun argue from what they 'find in themselves'. What do you find in yourself when you reflect on your own perception and knowledge? Does your experience confirm the distinction made here, and how should that affect your confidence in this theological reasoning?
?
This lesson says Allah is not described as 'Tasting' because it requires physical contact. Yet we read in the Qur'an that Allah 'hears' and 'sees'. Is there any quality in your worship that assumes Allah experiences things physically? Repent from that assumption and commit to learning more about His transcendent attributes.

Al-Ash'ari's View on Perception as Specific Knowledge
Al-Ash'ari defines perception as specific knowledge and contrasts it with absolute knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari defines perception as a specific kind of knowledge and how it differs from absolute knowledge in scope and conditions.

Core Idea
Perception is knowledge with conditions; absolute knowledge is unrestricted.

Al-Ash'ari, the founder of the Ash'ari school, takes a nuanced position on the relationship between perception and knowledge. He agrees that all perception is knowledge, but not all knowledge is perception. In this passage, he explains the special characteristics that make perception 'specific knowledge' and how it differs from absolute knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ash'ari's position
Read the first part where he states that every perception is knowledge in one sense, but not every knowledge is perception. Note his middle position.
He does not fully equate the two; perception is a subset.
2
Examine the definition of perception
Notice the two conditions: (1) it requires a specified perceived object, and (2) it relates only to existing things. Existence is the qualifier.
3
Compare with absolute knowledge
See what absolute knowledge can relate to: non-existent, existent, necessary, contingent, and impossible. It has no restrictions.
4
Explore the uncertainty about other senses
Al-Ash'ari affirms hearing and sight as specific knowledge, but wavers on smell, taste, touch. Some followers say they are other kinds of perception.
This shows the theological debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوى أبي الحسن الأشعري فإنه يقول: كل إدراك علم على قول، ولا يقول: كل علم إدراك، بل الإدراك علم مخصوص، فإنه يستدعي تعيين المدرك ويتعلق بالموجود فقط، والوجود هو المصحح بخلاف العلم المطلق فإنه لا يستدعي تعيين المدرك ولا يتعلق بالموجود من حيث هو موجود، بل يتعلق بالمعدوم والموجود والواجب والجائز والمستحيل، لكن من الإدراكات ما هو علم مخصوص كالسمع والبصر وتردد رأيه في سائر الإدراكات أهي علوم مخصوصة أم إدراكات؟ ورأي بعض أصحابه أنها إدراكات أخر، وليس الوجود بمجرده مصححا لها فقط.
Except for Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, for he says: Every perception is knowledge in one sense, but he does not say: every knowledge is perception. Rather, perception is specific knowledge (ilm makhsus). For it requires specifying the perceived object and relates only to the existent; existence is the qualifier (musahhih). In contrast, absolute knowledge does not require specifying the perceived object nor does it relate to the existent insofar as it is existent; rather, it relates to the non-existent, the existent, the necessary, the contingent, and the impossible. However, some perceptions are specific knowledge, like hearing and sight. And his opinion wavered regarding the other perceptions whether they are specific knowledge or perceptions. And the view of some of his followers is that they are other perceptions, and existence alone is not the qualifier for them.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari—the founder of the Ash'ari school—on the relationship between perception (idrak) and knowledge (ilm). Al-Ash'ari takes a middle position: he agrees that every perception is a kind of knowledge ('ilm), but he denies the converse (not every knowledge is perception). He defines perception as 'specific knowledge' ('ilm makhsus) because it has two special conditions: (1) it requires a specified perceived object (ta'yin al-mudrak) and (2) it relates only to existing things (al-mawjud). The condition of existence (al-wujud) is the qualifier (musahhih) for perception. In contrast, absolute knowledge (al-'ilm al-mutlaq) is broader: it does not require a specified object and can relate to non-existent, existent, necessary, contingent, and impossible things. For example, we can know about non-existent things (like the past or future). Among perceptions, al-Ash'ari affirms that hearing and sight are specific knowledge. However, he expressed uncertainty (taraddada ra'yuhu) about the other three senses (smell, taste, touch) whether they are also specific knowledge or mere perceptions. Some of his followers held that they are other perceptions (idrakat ukhra) and that existence alone is not a sufficient qualifier for them (i.e., they may have additional conditions). This shows the nuanced Ash'ari theology regarding the divine attributes: God's hearing and sight are real attributes of knowledge, but His other perceptual attributes are not affirmed.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari)
إدراك (idrak)
علم (ilm)
علم مخصوص (ilm makhsus)
وجود (wujud)
مصحح (musahhih)
تعيين المدرك (ta'yin al-mudrak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari equates perception and knowledge completely.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari makes perception a subset of knowledge: every perception is knowledge but not every knowledge is perception.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what is the primary distinction between perception (idrak) and absolute knowledge (al-ilm al-mutlaq)?
A) Perception relates only to existing things, while absolute knowledge relates to both existent and non-existent
B) Perception is physical, absolute knowledge is spiritual
C) Perception requires a sense organ, absolute knowledge does not
D) Perception is always true, absolute knowledge can be false

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defines perception as 'specific knowledge' that requires specification and existence, while absolute knowledge is unrestricted in scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God hears and sees everything. But is that belief 'specific knowledge' in your heart that shapes your actions, or just general information? What one concrete change could you make today to live as if you are truly seen and heard?
?
Al-Ash'ari says absolute knowledge can relate to non-existent things. What non-existent reality—like your grave or the Day of Judgment—do you know about but fail to let that knowledge stir your soul? Why do you keep that knowledge abstract?
?
If perception requires specifying the perceived object, how specifically do you perceive God's attributes in your worship? Is your awareness of His hearing specific enough to make you careful with your words right now?

Perception and Knowledge: The Sensory Distinction
The author contrasts the five senses to argue that perception and knowledge are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the different conditions of the senses (sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch) show that perception and knowledge are not identical, and why this limits the attributes we ascribe to Allah.

Core Idea
The conditions of the senses prove perception differs from knowledge.

The author begins by showing that perception (idrak) and knowledge (ilm) cannot be the same because they have different conditions. For example, seeing requires facing, while hearing does not. This leads to a key conclusion: Allah can be described as Hearing and Seeing but not as Smelling, Tasting, or Touching, since those would require physical contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the five senses
Read the first part of the passage where the author describes how each sense (sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch) has different requirements. Notice that sight requires facing, hearing does not, and smell/taste/touch require physical contact.
2
Observe the conclusion about Allah's attributes
Because of these differences, the author argues that Allah can be called Hearing and Seeing but not Smelling, Tasting, or Touching. Understand why: the latter imply physical contact, impossible for Allah.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
The passage concludes that perceptions differ in both reality and locus, so one who distinguishes perception from knowledge holds this view, while one who equates them unifies the locus and reality. Think about why this distinction matters in theology.
This prepares for al-Ash'ari's view in the next lesson.
