The Principle of Composite Denial in Theological Divisions
Explanation of the rule about denying composites in divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle logical rule that when a judgment attaches to a composite of parts, denying the individual parts does not suffice; one must also deny the composite.

Core Idea
Denying parts individually does not deny the composite.

The objector deepens the critique by pointing out a common oversight in theological reasoning. He explains that when something is divided into parts, the judgment may be attached to the composite, not to the parts separately. Theologians often neglect this, but it is crucial to observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the distinction between individual parts and the composite.
The key phrase is 'ربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين'.
2
Understand the rule about composites
The objector states that sometimes a judgment is attached to a combination of two parts, not to each part alone. Therefore, denying each part individually does not adequately address the composite judgment.
This is a logical subtlety often missed.
3
Reflect on its importance
Consider how this principle applies to the current debate: the opponent might think denying hudduth as a qualifier is enough, but the objector argues that the qualifier might be a composite of existence and hudduth.
The objector criticizes theologians for neglecting this in their divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض رابع : لم قلتم : إن الحدوث لا يكون مصححا وقد عرفتم أن من الأحكام العقلية ما يتعلق بالحدوث دون الوجود؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن كونه مسبوقا بعدم ليس بمؤثر فإن معناه أنه وجود على صفة واعتبار، وكما أن المرئي أخص من المعلوم، والرؤية تتعلق بالمعلوم على صفة الوجود، كذلك نقول : تتعلق بالوجود على صفة الحدوث، وبالحادث على صفة المقابلة أو التكون، أو غير ذلك من الصفات والاعتبارات، وهذا السر وهو أن المنقسم ربما يورد أقسامًا وينفي بعض الأقسام مفردًا، وربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين لا بمفرد منهما فكما يجب نفي الأفراد من حيث هي أفراد فكذلك يجب نفي المركب من حيث هو مركب، وهذا مما أغفله المتكلم كثيرًا في مجاري تقسيماته، وهو واجب الرعاية جدا.
And a fourth objection: Why did you say that hudduth (origination) cannot be a qualifier (musahhih), while you know that among rational judgments there are those that attach to hudduth and not to existence? And no one can say that its being preceded by non-existence is ineffective, for its meaning is that it is existence qualified by an attribute and consideration. Just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known by the attribute of existence, similarly we say: it attaches to existence by the attribute of hudduth, and to the originated by the attribute of opposition or becoming [al-takawwun], or other attributes and considerations. And this secret is that the divided thing may sometimes be presented in parts and some parts be denied individually, and sometimes the judgment may be attached to a composite of the two parts and not to each alone. So just as it is necessary to deny individuals insofar as they are individuals, so too it is necessary to deny the composite insofar as it is composite. And this is something the theologian (mutakallim) often neglects in his divisions, and it is highly necessary to observe.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection posed by an opponent against the Ashʿarī position that the qualifier (musahhih) for vision is mere existence. The objector argues that hudduth (origination) can also serve as a qualifier, noting that rational judgments sometimes attach to hudduth rather than existence. He then explains that being preceded by non-existence is not ineffective; rather, it means existence with a specific attribute. He draws an analogy: just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known under the attribute of existence, so vision can attach to existence under the attribute of hudduth, or to the originated under the attribute of opposition or becoming. The objector then highlights a subtle point about division and composition: when something is divided into parts, one might deny some parts individually, but the judgment might actually attach to a composite of those parts. So denying the individual parts does not suffice; one must also deny the composite. The objector criticizes that theologians often neglect this nuance in their divisions.

Key Terms
المنقسم
أقسام
مركب
أفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying all parts individually is sufficient to deny the composite judgment.
Correction: The objector explains that if the judgment attaches to the composite, one must deny the composite as such, not just its parts.

Checkpoint
What is the subtle point the objector makes about theological divisions?
A) Theologians always consider composites.
B) Denying individuals suffices even for composites.
C) When a judgment is attached to a composite, denying individuals is not enough.
D) Hudduth is not a qualifier.

Key Takeaway
In theological divisions, one must deny the composite as such, not just its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you confidently defended a theological position by listing reasons against each individual argument of your opponent. Did you ever consider that your opponent's claim might be a composite — a combination of those reasons — and that by only denying the parts you left the composite untouched? How might this oversight have affected the truth of your defense? Will you examine your arguments more carefully from now on, especially regarding composite claims?
?
The passage warns that theologians often neglect this rule. If you are a student of theology, are you willing to admit that you might have fallen into this neglect? What change in your reasoning practice does this lesson demand of you today? Write down one specific step you will take to ensure you deny composites when necessary.
?
Your ultimate accountability is before Allah. If you present an argument that is logically flawed because you denied only parts, you might be misrepresenting the truth. Are you satisfied with that risk? What will you do to master this principle so that your defense of the faith is rigorous and complete?

Divine Attributes

The Fifth Objection: Generalizing the Qualifier to All Senses
Objection that if all senses perceive existence, then God would be perceived by all senses, which is shirk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed against the Ashʿarī claim that vision's qualifier is existence, and why the objection forces a choice between shirk and requiring a proof.

Core Idea
To generalize the qualifier to all senses implies shirk; to specify it to vision requires a proof.

The objector pushes the Ashʿarī onto the horns of a dilemma: either the qualifier for vision applies to all senses, making God perceptible by all—which is shirk—or it applies only to vision, but then a proof is needed. This lesson unpacks the objection and the attempted replies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage and identify the two options presented: generalize the qualifier (existence) to all senses, or specify it only to vision.
The dilemma is a classic 'if-then-else' structure.
2
Examine the first horn
If they generalize, they commit to God being heard, smelled, tasted, and touched—which the objector calls 'great shirk'.
Why is this considered shirk? Because it implies divine attributes are like created things.
3
Examine the second horn
If they specify to vision, they must produce a proof for that specification. The passage then explores two attempted proofs.
The burden of proof is on the Ashʿarī to show why vision is special.
4
Analyze the rebuttals
The objector dismisses the first proof as question-begging and counters the second by distinguishing intellectual determination from sensory determination.
Pay attention to the concept of 'determination' (taʿyīn) and its two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض خامس قد تحقق أن الرؤية من جملة الحواس الخمس أفتقولون أنها كلها تتعلق بالوجود والمصحح لها الوجود، أم الرؤية خاصة تتعلق بكل موجود، فإن عممتم الحكم فقد افتتحتم أمرًا وهو التزام كونه تعالى مسموعا مشمومًا مطعوماً ملموسًا وذلك شرك عظيم، وإن خصصتم الحكم بالرؤية وجب عليكم إظهار دليل التخصيص ، فإن قلتم : الدليل أنه لا يؤثر في المرئي ولا يتأثر منه ، فقيل فقيل : هو نفس المتنازع فيه، وإن قلتم : هو علم أو في حكم العلم إلا أنه يقتضي تعيين المدرك ، فيقال لكم التعيين قد يكون تعيين العقل، ولا شك أن الحس لا يستدعي ذلك التعيين فإن تعيين العقل كتخصيص العام، وتفصيل المجمل، وتقييد المطلق، وتحديد الشيء، ولا تختص أمثال هذه التعيينات بالرؤية والحس وإن قلتم التعيين تعيين الحس فهو يستدعي قبول الإشارة إليه، وذلك يقتضي حيزًا ومكانا وجهة ومقابلة، وهو محال.
And a fifth objection: It is established that vision is one of the five senses. Do you say that all of them attach to existence and the qualifier for them is existence, or is vision specifically attached to every existent? For if you generalize the judgment, you have initiated a matter—namely, committing to His being heard, smelled, tasted, and touched—and that is great shirk. And if you specify the judgment to vision, then you must produce a proof of specification. If you say: the proof is that it [vision] neither affects the visible nor is affected by it, then it is said: that is exactly the point of dispute. And if you say: it is knowledge or in the ruling of knowledge, except that it requires determination of the perceived object, then it is said to you: determination may be determination of the intellect. There is no doubt that sense does not necessitate that determination, for determination by the intellect is like specifying the general, detailing the ambiguous, restricting the absolute, and defining a thing. Such determinations are not specific to vision and sense. And if you say: determination is determination of sense, then it requires acceptance of pointing to it, and that necessitates a bounded location, a place, a direction, and opposition, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the Ashʿarī position that vision is attached to existence alone. The objector argues that since vision is one of the five senses, the Ashʿarī must either hold that all senses attach to existence (generalizing the qualifier) or that only vision is specific. If they generalize, then God would be perceptible by all senses—heard, smelled, tasted, touched—which is shirk. If they specify vision, they must provide a proof for that specification. The objector then considers two possible proofs they might offer: (1) that vision neither affects nor is affected by the visible—but that is exactly the point in dispute. (2) That vision is knowledge or like knowledge but requires determination of the perceived object. The objector replies that determination can be intellectual, not sensory, and such intellectual determination is not unique to vision. If they claim it is sensory determination, then that would require pointing, implying location and direction, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الحواس
الوجود
التخصيص
التعيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if existence is the qualifier for vision, then it must also be the qualifier for all senses, but the Ashʿarī do not claim that.
Correction: The Ashʿarī hold that vision is special; the objector is forcing them to justify that specificity.

Checkpoint
What is the main dilemma posed in the fifth objection?
A) If all senses perceive existence, God would be perceived by all senses, which is absurd; if only vision does, a proof of specification is needed.
B) The Ashʿarī cannot prove that vision attaches to existence.
C) Vision is not a sense but a form of knowledge.
D) The objector accepts that vision is special.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection forces the Ashʿarī to either admit shirk or provide a valid proof for singling out vision among the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that claiming God can be perceived by the senses is shirk. Have you ever considered God's transcendence so seriously that you avoid any mental image or sensory attribute? What subtle ways might you still allow your imagination to 'place' God somewhere, and how can you purify your belief today?
?
The objector demands a proof for specifying vision. In your own faith, what proofs do you rely on for the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter? Are they based on revelation alone or also on rational arguments like those discussed here? Are you content with your certainty, or is there a lingering doubt you need to address?
?
Ashʿarī theologians were forced to confront the implications of their claim. When you face a theological dilemma that could lead to shirk, do you honestly examine all options, or do you avoid the difficulty? What will you do with this lesson's challenge to ensure your beliefs are sound?
